The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The Three Types of Non-Direct Inference
Introduces the three types of non-direct inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of non-direct inference: induction, syllogism, and analogy, based on their definitions and uses in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Three tools of reasoning: induction, syllogism, analogy – each with a different path to knowledge.

In this chapter, we move from direct inference (like conversion) to more complex reasoning that requires a middle term or multiple steps. The author presents the three classical forms of non-direct inference: induction (istiqra'), syllogism (qiyas), and analogy (tamthil). Understanding these three is essential for analyzing arguments in theology, jurisprudence, and other Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Read the Arabic list: استقراء, قياس, تمثيل. These are the three branches of non-direct inference.
Memorize the Arabic terms; they appear frequently in later chapters.
2
Understand the meaning of each
Induction (istiqra') moves from many particular observations to a general rule. Syllogism (qiyas) deduces a necessary conclusion from two premises. Analogy (tamthil) reasons from one specific case to another based on a shared attribute.
Syllogism yields certainty; induction and analogy may yield only probability.
3
Distinguish direct vs non-direct
Direct inference, like conversion (الاستدلال المباشر), does not require a middle term. Non-direct inference always involves a middle term or multiple steps.
This distinction is crucial for recognizing argument types.
4
Apply the classification
Given an argument, decide whether it uses induction, syllogism, or analogy. For example: 'All intoxicants are forbidden, nabidh is an intoxicant, so nabidh is forbidden' is a syllogism.
Practice with examples from kalam and fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني
الاستدلال غير المباشر
١ - الاستقراء .
٢ - القياس .
٣ - التمثيل .
Chapter Two: Non-direct inference. 1- Induction. 2- Syllogism. 3- Analogy.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of a new chapter on non-direct inference (al-istidlal ghayr al-mubashir). It lists the three traditional types: induction (istiqra'), which is reasoning from particular instances to a general rule; syllogism (qiyas), which is deductive reasoning from premises to a necessary conclusion; and analogy (tamthil), which is reasoning from one particular case to another based on a shared attribute. These three forms are distinguished from direct inference (like conversion) by requiring a middle term or multiple steps. This classification is standard in classical Islamic logic.

Key Terms
استقراء
قياس
تمثيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a type of non-direct inference?
A) Induction (استقراء)
B) Syllogism (قياس)
C) Analogy (تمثيل)
D) Conversion (عكس)

Key Takeaway
Non-direct inference in Islamic logic includes induction, syllogism, and analogy, each serving a distinct reasoning function.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the following argument: 'All temporally originated things have a cause. The world is temporally originated. Therefore, the world has a cause.' Which type of non-direct inference is this? (Answer: syllogism/qiyas)
?
Find an example of induction (istiqra') from Islamic theology: For instance, 'Every observed temporally originated thing has a cause, so all temporally originated things have a cause.' Is this complete or incomplete induction?

The Syllogism
beginner

Definition of Qiyas (Logical Syllogism)
Author defines qiyas as a formal argument and indirect reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two-stage structure of a qiyas and distinguish it from immediate inference.

Core Idea
The logical syllogism is indirect inference: it requires two premises with a common term to yield a conclusion.

This passage lays the foundation for all syllogistic reasoning. The author defines qiyas as a formal argument that reminds us of overlooked truths or compels an opponent. He distinguishes it as indirect inference because it requires apprehending two propositions and then a relation of inclusion or implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: القياس (al-qiyās). The whole passage revolves around defining what this term means.
2
Extract the genus
The author states that qiyas is 'من الحجج' (a type of proof) and 'من طرق الاستدلال غير المباشر' (one of the methods of indirect inference). So the genus is 'proof' and more specifically 'indirect inference'.
The genus is not a single word; it is expressed through two phrases indicating its category.
3
Identify the differentia
The differentia is that it requires at least two stages: (1) apprehending two propositions with a common term, and (2) apprehending inclusion, equivalence, or necessary implication. This sets it apart from immediate inference, which has one stage.
The passage explicitly mentions 'ذات مرحلتين' (two stages).
4
Distinguish from immediate inference
Contrast qiyas with direct inference (like conversion or contraposition). In qiyas, you cannot reach the conclusion without the intermediate stage; in immediate inference, one proposition directly yields another.
For example, 'All A are B' directly implies 'Some B are A' – that is immediate, not qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس المنطقي صيغة شكلية لإثبات حقائق سبق العلم بها، ولكن حصلت الغفلة عن جوانب منها، إذ يأتي القياس المنطقي منبهاً عليها، أو ملزماً الخصم بالتسليم بها إذا هو أنكرها.
أما حقائق المعرفة التي تتألف منها قضايا الأقيسة، فهي المواد الأساسية للصيغ الشكلية التي يتألف منها القياس، وطريق الوصول إليها أحد الطرق الموصلة إلى المعرفة التي سبق بيانها في القسم الثاني من هذا الكتاب.
والقياس: هو من الحجج، وهو أحد طرق الاستدلال غير المباشر، وأقومها إنتاجاً.
وإنما كان استدلالاً غير مباشر، لتوقف تحصيل النتيجة فيه على إدراكِ مسلَّم به ذي مرحلتين على أقل تقدير، تمثل المرحلة الأولى منهما إدراك قضيتين بينهما حد مشترك، وتمثل المرحلة الثانية منهما إدراك اندراج(١) أحد الحدين الآخرين بصاحبه، أو أنه مساوٍ له في الماصدق، أو إدراك تحقق وجه من وجوه اللزوم المسلَّم به فيه.
والحجة: بيان يؤتى به لإثبات مطلوب تصديقي.
(١) والاندراج يكون بدخول الجزئي ضمن أفراد الكلي.
The logical syllogism is a formal formula for proving truths that were previously known, but aspects of which were overlooked; the logical syllogism comes to draw attention to them, or to compel the opponent to accept them if he denies them. As for the truths of knowledge that constitute the propositions of syllogisms, they are the basic materials for the formal formulas that constitute the syllogism, and the way to attain them is one of the methods leading to knowledge that were previously explained in the second part of this book. The syllogism (qiyas) is a type of proof (hujaj), and it is one of the methods of indirect inference, and the most sound of them in producing conclusions. It is an indirect inference because obtaining the conclusion depends on apprehending a conceded (premise) with at least two stages: the first stage is apprehending two propositions with a common term, and the second stage is apprehending the inclusion of one of the other two terms with its companion, or that it is equal to it in extension, or apprehending the realization of a type of necessary implication conceded therein. The proof (hujja) is a statement brought forth to prove a tasdiqi (assent-based) claim. (1) Inclusion is by the particular falling under the individuals of the universal.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage defines the logical syllogism (qiyas). The author opens by stating that the syllogism is a formal formula used to prove truths that were already known but overlooked, thus it either reminds or compels an opponent. The material of syllogisms are truths of knowledge obtained through methods explained earlier in the book. Then he defines qiyas as a type of proof (hujja) and as indirect inference, the most sound in producing conclusions. The indirectness is because reaching the conclusion requires at least two stages: first, apprehending two propositions with a common term (the middle term), and second, apprehending either the inclusion of one of the other terms in the other, or equivalence in extension, or a necessary implication. Finally, the author defines hujja as a statement used to prove an assent-based (tasdiqi) claim. A footnote explains that inclusion means a particular falling under the individuals of a universal. The passage follows a clear definitional order: first what qiyas does, then its material, then its definition as indirect reasoning with stages.

الاستدلال غير المباشر
الحجة
الاندراج
مطلوب تصديقي

Checkpoint

A) Because it uses three terms.

C) Because it relies on intuition.
D) Because it is a formal formula.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is indirect inference because it requires at least two stages of apprehension from two premises with a common term.

?

The Syllogism
beginner
7 min
Definition and Structure of the Logical Syllogism
Author defines syllogism as indirect inference with a two-stage structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two stages of indirect inference and the components of a correct syllogism.

Core Idea
A syllogism is a two-stage argument that yields necessary conclusions from shared terms.

In this passage, the author introduces the logical syllogism as the most reliable form of indirect inference. He explains that a syllogism is not a single step but requires two stages: first, grasping two premises that share a common term, and second, recognising how the remaining terms relate through inclusion, equality, or entailment. This definition lays the foundation for all subsequent study of syllogistic reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of syllogism
Find the exact phrase 'القِياسُ: هُوَ قَوْلٌ مُؤَلَّفٌ مِنْ قَضَايَا مَتَى حَصَلَ التَّسْلِيمُ بِهَا لَزِمَ عَنْهُ لِذَاتِهِ قَوْلٌ آخَرُ'. This is the formal definition: a statement composed of premises that, when accepted, necessarily entail another statement by itself.
The term 'قول' here means a composite utterance, not a single word.
2
Distinguish indirect from direct inference
The author says the syllogism is 'استدلال غير مباشر' (indirect inference) because obtaining the conclusion depends on a two-stage process. Direct inference would be a single step, like immediate inference (e.g., conversion).
Indirect inference always involves a middle term connecting the premises.
3
Identify the two stages of indirect inference
First stage: perceiving two propositions that share a common term (حد مشترك). Second stage: perceiving the inclusion (اندراج) of one term within the other, or their equality in extension, or a necessary entailment between them.
The common term is what makes the syllogism work; without it, no conclusion follows.
4
Verify the condition of a correct syllogism
The definition adds 'متى حصل التسليم بها لزم عنه لذاته' – the conclusion must follow from the premises themselves, not from external assumptions. This ensures necessity (ضرورة) in the conclusion.
If the conclusion requires an extra premise, it is not a complete syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القِياسُ
القياس المنطقي صيغة شكلية لإثبات حقائق سبق العلم بها، ولكن حصلت الغفلة عن جوانب منها، إذ يأتي القياس المنطقي منبهاً عليها، أو ملزماً الخصم بالتسليم بها إذا هو أنكرها.
أما حقائق المعرفة التي تتألف منها قضايا الأقيسة، فهي المواد الأساسية للصيغ الشكلية التي يتألف منها القياس، وطريق الوصول إليها أحد الطرق الموصلة إلى المعرفة التي سبق بيانها في القسم الثاني من هذا الكتاب.
والقياس: هو من الحجج، وهو أحد طرق الاستدلال غير المباشر، وأقومها إنتاجاً.
وإنما كان استدلالاً غير مباشر، لتوقف تحصيل النتيجة فيه على إدراكِ مسلَّم به ذي مرحلتين على أقل تقدير، تمثل المرحلة الأولى منهما إدراك قضيتين بينهما حد مشترك، وتمثل المرحلة الثانية منهما إدراك اندراج(١) أحد الحدين الآخرين بصاحبه، أو أنه مساوٍ له في الماصدق، أو إدراك تحقق وجه من وجوه اللزوم المسلَّم به فيه.
والحجة: بيان يؤتى به لإثبات مطلوب تصديقي.
(١) والاندراج يكون بدخول الجزئي ضمن أفراد الكلي.
والقياس الصحيح : هو قول مؤلف من قضايا متى حصل التسليم بها لزم عنه لذاته قول آخر(١) :
وهو ينقسم إلى قسمين رئيسين(٢) :
القسم الأول : القياس الاقتراني وهو قسمان : حملي وشرطي .
القسم الثاني : القياس الاستثنائي .
ويمتاز القياس الاقتراني بأن عناصره فيها اقتران، وبأن نتيجته موجودة في مقدمتيه بالقوة لا بالفعل، أي بالمادة لا بالصورة والهيئة .
ويمتاز القياس الاستثنائي بوجود حرف الاستثناء بين مقدمتيه، وبأن نتيجته أو نقيضها موجودة بالفعل فيهما، دون حاجة إلى تعديل في الصيغة، إلا أن حكمها في المقدمتين شرطي، وفي النتيجة مجزوم به .
القياس الاقتراني :
قبل أن ندخل في تفصيلات أحكام القياس وصوره وأشكاله المنتجة، نوضحه بأمثلة من أمثلته، ليكون حاضراً في التصور بوجه عام .
سبق أن أوضحنا أنه لا بد في القياس من قضيتين على أقل تقدير، وبين هاتين القضيتين حد مشترك متكرر فيهما، فلنضع ذلك في أمثلة محددة .
القضية الأولى القضية الثانية
الشمس كتلة من نار وكل نار محرقة
هاتان قضيتان يجمعهما أنهما مشتركتان في حد متكرر فيهما هي النار .
The Syllogism
Logical syllogism is a formal formula for establishing truths that were previously known, but forgetfulness occurred regarding some aspects of them, so the logical syllogism comes to alert to them, or to compel the opponent to concede them if he denies them.
As for the truths of knowledge that constitute the propositions of syllogisms, they are the basic materials for the formal formulas that constitute the syllogism, and the way to reach them is one of the ways leading to knowledge that were previously explained in the second section of this book.
And the syllogism: it is among proofs, and it is one of the methods of indirect inference, and the most upright of them in producing results.
And it is indirect inference only because obtaining the conclusion in it depends on perceiving an accepted thing having at least two stages, the first stage of which represents perceiving two propositions between which there is a common term, and the second stage of which represents perceiving the inclusion(1) of one of the other two terms with its fellow, or that it is equal to it in extension, or perceiving the realization of an aspect of accepted entailment in it.
And the proof: is a statement brought to establish a desired tasdiqi (assertoric).
(1) And inclusion is by the entry of the particular within the individuals of the universal.
And the correct syllogism: is a statement composed of propositions which, if they are accepted, entails by itself another statement(1):
And it is divided into two main divisions(2):
The first division: the conjunctive syllogism, and it is of two types: categorical and conditional.
The second division: the exceptive syllogism.
And the conjunctive syllogism is distinguished by the fact that its elements have conjunction, and that its conclusion exists in its premises potentially not actually, i.e., materially not in form and figure.
And the exceptive syllogism is distinguished by the presence of the exceptive particle between its premises, and that its conclusion or its contradictory exists actually in them, without need for modification in the formula, except that its judgment in the premises is conditional, and in the conclusion it is decisive.
The Conjunctive Syllogism:
Before we enter into the details of the rules of the syllogism and its figures and its productive moods, we clarify it with examples from its examples, so that it may be present in conception in general.
We have previously explained that in a syllogism there must be at least two propositions, and between these two propositions there is a common term repeated in them. So let us put that in specific examples.
First proposition: The sun is a mass of fire.
Second proposition: Every fire is a burner.
These are two propositions that are united by being shared in a term repeated in them, which is 'fire'.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage defines the logical syllogism (qiyas) and presents its two primary divisions. The author begins by describing the syllogism as a formal formula that reminds us of forgotten truths or compels an opponent to concede them. It is a proof (hujja) and the most reliable form of indirect inference, because obtaining the conclusion requires two stages: first, understanding two premises that share a common term; second, recognizing the inclusion, equality, or entailment between the remaining terms. A correct syllogism is a composition of premises that, once accepted, necessarily yields a conclusion by itself. The author then divides the syllogism into two main kinds: conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i). The conjunctive is further split into categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti). Conjunctive syllogisms have their conclusion potentially in the premises, while exceptive syllogisms have their conclusion or its negation actually present, requiring only the exceptive particle. Finally, an example is given: 'The sun is a mass of fire' and 'Every fire is a burner' share the term 'fire', illustrating the common term necessary for a syllogism.

Key Terms
القياس
الحجة
الاستدلال غير المباشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two stages required for indirect inference in a syllogism?
A) Stating the premises and then uttering the conclusion.
B) Perceiving two propositions with a common term, then perceiving inclusion, equality, or entailment.
C) Accepting the premises and then adding an exceptive particle.
D) Forming a conjunctive then an exceptive syllogism.

Key Takeaway
A correct syllogism is a self-contained argument that, through a common term in two premises, necessarily yields a conclusion in two stages.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Module 14 · Lesson 4

The Syllogism
beginner
6 min
The Two Main Divisions of Syllogism: Conjunctive and Exceptive
Author divides syllogism into conjunctive and exceptive, explaining their characteristics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish conjunctive (iqtirani) from exceptive (istithna'i) syllogisms and identify their subtypes.

Core Idea
Conjunctive syllogisms contain their conclusion potentially; exceptive syllogisms contain it actually.

After defining the syllogism, the author divides it into two main kinds: conjunctive and exceptive. This division is based on whether the conclusion exists potentially or actually in the premises. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the correct rules of inference in different argument forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions
The author states: 'وهو ينقسم إلى قسمين رئيسين: الأول: القياس الاقتراني... الثاني: القياس الاستثنائي'. Note that conjunctive is further divided into categorical and conditional.
These two cover all valid syllogisms in Aristotelian logic.
2
Characterise the conjunctive syllogism
The conjunctive syllogism (اقتراني) has its conclusion in the premises potentially (بالقوة), meaning the conclusion is materially present but not yet formed. Its elements are in conjunction without an exceptive particle.
The premises are all asserted categorically or conditionally; the conclusion emerges when the terms are combined.
3
Characterise the exceptive syllogism
The exceptive syllogism (استثنائي) has an exceptive particle (like 'لا', 'لكن', 'إذا') between premises. Its conclusion or its contradictory exists actually (بالفعل) in the premises, so no further modification is needed—just extraction.
Example: 'If it rains then the ground is wet; it rains; therefore the ground is wet.' The conclusion 'ground is wet' is already present conditionally.
4
Recognise the subtypes of conjunctive syllogism
The author says conjunctive is of two types: حملي (categorical) and شرطي (conditional). Categorical deals with absolute assertions (e.g., 'Every A is B'), while conditional deals with hypotheticals (e.g., 'If A then B').
Both follow the same basic structure but differ in the form of premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أما حقائق المعرفة التي تتألف منها قضايا الأقيسة، فهي المواد الأساسية للصيغ الشكلية التي يتألف منها القياس، وطريق الوصول إليها أحد الطرق الموصلة إلى المعرفة التي سبق بيانها في القسم الثاني من هذا الكتاب.

وهو ينقسم إلى قسمين رئيسين(٢) :

هاتان قضيتان يجمعهما أنهما مشتركتان في حد متكرر فيهما هي النار .
The Syllogism
Logical syllogism is a formal formula for establishing truths that were previously known, but forgetfulness occurred regarding some aspects of them, so the logical syllogism comes to alert to them, or to compel the opponent to concede them if he denies them.
As for the truths of knowledge that constitute the propositions of syllogisms, they are the basic materials for the formal formulas that constitute the syllogism, and the way to reach them is one of the ways leading to knowledge that were previously explained in the second section of this book.
And the syllogism: it is among proofs, and it is one of the methods of indirect inference, and the most upright of them in producing results.
And it is indirect inference only because obtaining the conclusion in it depends on perceiving an accepted thing having at least two stages, the first stage of which represents perceiving two propositions between which there is a common term, and the second stage of which represents perceiving the inclusion(1) of one of the other two terms with its fellow, or that it is equal to it in extension, or perceiving the realization of an aspect of accepted entailment in it.
And the proof: is a statement brought to establish a desired tasdiqi (assertoric).
(1) And inclusion is by the entry of the particular within the individuals of the universal.
And the correct syllogism: is a statement composed of propositions which, if they are accepted, entails by itself another statement(1):
And it is divided into two main divisions(2):
The first division: the conjunctive syllogism, and it is of two types: categorical and conditional.
The second division: the exceptive syllogism.
And the conjunctive syllogism is distinguished by the fact that its elements have conjunction, and that its conclusion exists in its premises potentially not actually, i.e., materially not in form and figure.
And the exceptive syllogism is distinguished by the presence of the exceptive particle between its premises, and that its conclusion or its contradictory exists actually in them, without need for modification in the formula, except that its judgment in the premises is conditional, and in the conclusion it is decisive.
The Conjunctive Syllogism:
Before we enter into the details of the rules of the syllogism and its figures and its productive moods, we clarify it with examples from its examples, so that it may be present in conception in general.
We have previously explained that in a syllogism there must be at least two propositions, and between these two propositions there is a common term repeated in them. So let us put that in specific examples.
First proposition: The sun is a mass of fire.
Second proposition: Every fire is a burner.
These are two propositions that are united by being shared in a term repeated in them, which is 'fire'.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage defines the logical syllogism (qiyas) and presents its two primary divisions. The author begins by describing the syllogism as a formal formula that reminds us of forgotten truths or compels an opponent to concede them. It is a proof (hujja) and the most reliable form of indirect inference, because obtaining the conclusion requires two stages: first, understanding two premises that share a common term; second, recognizing the inclusion, equality, or entailment between the remaining terms. A correct syllogism is a composition of premises that, once accepted, necessarily yields a conclusion by itself. The author then divides the syllogism into two main kinds: conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i). The conjunctive is further split into categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti). Conjunctive syllogisms have their conclusion potentially in the premises, while exceptive syllogisms have their conclusion or its negation actually present, requiring only the exceptive particle. Finally, an example is given: 'The sun is a mass of fire' and 'Every fire is a burner' share the term 'fire', illustrating the common term necessary for a syllogism.

القياس الاقتراني
القياس الاستثنائي
حملي
شرطي
الاستثناء

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes the exceptive (istithna'i) syllogism from the conjunctive (iqtirani) syllogism?
A) Its conclusion is always a conditional proposition.
B) The exceptive syllogism has an exceptive particle between premises, and its conclusion or its contradictory exists actually in the premises.
C) The exceptive syllogism requires only one premise.
D) The exceptive syllogism is not considered a valid proof.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism is either conjunctive (conclusion potential) or exceptive (conclusion actual), and conjunctive further divides into categorical and conditional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following argument as conjunctive categorical, conjunctive conditional, or exceptive: 'If Zaid is in the city, then he can be seen. Zaid is in the city. Therefore, Zaid can be seen.' (Answer: exceptive conditional syllogism).
?

The Syllogism
beginner

Definition and Divisions of a Correct Syllogism
Author defines correct qiyas and its two main divisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a correct syllogism and identify whether a given syllogism is conjunctive or exceptive.

Core Idea
A correct syllogism's conclusion follows necessarily from its form, not from external proofs.

In this passage, the author establishes the foundational definition of a correct syllogism. This is the bedrock of all syllogistic reasoning. He then immediately classifies syllogisms into two main divisions: conjunctive and exceptive. Understanding these divisions is essential for recognizing how arguments work in logic and in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition's components
Look for the three components: compositeness (مؤلف من قضايا), grantedness (متى حصل التسليم بها), and essential necessity (لزم عنه لذاته).
The phrase 'li-dhatihi' is crucial.
2
Contrast with other arguments
The phrase 'li-dhatihi' excludes arguments that rely on external evidence like equality (al-musawat) or comparative reasoning.
3
Map the division
Note the two main divisions: iqtirani (conjunctive) and istithna'i (exceptive). The conjunctive is further divided into categorical and conditional.
4
Differentiate the features
For iqtirani: conclusion exists potentially, no exceptive particle. For istithna'i: conclusion or its contradictory actually exists, exceptive particle present.
The exceptive particle is like 'illa'.

والقياس الصحيح : هو قول مؤلف من قضايا متى حصل التسليم بها لزم عنه لذاته قول آخر(۱) : وهو ينقسم إلى قسمين رئيسين(۲) : القسم الأول : القياس الاقتراني وهو قسمان : حملي وشرطي . القسم الثاني : القياس الاستثنائي . ويمتاز القياس الاقتراني بأن عناصره فيها اقتران، وبأن نتيجته موجودة في مقدمتيه بالقوة لا بالفعل، أي بالمادة لا بالصورة والهيئة . ويمتاز القياس الاستثنائي بوجود حرف الاستثناء بين مقدمتيه، وبأن نتيجته أو نقيضها موجودة بالفعل فيهما، دون حاجة إلى تعديل في الصيغة، إلا أن حكمها في المقدمتين شرطي، وفي النتيجة مجزوم به . القياس الاقتراني : قبل أن ندخل في تفصيلات أحكام القياس وصوره وأشكاله المنتجة، نوضحه بأمثلة من أمثلته، ليكون حاضراً في التصور بوجه عام . سبق أن أوضحنا أنه لا بد في القياس من قضيتين على أقل تقدير، وبين هاتين القضيتين حد مشترك متكرر فيهما، فلنضع ذلك في أمثلة محددة . القضية الأولى القضية الثانية الشمس كتلة من نار وكل نار محرقة هاتان قضيتان يجمعهما أنهما مشتركتان في حد متكرر فيهما هي النار . فإذا اكتفينا بأحد المتكررين نتج معنا من القضيتين: الشمس كتلة من نار محرقة. وبما أن كون الشمس كتلة من نار أمر معلوم لنا في القضية المسلّم بها الأولى، وبما أن الذي كان غير مُدرَك لنا فيها هو كونها محرقة، فلنحذف كونها كتلة من نار، ولنقتصر على ما استفدناه جديداً، لتمييز ما استنتجناه بهذه العملية العقلية عن غيره، فلنقل: (الشمس محرقة). هذه القضية الجديدة قضية أدركناها إدراكاً ضرورياً بعد إدراكنا للقضية الأولى، وإدراكنا للقضية الثانية، وملاحظتنا دخول الشمس في عموم النار المحرقة، فاستحقت بذلك أن يحكم عليها بأنها محرقة. مثل هذه العملية العقلية تسمّى قياساً منطقياً. وليس خافياً علينا أن القضيتين (الشمس كتلة من نار، وكل نار محرقة) هما من القضايا (الحملية)، وأن كلاً منهما تتألف من موضوع ومحمول، وأن الأولى (شخصية) وأن الثانية (كلية). لكننا بعد أن جمعناهما على الطريقة السابقة، وكونا منهما قياساً استفدنا منه قضية جديدة هي (الشمس محرقة) كان لا بد لنا من وضع حدودٍ جزئية لعناصر هذا القياس، وتمييز هذه الحدود بأسماء اصطلاحية، وتسمية القياس المؤلف من القضايا الحملية، للتفريق بينه وبين المؤلف من القضايا الشرطية. فلنسم القياس الاقتراني المؤلف من القضايا الحملية (قياساً اقترانياً حملياً) لأنه مؤلف من الحمليات الصرف، وليس فيه من الشرطيات شيء. ولما كان الحد المتكرر في القضيتين هو الوسيط الجامع بينهما كان جديراً بأن نُسمّيه (الحد الأوسط). ولما كان موضوع القضية الأولى في مثالنا هو الحد الذي صار موضوعاً
The correct syllogism is: a statement composed of propositions such that when they are granted, there necessarily follows from them, by their own essence, another statement. It is divided into two main divisions: The first division: the conjunctive syllogism, and it is of two types: categorical and conditional. The second division: the exceptive syllogism. The conjunctive syllogism is distinguished by the fact that its elements contain conjunction, and that its conclusion exists in its premises potentially not actually, i.e., in matter not in form and shape. The exceptive syllogism is distinguished by the presence of the exceptive particle between its premises, and that its conclusion or its contradictory exists actually in them, without need for alteration in the formula, except that its judgment in the premises is conditional, and in the conclusion it is decisive. The conjunctive syllogism: Before we enter into the details of the rulings of the syllogism, its figures, and its productive moods, we clarify it with examples of its examples, so that it may be present in conception in general. We have already explained that a syllogism necessarily requires at least two propositions, and between these two propositions there is a common term repeated in them. So let us set that in specific examples. The first proposition: The sun is a mass of fire. The second proposition: Every fire is burning. These are two propositions united by their sharing a common term repeated in them, which is 'fire'. So if we suffice with one of the two repeated terms, there results from the two propositions: The sun is a mass of burning fire. And since the fact that the sun is a mass of fire is known to us in the first granted proposition, and since what was unknown to us in it is its being burning, let us eliminate its being a mass of fire, and restrict ourselves to what we have newly gained, to distinguish what we have concluded by this mental operation from other things. So let us say: (The sun is burning). This new proposition is a proposition we have perceived necessarily after our perception of the first proposition, and our perception of the second proposition, and our observation of the sun's entry into the generality of burning fire, so it deserved to be judged as burning. Such a mental operation is called a logical syllogism. It is not hidden from us that the two propositions (The sun is a mass of fire, and every fire is burning) are among categorical propositions, and each of them is composed of a subject and a predicate, and that the first is particular and the second is universal. But after we combined them in the previous manner, and formed from them a syllogism, we derived from it a new proposition, namely (The sun is burning). It was necessary for us to place specific boundaries for the elements of this syllogism, and distinguish these boundaries with technical names, and name the syllogism composed of categorical propositions, to differentiate between it and the one composed of conditional propositions. So let us call the conjunctive syllogism composed of categorical propositions a 'categorical conjunctive syllogism' because it is composed of pure categoricals, and there is nothing of conditionals in it. And since the repeated term in the two propositions is the mediating link between them, it deserves that we call it the 'middle term'. And since the subject of the first proposition in our example is the term which became subject
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage introduces the definition and classification of correct syllogism (qiyas) in logic. The author begins by defining a correct syllogism: a compound of propositions such that, when they are granted, another statement necessarily follows 'by its own essence', meaning the logical form itself, not an external proof like equality or comparative reasoning. This condition is crucial for distinguishing a valid syllogism from other argument forms. Next, the syllogism is divided into two main types: conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i). The conjunctive syllogism is further divided into categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti). The author then highlights the distinguishing features: in a conjunctive syllogism, the conclusion is potentially present in the premises—it needs to be extracted; in an exceptive syllogism, the conclusion or its opposite is actually present in the premises, and the exceptive particle explicitly indicates the inference. The rest of the passage illustrates the conjunctive categorical syllogism with an example: 'The sun is a mass of fire' and 'Every fire is burning' yield 'The sun is burning'. The author walks through the reasoning step by step: the common term 'fire' appears in both premises; by omitting the redundant part, the new conclusion emerges. This example demonstrates the role of the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) as the common link between the premises. The author then explains that the first premise is a particular proposition and the second is universal, but the resulting conclusion is a new universal judgment. The passage ends by naming the components: the repeated term is the 'middle term', and the syllogism is specifically called a 'categorical conjunctive syllogism' because it consists only of categorical propositions. The abrupt ending at the last line (likely continuing to discuss the subject term) does not detract from the core lesson.

C) Syllogisms with conditional premises

Key Takeaway
A correct syllogism is defined by the essential necessity of its conclusion from the premises, divided into conjunctive and exceptive types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following argument is a conjunctive or exceptive syllogism: 'If the prayer is invalid, then it must be repeated; this prayer is invalid; therefore it must be repeated.' Answer: exceptive (istithna'i) because of the 'if...then' structure and the exceptive inference.

The Categorical Syllogism Example and the Middle Term
Author illustrates conjunctive categorical syllogism with example and defines middle term

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the middle term in a categorical syllogism and explain how the conclusion is derived from the premises.

Core Idea
The middle term is the repeated term that links the premises, making the conclusion necessary.

In this part of the passage, the author illustrates the conjunctive categorical syllogism with a concrete example. He shows how two propositions sharing a common term produce a new conclusion. This example not only demonstrates the mechanics of a syllogism but also introduces the concept of the 'middle term' (al-hadd al-awsat) as the mediating link. Understanding this is crucial for constructing and analyzing any categorical syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
The first premise: 'The sun is a mass of fire.' The second: 'Every fire is burning.'
These are from the passage.
2
Find the common term
The term 'fire' appears in both premises. It is the repeated term.
3
Derive the conclusion
By eliminating the redundant part, we get 'The sun is burning.' This is the new proposition.
The author emphasizes that this conclusion is perceived necessarily.
4
Designate the middle term
The common term 'fire' is the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat). It mediates between the subject and predicate of the conclusion.
5
Classify the premises
The first premise is particular (shakhsiyya) and the second is universal (kulliyya). The conclusion is a universal judgment.
The author names this syllogism a 'categorical conjunctive syllogism'.

The correct syllogism is: a statement composed of propositions such that when they are granted, there necessarily follows from them, by their own essence, another statement. It is divided into two main divisions: The first division: the conjunctive syllogism, and it is of two types: categorical and conditional. The second division: the exceptive syllogism. The conjunctive syllogism is distinguished by the fact that its elements contain conjunction, and that its conclusion exists in its premises potentially not actually, i.e., in matter not in form and shape. The exceptive syllogism is distinguished by the presence of the exceptive particle between its premises, and that its conclusion or its contradictory exists actually in them, without need for alteration in the formula, except that its judgment in the premises is conditional, and in the conclusion it is decisive. The conjunctive syllogism: Before we enter into the details of the rulings of the syllogism, its figures, and its productive moods, we clarify it with examples of its examples, so that it may be present in conception in general. We have already explained that a syllogism necessarily requires at least two propositions, and between these two propositions there is a common term repeated in them. So let us set that in specific examples. The first proposition: The sun is a mass of fire. The second proposition: Every fire is burning. These are two propositions united by their sharing a common term repeated in them, which is 'fire'. So if we suffice with one of the two repeated terms, there results from the two propositions: The sun is a mass of burning fire. And since the fact that the sun is a mass of fire is known to us in the first granted proposition, and since what was unknown to us in it is its being burning, let us eliminate its being a mass of fire, and restrict ourselves to what we have newly gained, to distinguish what we have concluded by this mental operation from other things. So let us say: (The sun is burning). This new proposition is a proposition we have perceived necessarily after our perception of the first proposition, and our perception of the second proposition, and our observation of the sun's entry into the generality of burning fire, so it deserved to be judged as burning. Such a mental operation is called a logical syllogism. It is not hidden from us that the two propositions (The sun is a mass of fire, and every fire is burning) are among categorical propositions, and each of them is composed of a subject and a predicate, and that the first is particular and the second is universal. But after we combined them in the previous manner, and formed from them a syllogism, we derived from it a new proposition, namely (The sun is burning). It was necessary for us to place specific boundaries for the elements of this syllogism, and distinguish these boundaries with technical names, and name the syllogism composed of categorical propositions, to differentiate between it and the one composed of conditional propositions. So let us call the conjunctive syllogism composed of categorical propositions a 'categorical conjunctive syllogism' because it is composed of pure categoricals, and there is nothing of conditionals in it. And since the repeated term in the two propositions is the mediating link between them, it deserves that we call it the 'middle term'. And since the subject of the first proposition in our example is the term which became subject

This passage introduces the definition and classification of correct syllogism (qiyas) in logic. The Syllogism
beginner
10 min
The Three Terms and Two Premises of a Categorical Syllogism
Author defines the components of a categorical syllogism with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor, middle, and major terms and the minor and major premises in any categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Every categorical syllogism has exactly three terms and two premises; the middle term is the key.

A categorical syllogism is the basic unit of logical proof in Islamic disciplines like kalām and usul al-fiqh. This lesson breaks down its anatomy using the example of the sun and fire, and a standard argument from kalām about bodies being originated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion (natīja)
Find the new proposition derived from the two premises. In the sun example, the conclusion is 'The sun is burning'. The conclusion contains the minor term as subject and the major term as predicate.
The conclusion is what the syllogism proves.
2
Find the repeated term (middle term)
Locate the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. In the sun example, 'fire' (or 'mass of fire') is repeated: it appears in 'The sun is a mass of fire' and 'Every fire is burning'. This is the middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ).
The middle term is the connecting mediator between the other two terms.
3
Identify the minor term and minor premise
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion ('sun'). The premise that contains the minor term is the minor premise: 'The sun is a mass of fire'.
The minor premise is always the one with the minor term.
4
Identify the major term and major premise
The major term is the predicate of the conclusion ('burning'). The premise containing the major term is the major premise: 'Every fire is burning'.
The major term is more general than or equal to the minor term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا اكتفينا بأحد المتكررين نتج معنا من القضيتين: الشمس كتلة من نار محرقة. وبما أن كون الشمس كتلة من نارٍ أمرٌ معلوم لنا في القضية المسلّم بها الأولى، وبما أن الذي كان غير مُدرَك لنا فيها هو كونها محرقة، فلنحذف كونها كتلة من نار، ولنقتصر على ما استفدناه جديداً، لتمييز ما استنتجناه بهذه العملية العقلية عن غيره، فلنقل: (الشمس محرقة). هذه القضية الجديدة قضية أدركناها إدراكاً ضرورياً بعد إدراكنا للقضية الأولى، وإدراكنا للقضية الثانية، وملاحظتنا دخول الشمس في عموم النار المحرقة، فاستحقت بذلك أن يحكم عليها بأنها محرقة. مثل هذه العملية العقلية تسمّى قياساً منطقياً.
وليس خافياً علينا أن القضيتين (الشمس كتلة من نار، وكل نار محرقة) هما من القضايا (الحملية)، وأن كلاً منهما تتألف من موضوع ومحمول، وأن الأولى (شخصية) وأن الثانية (كلية). لكننا بعد أن جمعناهما على الطريقة السابقة، وكونا منهما قياساً استفدنا منه قضية جديدة هي (الشمس محرقة) كان لا بد لنا من وضع حدودٍ جزئية لعناصر هذا القياس، وتمييز هذه الحدود بأسماء اصطلاحية، وتسمية القياس المؤلف من القضايا الحملية، للتفريق بينه وبين المؤلف من القضايا الشرطية. فلنسم القياس الاقتراني المؤلف من القضايا الحملية (قياساً اقترانياً حملياً) لأنه مؤلف من الحمليات الصرف، وليس فيه من الشرطيات شيء.
ولما كان الحد المتكرر في القضيتين هو الوسيط الجامع بينهما كان جديراً بأن نُسمّيه (الحد الأوسط). ولما كان موضوع القضية الأولى في مثالنا هو الحد الذي صار موضوعاً في القضية المستفادة الجديدة ناسب أن نُسمّيه (الحدّ الأصغر). ولما كان محمول القضية الثانية في مثالنا هو الحد الذي صار محمولاً في القضية المستفادة الجديدة، ناسب أن نُسمّيه (الحدّ الأكبر)، وهو أحق بأن يكون أكبر لأن من أحواله أن يكون أعمَّ من الحدّ الأصغر، ولا ينزل بحال عن كونه مساوياً، وإلا لم يصح الحمل.
فالقياس إذاً يتألف من ثلاثة حدود: (الحدّ الأصغر ـ الحدّ الأوسط ـ الحدّ الأكبر). والقضية التي تشتمل على الحد الأصغر نسميها: (المقدمة الصغرى). والقضية التي تشتمل على الحد الأكبر نسميها: (المقدمة الكبرى). والقضية الجديدة التي نستفيدها من القياس نسميها: (النتيجة). وهذه القضية الجديدة حين تكون مطلوبنا قبل القياس نسميها (مطلوباً). فـ (الحد الأصغر) في القياس هو الذي يأتي موضوعاً في النتيجة، والقضية المشتملة عليه في القياس هي (المقدمة الصغرى). و (الحد الأكبر) في القياس هو الذي يأتي محمولاً في النتيجة، والقضية المشتملة عليه في القياس هي (المقدمة الكبرى). والقضية المطلوبة عن طريق القياس هي: (المطلوب). ومتى أنتجها القياس فهي (النتيجة). فلنضع هذه الأسماء الاصطلاحية في مواضعها على مثالٍ آخر نقدمه: قياس اقتراني حملي المقدمة الصغرى كل جسم مؤلف المقدمة الكبرى وكل مؤلف حادث حد أصغر حد أوسط حد أكبر المطلوب .................. فكل جسم حادث النتيجة ولا يشترط في الحد الأوسط أن يكون موضعه بهذا الشكل دائماً
If we are satisfied with one of the two repeated [terms], there results for us from the two propositions: 'The sun is a mass of burning fire.' And since the fact that the sun is a mass of fire is known to us in the first accepted proposition, and since what was not perceived by us in it is its being burning, let us delete its being a mass of fire, and confine ourselves to what we newly gained, to distinguish what we inferred by this mental operation from others, so let us say: (The sun is burning). This new proposition is a proposition we perceived necessarily after our perception of the first proposition, our perception of the second proposition, and our observation of the inclusion of the sun under the generality of burning fire, so it deserved thereby to be judged as burning. Such a mental operation is called a logical syllogism.
It is not hidden from us that the two propositions (The sun is a mass of fire, and Every fire is burning) are from the (categorical) propositions, and each of them consists of a subject and a predicate, and the first is (particular) and the second is (universal). But after we combined them in the previous manner, and they formed a syllogism from which we derived a new proposition which is (The sun is burning), it was necessary for us to assign specific terms to the elements of this syllogism, and to distinguish these terms with conventional names, and to name the syllogism composed of categorical propositions, to differentiate it from that composed of conditional propositions. So let us name the conjunctive syllogism composed of categorical propositions (a conjunctive categorical syllogism) because it is composed of pure categoricals, and there is nothing of conditionals in it.
Since the repeated term in the two propositions is the connecting mediator between them, it was deserving that we name it (the middle term). Since the subject of the first proposition in our example is the term that became the subject in the newly derived proposition, it was appropriate that we name it (the minor term). Since the predicate of the second proposition in our example is the term that became the predicate in the newly derived proposition, it was appropriate that we name it (the major term), and it is more deserving to be major because among its properties is that it is more general than the minor term, and it does not fall below being equal to it, otherwise predication would not be valid.
Thus the syllogism consists of three terms: (the minor term – the middle term – the major term). The proposition that contains the minor term we name: (the minor premise). The proposition that contains the major term we name: (the major premise). The new proposition that we derive from the syllogism we name: (the conclusion). And this new proposition when it is our sought [proposition] before the syllogism we name (the sought). The minor term in the syllogism is that which appears as subject in the conclusion, and the proposition containing it in the syllogism is the minor premise. The major term in the syllogism is that which appears as predicate in the conclusion, and the proposition containing it in the syllogism is the major premise. The proposition sought through the syllogism is: (the sought). When the syllogism produces it, it is (the conclusion). So let us place these conventional names in their positions with another example we present: Conjunctive categorical syllogism: Minor premise: Every body is composed. Major premise: And every composed is originated. Minor term – Middle term – Major term – The sought: Therefore every body is originated. The conclusion. And it is not required that the middle term always be positioned in this form.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory expansion of the structure of a conjunctive categorical syllogism (al-qiyās al-iqṭirānī al-ḥamlī). The author begins with a concrete example: from 'The sun is a mass of fire' and 'Every fire is burning' we derive 'The sun is burning'. The mental operation of combining these two propositions is called a logical syllogism. The author notes that both propositions are categorical (ḥamliyya), the first is particular (shakhṣiyya) and the second is universal (kulliyya). After explaining the example, the author introduces the technical names for the components. The term repeated in both premises (نار 'fire') is called the middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ). The subject of the first premise, which becomes the subject of the conclusion (الشمس 'the sun'), is called the minor term (al-ḥadd al-aṣghar). The predicate of the second premise, which becomes the predicate of the conclusion (محرقة 'burning'), is called the major term (al-ḥadd al-akbar). The major term is more general than the minor or at least equal; otherwise predication would fail. The syllogism thus consists of three terms. The premise containing the minor term is the minor premise (al-muqaddima al-ṣughrā); the premise containing the major term is the major premise (al-muqaddima al-kubrā). The newly derived proposition is the conclusion (al-natīja), and when it is what we sought before the syllogism, it is called the sought (al-maṭlūb). The author then repeats the definitions: the minor term is subject of the conclusion, the major term is predicate of the conclusion. Finally, another example is given: 'Every body is composed' and 'Every composed is originated' yields 'Therefore every body is originated'. This example, drawn from kalām, clearly illustrates the three terms: 'body' (minor), 'composed' (middle), 'originated' (major). The passage ends with a note that the middle term's position is not fixed to this form (i.e., it can be in different positions in the premises, as in the four figures).

Key Terms
الحد الأوسط
الحد الأصغر
الحد الأكبر
المقدمة الصغرى
المقدمة الكبرى
النتيجة
المطلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the author's example of the sun, what is the middle term?
A) The sun (الشمس)
B) Burning (محرقة)
C) Fire (نار)
D) The sun and fire

Key Takeaway
Every categorical syllogism is built from three terms (minor, middle, major) and two premises (minor, major); the middle term must connect the other two without appearing in the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the kalām syllogism 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated requires a cause; therefore the world requires a cause', identify the minor, middle, and major terms, and state which premise is the minor and which is the major.
?
Construct your own categorical syllogism on an Islamic legal topic (e.g., the prohibition of ribā) by providing two premises and a conclusion, and label the three terms and two premises.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Identifying the Three Terms and Premises in a Categorical Syllogism
Defines the three terms and names of premises and conclusion in a categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor, middle, and major terms in any categorical syllogism and correctly label the premises and conclusion.

Core Idea
A syllogism is built from three terms, and their roles in the conclusion determine the names of the premises.

Before you can construct or evaluate a categorical syllogism, you must be able to dissect it into its components. The passage defines the three terms — minor, middle, major — and the corresponding premises and conclusion. We will walk through the example to see how these parts fit together.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is the derived proposition from the two premises. In the example, 'every body is originated' is the conclusion. Find which term is the subject and which is the predicate.
2
Label the minor and major terms
The subject of the conclusion is the minor term (body); the predicate of the conclusion is the major term (originated).
3
Find the middle term
The term common to both premises but absent from the conclusion is the middle term (composed).
4
Name the premises
The premise containing the minor term (body) is the minor premise; the premise containing the major term (originated) is the major premise.
5
Verify the structure
Check that the minor premise is 'every body is composed' and the major premise is 'every composed thing is originated'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في القضية المستفادة الجديدة ناسب أن نُسمّيه (الحدّ الأصغر). ولما كان محمول القضية الثانية في مثالنا هو الحد الذي صار محمولاً في القضية المستفادة الجديدة، ناسب أن نُسمّيه (الحدّ الأكبر)، وهو أحق بأن يكون أكبر لأن من أحواله أن يكون أعمَّ من الحدّ الأصغر، ولا ينزل بحال عن كونه مساوياً، وإلا لم يصح الحمل. فالقياس إذاً يتألف من ثلاثة حدود: (الحدّ الأصغر ـ الحدّ الأوسط ـ الحدّ الأكبر). والقضية التي تشتمل على الحد الأصغر نسميها: (المقدمة الصغرى). والقضية التي تشتمل على الحد الأكبر نسميها: (المقدمة الكبرى). والقضية الجديدة التي نستفيدها من القياس نسميها: (النتيجة). وهذه القضية الجديدة حين تكون مطلوبنا قبل القياس نسميها (مطلوباً). فـ (الحد الأصغر) في القياس هو الذي يأتي موضوعاً في النتيجة، والقضية المشتملة عليه في القياس هي (المقدمة الصغرى). و (الحد الأكبر) في القياس هو الذي يأتي محمولاً في النتيجة، والقضية المشتملة عليه في القياس هي (المقدمة الكبرى). والقضية المطلوبة عن طريق القياس هي: (المطلوب). ومتى أنتجها القياس فهي (النتيجة). فلنضع هذه الأسماء الاصطلاحية في مواضعها على مثالٍ آخر نقدمه: قياس اقتراني حملي المقدمة الصغرى كل جسم مؤلف المقدمة الكبرى وكل مؤلف حادث حد أصغر حد أوسط حد أكبر المطلوب .................. فكل جسم حادث النتيجة ولا يشترط في الحد الأوسط أن يكون موضعه بهذا الشكل دائماً (محمولاً) في المقدمة الصغرى (موضوعاً) في المقدمة الكبرى، فقد يأتي (محمولاً) فيهما معاً، وقد يأتي (موضوعاً) فيهما معاً، وقد يأتي (موضوعاً) في الصغرى (محمولاً) في الكبرى.
In the new derived proposition, it is appropriate to call it (the minor term). Since the predicate of the second proposition in our example is the term that became a predicate in the newly derived proposition, it is appropriate to call it (the major term), and it is more deserving to be major because among its states is that it is more general than the minor term, and it never descends below being equal, otherwise predication would not be valid. So the syllogism consists of three terms: (the minor term, the middle term, the major term). The proposition that includes the minor term we call: (the minor premise). The proposition that includes the major term we call: (the major premise). The new proposition that we derive from the syllogism we call: (the conclusion). And this new proposition, when it is our sought before the syllogism, we call it (the sought proposition). So the minor term in the syllogism is that which comes as a subject in the conclusion, and the proposition containing it in the syllogism is the minor premise. The major term in the syllogism is that which comes as a predicate in the conclusion, and the proposition containing it in the syllogism is the major premise. The proposition sought through syllogism is: the sought proposition. And when the syllogism produces it, it is the conclusion. So let us place these technical terms in their positions on another example we present: Conjunctive categorical syllogism: minor premise: every body is composed, major premise: and every composed thing is originated, minor term, middle term, major term, sought: .................. so every body is originated, conclusion. And it is not required that the middle term always have this form (predicate) in the minor premise (subject) in the major premise, for it may come as predicate in both, or subject in both, or subject in the minor and predicate in the major.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage explains the fundamental structure of a categorical syllogism. It begins by defining the three terms: the minor term (حد أصغر) which becomes the subject of the conclusion, the major term (حد أكبر) which becomes the predicate of the conclusion, and the middle term (حد أوسط) which connects the two premises but does not appear in the conclusion. The author then names the premises: the premise containing the minor term is the minor premise (مقدمة صغرى), and the premise containing the major term is the major premise (مقدمة كبرى). The derived proposition is the conclusion (نتيجة), and before the syllogism it is called the sought (مطلوب). To solidify this, a concrete example is given: the syllogism 'Every body is composed; every composed thing is originated; therefore every body is originated.' The diagram labels the terms and premises. Finally, the author notes that the middle term is not fixed in position: it can be the predicate in both premises, the subject in both, or subject in the minor and predicate in the major. This flexibility is important for recognizing syllogistic forms in arguments.

Key Takeaway
Every categorical syllogism contains exactly three terms, each with a specific role based on the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following fiqh analogy: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every wine is an intoxicant; therefore every wine is forbidden.' Identify the minor term, middle term, and major term, then name the minor and major premises.
?

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Variable Position of the Middle Term
Defines the three terms and names of premises and conclusion in a categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three possible positions of the middle term in a categorical syllogism and classify them as figures.

Core Idea
The middle term can appear in different positions, which gives rise to the four figures of the syllogism.

The passage concludes with an important flexibility: the middle term is not fixed to one pattern. It can be the predicate in both premises, subject in both, or subject in the minor and predicate in the major. This variation generates the four figures of the syllogism, each with its own validity conditions.

Guided Path
1
Recall the example's middle term position
In the example, the middle term 'composed' is the predicate in the minor premise (body is composed) and the subject in the major premise (composed is originated). This is one valid pattern.
2
Identify the three other patterns from the passage
The passage lists three additional possibilities: (a) middle term as predicate in both premises, (b) subject in both premises, (c) subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major premise.
3
Map patterns to figures
In Islamic logic, these patterns correspond to figures: (a) predicate in both = second figure, (b) subject in both = third figure, (c) subject in minor + predicate in major = fourth figure (sometimes considered). The example pattern is the first figure.
4
Recognize the significance
The figure determines the validity of the syllogism. Not all figures yield certain conclusions without additional steps like conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في القضية المستفادة الجديدة ناسب أن نُسمّيه (الحدّ الأصغر). ولما كان محمول القضية الثانية في مثالنا هو الحد الذي صار محمولاً في القضية المستفادة الجديدة، ناسب أن نُسمّيه (الحدّ الأكبر)، وهو أحق بأن يكون أكبر لأن من أحواله أن يكون أعمَّ من الحدّ الأصغر، ولا ينزل بحال عن كونه مساوياً، وإلا لم يصح الحمل. فالقياس إذاً يتألف من ثلاثة حدود: (الحدّ الأصغر ـ الحدّ الأوسط ـ الحدّ الأكبر). والقضية التي تشتمل على الحد الأصغر نسميها: (المقدمة الصغرى). والقضية التي تشتمل على الحد الأكبر نسميها: (المقدمة الكبرى). والقضية الجديدة التي نستفيدها من القياس نسميها: (النتيجة). وهذه القضية الجديدة حين تكون مطلوبنا قبل القياس نسميها (مطلوباً). فـ (الحد الأصغر) في القياس هو الذي يأتي موضوعاً في النتيجة، والقضية المشتملة عليه في القياس هي (المقدمة الصغرى). و (الحد الأكبر) في القياس هو الذي يأتي محمولاً في النتيجة، والقضية المشتملة عليه في القياس هي (المقدمة الكبرى). والقضية المطلوبة عن طريق القياس هي: (المطلوب). ومتى أنتجها القياس فهي (النتيجة). فلنضع هذه الأسماء الاصطلاحية في مواضعها على مثالٍ آخر نقدمه: قياس اقتراني حملي المقدمة الصغرى كل جسم مؤلف المقدمة الكبرى وكل مؤلف حادث حد أصغر حد أوسط حد أكبر المطلوب .................. فكل جسم حادث النتيجة ولا يشترط في الحد الأوسط أن يكون موضعه بهذا الشكل دائماً (محمولاً) في المقدمة الصغرى (موضوعاً) في المقدمة الكبرى، فقد يأتي (محمولاً) فيهما معاً، وقد يأتي (موضوعاً) فيهما معاً، وقد يأتي (موضوعاً) في الصغرى (محمولاً) في الكبرى.
In the new derived proposition, it is appropriate to call it (the minor term). Since the predicate of the second proposition in our example is the term that became a predicate in the newly derived proposition, it is appropriate to call it (the major term), and it is more deserving to be major because among its states is that it is more general than the minor term, and it never descends below being equal, otherwise predication would not be valid. So the syllogism consists of three terms: (the minor term, the middle term, the major term). The proposition that includes the minor term we call: (the minor premise). The proposition that includes the major term we call: (the major premise). The new proposition that we derive from the syllogism we call: (the conclusion). And this new proposition, when it is our sought before the syllogism, we call it (the sought proposition). So the minor term in the syllogism is that which comes as a subject in the conclusion, and the proposition containing it in the syllogism is the minor premise. The major term in the syllogism is that which comes as a predicate in the conclusion, and the proposition containing it in the syllogism is the major premise. The proposition sought through syllogism is: the sought proposition. And when the syllogism produces it, it is the conclusion. So let us place these technical terms in their positions on another example we present: Conjunctive categorical syllogism: minor premise: every body is composed, major premise: and every composed thing is originated, minor term, middle term, major term, sought: .................. so every body is originated, conclusion. And it is not required that the middle term always have this form (predicate) in the minor premise (subject) in the major premise, for it may come as predicate in both, or subject in both, or subject in the minor and predicate in the major.

Commentary
This passage explains the fundamental structure of a categorical syllogism. It begins by defining the three terms: the minor term (حد أصغر) which becomes the subject of the conclusion, the major term (حد أكبر) which becomes the predicate of the conclusion, and the middle term (حد أوسط) which connects the two premises but does not appear in the conclusion. The author then names the premises: the premise containing the minor term is the minor premise (مقدمة صغرى), and the premise containing the major term is the major premise (مقدمة كبرى). The derived proposition is the conclusion (نتيجة), and before the syllogism it is called the sought (مطلوب). To solidify this, a concrete example is given: the syllogism 'Every body is composed; every composed thing is originated; therefore every body is originated.' The diagram labels the terms and premises. Finally, the author notes that the middle term is not fixed in position: it can be the predicate in both premises, the subject in both, or subject in the minor and predicate in the major. This flexibility is important for recognizing syllogistic forms in arguments.

The Five Types of Conjunctive Conditional Syllogisms
Lists five types of conjunctive conditional syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and distinguish the five types of conjunctive conditional syllogisms based on their premise composition.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms have five forms, mixing conjunctive, disjunctive, and categorical premises.

The passage enumerates all possible combinations of premises that form a conjunctive conditional syllogism. This classification helps us understand the logical structure of arguments found in Islamic texts where conditional reasoning is used, such as in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Read the five types
Examine the list from the passage: types 1 through 5. Note the premise types for each.
Types are: two conjunctives, two disjunctives, categorical+conjunctive, categorical+disjunctive, conjunctive+disjunctive.
2
Identify the premise types
For each type, determine whether the premises are conjunctive (muttasila), disjunctive (munfasila), or categorical (hamliyya).
Categorical premises are ordinary subject-predicate propositions.
3
Check the conclusion type
Observe what type of conclusion each combination yields: conjunctive, disjunctive, or either (for type 5).
Type 5 can produce either a conjunctive or a disjunctive conclusion depending on the logical relationship.
4
Memorize the pattern
Rehearse the five combinations and their outputs. This taxonomy is foundational for recognizing valid conditional syllogisms.

Examples of conjunctive syllogism from conditional propositions: The conjunctive syllogism may consist of conditional propositions, or conditional propositions may enter into it, and it is then called (conjunctive conditional syllogism), meaning: it does not consist purely of categorical propositions, but rather consists of two conditionals, or a categorical and a conditional. And the conditional may be conjunctive or disjunctive. By enumerating the possibilities, it appears to us that there are five types: 1 - That which consists of two conjunctives and produces a conjunctive. 2 - That which consists of two disjunctives and produces a disjunctive. 3 - That which consists of a categorical and a conjunctive and produces a conjunctive. 4 - That which consists of a categorical and a disjunctive and produces a disjunctive. 5 - That which consists of a conjunctive and a disjunctive and produces a conjunctive or a disjunctive. And the minor term in the conjunctive conditional syllogism is the antecedent of the conclusion, and the proposition containing the minor term is the minor premise. And the major term in the conjunctive conditional syllogism is the consequent of the conclusion, and the proposition containing the major term is the major premise. And the middle term in the conjunctive conditional syllogism is the shared term repeated between the two premises. A - Example of a conjunctive syllogism from two conjunctive conditionals: Minor premise: Whenever the crescent of Ramadan is sighted, then the month of fasting has entered; minor term, middle term. Major premise: And whenever the month of fasting has entered, then fasting becomes obligatory; middle term, major term. Sought: (So whenever the crescent of Ramadan is sighted, then fasting becomes obligatory); it is the conclusion.

Commentary
This passage introduces conjunctive conditional syllogisms (قياس اقتراني شرطي). The author first defines them as syllogisms that include conditional propositions (either conjunctive or disjunctive) alongside categorical ones. He then enumerates five possible forms based on the types of premises: (1) two conjunctives producing a conjunctive, (2) two disjunctives producing a disjunctive, (3) a categorical and a conjunctive producing a conjunctive, (4) a categorical and a disjunctive producing a disjunctive, (5) a conjunctive and a disjunctive producing either a conjunctive or disjunctive. The author then redefines the terms for conditional syllogisms: the minor term becomes the antecedent (مقدم) of the conclusion, the major term the consequent (تالي) of the conclusion, and the middle term is the repeated shared term between premises. An example is given using the sighting of the Ramadan crescent: 'Whenever the crescent of Ramadan is sighted, the month of fasting has entered; whenever the month of fasting has entered, fasting becomes obligatory; therefore whenever the crescent is sighted, fasting becomes obligatory.' This is a syllogism of type 1, with two conjunctive conditionals leading to a conjunctive conclusion.

Terms in Conditional Syllogisms and the Ramadan Example
Lists five types of conjunctive conditional syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor, major, and middle terms in a conditional syllogism and apply them to a concrete Islamic example.

Core Idea
In conditional syllogisms, the antecedent is the minor term, the consequent is the major, and the repeated term is the middle.

After listing the types, the author defines the roles of terms in conditional syllogisms and provides a concrete example involving the sighting of the Ramadan crescent. This shows how the abstract framework applies to a real Islamic practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is 'Whenever the crescent of Ramadan is sighted, fasting becomes obligatory.'
2
Extract antecedent and consequent
The antecedent (muqaddam) is 'the crescent is sighted'; the consequent (tali) is 'fasting becomes obligatory'.
In a conditional, the antecedent is the condition and the consequent is the result.
3
Find the minor and major premises
The minor premise contains the antecedent of the conclusion: 'Whenever the crescent is sighted, the month of fasting has entered.' The major premise contains the consequent: 'Whenever the month of fasting has entered, fasting becomes obligatory.'
The minor premise is the one with the minor term (antecedent); the major premise has the major term (consequent).
4
Locate the middle term
The term repeated in both premises is 'the month of fasting has entered'. This is the middle term (hadd al-awsat) that connects the two premises.
The middle term is the shared term that links the antecedent to the consequent.
5
Check the syllogism type
This example is type 1: two conjunctive conditionals producing a conjunctive conclusion.
