The Five Forms of Conditional Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author enumerates five forms of conditional syllogisms with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five possible combinations of premise types in a conditional conjunctive syllogism and identify which type a given syllogism belongs to.

Core Idea
A conditional conjunctive syllogism can mix hypotheticals, disjunctives, and categoricals in five distinct patterns.

The author explains that a conjunctive syllogism may include conditional propositions, not just categorical ones. By systematically considering the types of propositions (hypothetical, disjunctive, categorical), we find exactly five possible forms. Understanding these forms is essential for analyzing complex arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three proposition types
Familiarize yourself with hypothetical (if-then), disjunctive (either-or), and categorical (subject-predicate) propositions. The author defines these as the building blocks.
Hypotheticals are called muttasila, disjunctives munfasila.
2
Enumerate all possible premise pairs
The author systematically pairs two premises drawn from the three types, ignoring order. This yields five unique combinations: two hypotheticals, two disjunctives, hypothetical + categorical, disjunctive + categorical, hypothetical + disjunctive.
3
Match each pair to its conclusion type
For each pair, note the conclusion type given by the author: two hypotheticals yield a hypothetical; two disjunctives yield a disjunctive; hypothetical + categorical yields a hypothetical; disjunctive + categorical yields a disjunctive; hypothetical + disjunctive yields either a hypothetical or a disjunctive.
The fifth form is flexible depending on which premise provides the link.
4
Identify the form of examples
Examine the provided examples: the crescent moon example uses two hypotheticals (form 1); the number example uses two disjunctives (form 2); the mineral example uses hypothetical + categorical (form 3); the mental percept example uses disjunctive + categorical (form 4); the triangle example uses hypothetical + disjunctive (form 5).
Practice by matching each example to its form number.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يتألف القياس الاقتراني من قضايا شرطية، أو يدخل فيه قضايا شرطية فيسمى عندئذ (قياساً اقترانياً شرطياً) أي : لم يتألف من قضايا حملية صرف، وإنما تألف من شرطيتين، أو من حملية وشرطية، والشرطية قد تكون متصلة وقد تكون منفصلة، وبإحصاء الاحتمالات يظهر لنا أنها وجوه خمسة :
١ - ما يتألف من متصلتين وينتج متصلة .
٢ - ما يتألف من منفصلتين وينتج منفصلة .
٣ - ما يتألف من حملية ومتصلة وينتج متصلة .
٤ - ما يتألف من حملية ومنفصلة وينتج منفصلة .
٥ - ما يتألف من متصلة ومنفصلة وينتج متصلة أو منفصلة .
و (الحد الأصغر) في القياس الاقتراني الشرطي هو (مقدم النتيجة)، والقضية المشتملة على الحد الأصغر هي «الصغرى». و (الحد الأكبر) في القياس الاقتراني الشرطي هو (تالي النتيجة)، والقضية المشتملة على الحد الأكبر هي «الكبرى». و (الحد الأوسط) في القياس الاقتراني الشرطي هو (الحد المشترك المكرر بين المقدمتين).
أ - مثال للاقتراني من شرطيتين متصلتين :
المقدمة الصغرى ← كلما رؤي هلال رمضان فقد دخل شهر الصوم
المقدمة الكبرى ← وكلما دخل شهر الصوم فقد وجب الصيام
المطلوب ← ( فكلما رؤي هلال رمضان فقد وجب الصيام )
ب - مثال للاقتراني من شرطيتين منفصلتين :
المقدمة الصغرى ← دائماً إما أن يكون العدد فرداً أو زوجاً
المقدمة الكبرى ← ودائماً إما أن يكون الزوج قابلاً للقسمة إلى فردين أو إلى زوجين
المطلوب ← ( فدائماً إما أن يكون العدد فرداً أو قابلاً للقسمة إلى فردين أو إلى زوجين )
جـ - مثال للاقتراني من متصلة وحملية :
المقدمة الصغرى ← كلما كان المستخرج من المنجم ذهباً أو حديداً فهو معدن
المقدمة الكبرى ← وكل معدن يتمدد بالحرارة
المطلوب ← ( فكلما كان المستخرج ذهباً أو حديداً فهو يتمدد بالحرارة )
د - مثال للاقتراني من منفصلة وحملية :
المقدمة الصغرى ← دائماً إما أن يكون المدرك الذهني موجوداً في الخارج أو معدوماً
المقدمة الكبرى ← وكل معدوم لا تأثير له في الوجود
المطلوب ← ( فدائماً إما أن يكون المدرك الذهني موجوداً أو لا تأثير له في الوجود )
هـ - مثال للاقتراني من متصلة ومنفصلة :
المقدمة الصغرى ← كلما كان الشكل الهندسي ذا ثلاث زوايا فهو مثلث
المقدمة الكبرى ← ودائماً إما أن يكون المثلث قائم الزاوية أو منفرج الزاوية أو حاد الزاوية
المطلوب { فكلما كان الشكل ذا ثلاث زوايا فهو إما أن يكون قائم الزاوية أو منفرج الزاوية أو حاد الزوايا }
And the conjunctive syllogism may be composed of conditional propositions, or conditional propositions may enter into it, so it is then called a 'conditional conjunctive syllogism', i.e., it is not composed of purely categorical propositions, but rather it is composed of two conditionals, or of a categorical and a conditional. The conditional may be hypothetical or disjunctive. By enumerating the possibilities, it appears to us that they are five forms:
1. What is composed of two hypotheticals and produces a hypothetical.
2. What is composed of two disjunctives and produces a disjunctive.
3. What is composed of a categorical and a hypothetical and produces a hypothetical.
4. What is composed of a categorical and a disjunctive and produces a disjunctive.
5. What is composed of a hypothetical and a disjunctive and produces a hypothetical or a disjunctive.
And (the minor term) in the conditional conjunctive syllogism is (the antecedent of the conclusion), and the proposition containing the minor term is the 'minor premise'. And (the major term) in the conditional conjunctive syllogism is (the consequent of the conclusion), and the proposition containing the major term is the 'major premise'. And (the middle term) in the conditional conjunctive syllogism is (the shared repeated term between the two premises).
A - Example of a conjunctive from two hypotheticals:
Minor premise: Whenever the crescent of Ramadan is sighted, then the month of fasting has entered.
Major premise: And whenever the month of fasting has entered, then fasting has become obligatory.
Conclusion: (So whenever the crescent of Ramadan is sighted, fasting has become obligatory.)
B - Example of a conjunctive from two disjunctives:
Minor premise: Always, either a number is odd or even.
Major premise: And always, either the even is divisible into two odd numbers or into two evens.
Conclusion: (So always, either the number is odd or divisible into two odds or into two evens.)
C - Example of a conjunctive from a hypothetical and a categorical:
Minor premise: Whenever what is extracted from the mine is gold or iron, it is a mineral.
Major premise: And every mineral expands with heat.
Conclusion: (So whenever what is extracted is gold or iron, it expands with heat.)
D - Example of a conjunctive from a disjunctive and a categorical:
Minor premise: Always, either the mental percept exists externally or is non-existent.
Major premise: And every non-existent has no effect in existence.
Conclusion: (So always, either the mental percept exists or has no effect in existence.)
E - Example of a conjunctive from a hypothetical and a disjunctive:
Minor premise: Whenever a geometric shape has three angles, it is a triangle.
Major premise: And always, either the triangle is right-angled, obtuse-angled, or acute-angled.
Conclusion: (So whenever a shape has three angles, it is either right-angled, obtuse-angled, or acute-angled.)
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the concept of conditional conjunctive syllogisms (al-qiyas al-iqtirani al-sharti). The author explains that a conjunctive syllogism may be composed entirely of conditional propositions (shartiyya) or may include them, as opposed to being purely categorical (hamli). Conditionals are of two types: hypothetical (muttasila, if-then) and disjunctive (munfasila, either-or). By exhaustive enumeration, the author identifies five possible combinations of premise types: (1) two hypotheticals yielding a hypothetical conclusion; (2) two disjunctives yielding a disjunctive; (3) a categorical with a hypothetical yielding a hypothetical; (4) a categorical with a disjunctive yielding a disjunctive; and (5) a hypothetical with a disjunctive yielding either a hypothetical or a disjunctive. The author then defines the logical terms for such syllogisms: the minor term (al-hadd al-asghar) is the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conclusion; the major term (al-hadd al-akbar) is the consequent (tali) of the conclusion; and the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) is the term repeated between the two premises. Examples are provided for each form. The first example uses an Islamic context: sighting the Ramadan crescent leads to entering the month of fasting, which obliges fasting; the conclusion chains the antecedent to the final consequent. The other examples are non-Islamic but illustrate the logical structure. In Islamic logic, such structures are used in juridical reasoning, e.g., 'Whenever prayer is valid, purity is required; whenever purity is required, water is needed; so whenever prayer is valid, water is needed.'

Key Terms
قياس اقتراني شرطي
الحد الأصغر
الحد الأكبر
الحد الأوسط

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Identifying Minor, Major, and Middle Terms in Conditional Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author defines minor, major, and middle terms for conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor, major, and middle terms in any conditional conjunctive syllogism.

Core Idea
In conditional conjunctive syllogisms, the minor term is the antecedent of the conclusion, the major term is the consequent, and the middle term is the repeated link.

The author redefines the familiar logical terms for conditional syllogisms: the minor term is the antecedent (the 'if' part) of the conclusion, the major term is the consequent (the 'then' part), and the middle term is what connects the two premises. This shift from categorical definitions is crucial for correctly analyzing these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Locate the conclusion
The conclusion is the proposition that follows 'so' (fa) in the syllogism. For example, in 'Whenever the crescent of Ramadan is sighted, fasting has become obligatory', the whole conditional is the conclusion.
2
Identify the antecedent and consequent of the conclusion
The antecedent (muqaddam) is the part after 'whenever' or 'if'; the consequent (tali) is the part after 'then'. In the example, 'the crescent of Ramadan is sighted' is the antecedent (minor term), and 'fasting has become obligatory' is the consequent (major term).
3
Find the middle term
Look for a term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. The middle term is the repeated link. In the Ramadan example, 'the month of fasting has entered' appears in both premises (once as consequent of the minor premise, once as antecedent of the major premise) and is the middle term.
4
Check the assignment of premises
The minor premise contains the minor term (antecedent of conclusion); the major premise contains the major term (consequent of conclusion). Verify that the middle term connects them appropriately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يتألف القياس الاقتراني من قضايا شرطية، أو يدخل فيه قضايا شرطية فيسمى عندئذ (قياساً اقترانياً شرطياً) أي : لم يتألف من قضايا حملية صرف، وإنما تألف من شرطيتين، أو من حملية وشرطية، والشرطية قد تكون متصلة وقد تكون منفصلة، وبإحصاء الاحتمالات يظهر لنا أنها وجوه خمسة :
١ - ما يتألف من متصلتين وينتج متصلة .
٢ - ما يتألف من منفصلتين وينتج منفصلة .
٣ - ما يتألف من حملية ومتصلة وينتج متصلة .
٤ - ما يتألف من حملية ومنفصلة وينتج منفصلة .
٥ - ما يتألف من متصلة ومنفصلة وينتج متصلة أو منفصلة .
و (الحد الأصغر) في القياس الاقتراني الشرطي هو (مقدم النتيجة)، والقضية المشتملة على الحد الأصغر هي «الصغرى». و (الحد الأكبر) في القياس الاقتراني الشرطي هو (تالي النتيجة)، والقضية المشتملة على الحد الأكبر هي «الكبرى». و (الحد الأوسط) في القياس الاقتراني الشرطي هو (الحد المشترك المكرر بين المقدمتين).
أ - مثال للاقتراني من شرطيتين متصلتين :
المقدمة الصغرى ← كلما رؤي هلال رمضان فقد دخل شهر الصوم
المقدمة الكبرى ← وكلما دخل شهر الصوم فقد وجب الصيام
المطلوب ← ( فكلما رؤي هلال رمضان فقد وجب الصيام )
ب - مثال للاقتراني من شرطيتين منفصلتين :
المقدمة الصغرى ← دائماً إما أن يكون العدد فرداً أو زوجاً
المقدمة الكبرى ← ودائماً إما أن يكون الزوج قابلاً للقسمة إلى فردين أو إلى زوجين
المطلوب ← ( فدائماً إما أن يكون العدد فرداً أو قابلاً للقسمة إلى فردين أو إلى زوجين )
جـ - مثال للاقتراني من متصلة وحملية :
المقدمة الصغرى ← كلما كان المستخرج من المنجم ذهباً أو حديداً فهو معدن
المقدمة الكبرى ← وكل معدن يتمدد بالحرارة
المطلوب ← ( فكلما كان المستخرج ذهباً أو حديداً فهو يتمدد بالحرارة )
د - مثال للاقتراني من منفصلة وحملية :
المقدمة الصغرى ← دائماً إما أن يكون المدرك الذهني موجوداً في الخارج أو معدوماً
المقدمة الكبرى ← وكل معدوم لا تأثير له في الوجود
المطلوب ← ( فدائماً إما أن يكون المدرك الذهني موجوداً أو لا تأثير له في الوجود )
هـ - مثال للاقتراني من متصلة ومنفصلة :
المقدمة الصغرى ← كلما كان الشكل الهندسي ذا ثلاث زوايا فهو مثلث
المقدمة الكبرى ← ودائماً إما أن يكون المثلث قائم الزاوية أو منفرج الزاوية أو حاد الزاوية
المطلوب { فكلما كان الشكل ذا ثلاث زوايا فهو إما أن يكون قائم الزاوية أو منفرج الزاوية أو حاد الزوايا }
And the conjunctive syllogism may be composed of conditional propositions, or conditional propositions may enter into it, so it is then called a 'conditional conjunctive syllogism', i.e., it is not composed of purely categorical propositions, but rather it is composed of two conditionals, or of a categorical and a conditional. The conditional may be hypothetical or disjunctive. By enumerating the possibilities, it appears to us that they are five forms:
1. What is composed of two hypotheticals and produces a hypothetical.
2. What is composed of two disjunctives and produces a disjunctive.
3. What is composed of a categorical and a hypothetical and produces a hypothetical.
4. What is composed of a categorical and a disjunctive and produces a disjunctive.
5. What is composed of a hypothetical and a disjunctive and produces a hypothetical or a disjunctive.
And (the minor term) in the conditional conjunctive syllogism is (the antecedent of the conclusion), and the proposition containing the minor term is the 'minor premise'. And (the major term) in the conditional conjunctive syllogism is (the consequent of the conclusion), and the proposition containing the major term is the 'major premise'. And (the middle term) in the conditional conjunctive syllogism is (the shared repeated term between the two premises).
A - Example of a conjunctive from two hypotheticals:
Minor premise: Whenever the crescent of Ramadan is sighted, then the month of fasting has entered.
Major premise: And whenever the month of fasting has entered, then fasting has become obligatory.
Conclusion: (So whenever the crescent of Ramadan is sighted, fasting has become obligatory.)
B - Example of a conjunctive from two disjunctives:
Minor premise: Always, either a number is odd or even.
Major premise: And always, either the even is divisible into two odd numbers or into two evens.
Conclusion: (So always, either the number is odd or divisible into two odds or into two evens.)
C - Example of a conjunctive from a hypothetical and a categorical:
Minor premise: Whenever what is extracted from the mine is gold or iron, it is a mineral.
Major premise: And every mineral expands with heat.
Conclusion: (So whenever what is extracted is gold or iron, it expands with heat.)
D - Example of a conjunctive from a disjunctive and a categorical:
Minor premise: Always, either the mental percept exists externally or is non-existent.
Major premise: And every non-existent has no effect in existence.
Conclusion: (So always, either the mental percept exists or has no effect in existence.)
E - Example of a conjunctive from a hypothetical and a disjunctive:
Minor premise: Whenever a geometric shape has three angles, it is a triangle.
Major premise: And always, either the triangle is right-angled, obtuse-angled, or acute-angled.
Conclusion: (So whenever a shape has three angles, it is either right-angled, obtuse-angled, or acute-angled.)
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the concept of conditional conjunctive syllogisms (al-qiyas al-iqtirani al-sharti). The author explains that a conjunctive syllogism may be composed entirely of conditional propositions (shartiyya) or may include them, as opposed to being purely categorical (hamli). Conditionals are of two types: hypothetical (muttasila, if-then) and disjunctive (munfasila, either-or). By exhaustive enumeration, the author identifies five possible combinations of premise types: (1) two hypotheticals yielding a hypothetical conclusion; (2) two disjunctives yielding a disjunctive; (3) a categorical with a hypothetical yielding a hypothetical; (4) a categorical with a disjunctive yielding a disjunctive; and (5) a hypothetical with a disjunctive yielding either a hypothetical or a disjunctive. The author then defines the logical terms for such syllogisms: the minor term (al-hadd al-asghar) is the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conclusion; the major term (al-hadd al-akbar) is the consequent (tali) of the conclusion; and the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) is the term repeated between the two premises. Examples are provided for each form. The first example uses an Islamic context: sighting the Ramadan crescent leads to entering the month of fasting, which obliges fasting; the conclusion chains the antecedent to the final consequent. The other examples are non-Islamic but illustrate the logical structure. In Islamic logic, such structures are used in juridical reasoning, e.g., 'Whenever prayer is valid, purity is required; whenever purity is required, water is needed; so whenever prayer is valid, water is needed.'

Key Terms
الحد الأصغر
الحد الأكبر
الحد الأوسط
مقدم النتيجة
تالي النتيجة

The Syllogism

Mixed-Premise Conjunctive Syllogisms: Four Examples
Author provides four examples of iqtirani with different combinations of conditional and categorical premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the premises, middle term, and conclusion in conjunctive syllogisms that combine conditional and categorical premises.

Core Idea
Conjunctive syllogisms work with any premise type as long as the middle term links them.

Having learned the basic structure of conjunctive syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani), you now see how they operate even when premises are not both categorical. The author gives four examples mixing muttasila (conjunctive conditional), munfasila (disjunctive), and hamliyya (categorical) premises. Each example is a valid first-figure syllogism where the middle term appears in both premises. By analyzing these, you will gain mastery in recognizing syllogistic forms beyond simple categoricals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion (al-matlub)
In each example, find the sentence introduced by 'المطلوب' (the required) or marked as the conclusion. It combines the minor and major terms and follows necessarily from the premises.
The conclusion is set off by parentheses or braces in the text.
2
Label the terms: minor, middle, major
For each example, the author explicitly writes 'حد أصغر', 'حد أوسط', 'حد أكبر' under the premises. Verify that the middle term appears in both premises but not in the conclusion, and that the minor term is the subject of the conclusion.
The middle term is the key linking element.
3
Classify the premise types
Determine whether each premise is hamliyya (categorical: 'every X is Y'), muttasila (conjunctive conditional: 'whenever X is Y, Z is W'), or munfasila (disjunctive: 'always either X is Y or X is Z'). Note the combination types in each example.
Example ب has two munfasila; ج has muttasila and hamliyya; د has munfasila and hamliyya; هـ has muttasila and munfasila.
4
Verify syllogistic validity
Check that the middle term is distributed and that the conclusion is a direct combination of the minor and major terms without any extra term. Since these are first-figure examples, the validity is assured by the author's construction.
The middle term is the predicate of the minor premise and the subject of the major premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - مثال للاقتراني من شرطيتين منفصلتين :
المقدمة الصغرى ← دائماً إما أن يكون العدد فرداً أو زوجاً
حد أصغر حد أوسط
المقدمة الكبرى ← ودائماً إما أن يكون الزوج قابلاً للقسمة إلى فردين أو إلى زوجين
حد أوسط حد أكبر
المطلوب ← ( فدائماً إما أن يكون العدد فرداً أو قابلاً للقسمة إلى فردين أو إلى زوجين ) جد وهو النتيجة
جـ - مثال للاقتراني من متصلة وحملية :
المقدمة الصغرى ← كلما كان المستخرج من المنجم ذهباً أو حديداً فهو معدن
حد أصغر حد أوسط
المقدمة الكبرى ← وكل معدن يتمدد بالحرارة
حد أوسط حد أكبر
المطلوب ← ( فكلما كان المستخرج ذهباً أو حديداً فهو يتمدد بالحرارة ) جد وهو النتيجة
د - مثال للاقتراني من منفصلة وحملية :
المقدمة الصغرى ← دائماً إما أن يكون المدرك الذهني موجوداً في الخارج أو معدوماً
حد أصغر حد أوسط
المقدمة الكبرى ← وكل معدوم لا تأثير له في الوجود
حد أوسط حد أكبر
المطلوب ← ( فدائماً إما أن يكون المدرك الذهني موجوداً أو لا تأثير له في الوجود ) جد وهو النتيجة
هـ - مثال للاقتراني من متصلة ومنفصلة :
المقدمة الصغرى ← كلما كان الشكل الهندسي ذا ثلاث زوايا فهو مثلث
حد أصغر حد أوسط
المقدمة الكبرى ← ودائماً إما أن يكون المثلث قائم الزاوية أو منفرج الزاوية أو حاد الزاوية
حد أوسط حد أكبر
المطلوب { فكلما كان الشكل ذا ثلاث زوايا فهو إما أن يكون قائم الزاوية أو منفرج الزاوية أو حاد الزوايا } وهو النتيجة { أو دائماً إما أن يكون الشكل ذو الزوايا الثلاث قائم الزاوية أو منفرج الزاوية أو حاد الزوايا }
B - Example of a conjunctive syllogism from two disjunctives:
Minor premise: Always, the number is either odd or even.
Minor term: minor; Middle term: middle.
Major premise: And always, the even is either divisible into two odds or into two evens.
Middle term: middle; Major term: major.
Conclusion: (Thus, always the number is either odd or divisible into two odds or into two evens.) That is the result.

J - Example of a conjunctive syllogism from a conjunctive and a categorical:
Minor premise: Whenever what is extracted from the mine is gold or iron, it is a mineral.
Minor term: minor; Middle term: middle.
Major premise: And every mineral expands with heat.
Middle term: middle; Major term: major.
Conclusion: (Thus, whenever what is extracted is gold or iron, it expands with heat.) That is the result.

D - Example of a conjunctive syllogism from a disjunctive and a categorical:
Minor premise: Always, the mental percept is either existent externally or non-existent.
Minor term: minor; Middle term: middle.
Major premise: And every non-existent has no effect in existence.
Middle term: middle; Major term: major.
Conclusion: (Thus, always the mental percept is either existent or has no effect in existence.) That is the result.

H - Example of a conjunctive syllogism from a conjunctive and a disjunctive:
Minor premise: Whenever a geometric shape has three angles, it is a triangle.
Minor term: minor; Middle term: middle.
Major premise: And always, the triangle is either right-angled, obtuse-angled, or acute-angled.
Middle term: middle; Major term: major.
Conclusion: {Thus, whenever a shape has three angles, it is either right-angled, obtuse-angled, or acute-angled.} That is the result {Or: always, a shape with three angles is either right-angled, obtuse-angled, or acute-angled.}
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents four diverse examples of the conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani), demonstrating how conditional premises (muttasila and munfasila) combine with each other and with categorical premises (hamliyya) to yield necessary conclusions. The author labels each example and identifies the terms: minor term (حد أصغر), middle term (حد أوسط), and major term (حد أكبر). In each case, the conclusion follows necessarily from the conjunction of the premises, as both premises share the middle term. For instance, in the first example (ب), two disjunctive premises about numbers and evens produce a disjunctive conclusion about numbers. In the second (ج), a conditional (gold/iron → mineral) and a categorical (minerals expand) yield a conditional conclusion. The third (د) combines a disjunctive (mental percept exists or not) with a categorical (non-existent has no effect) to produce a disjunctive conclusion. The fourth (هـ) uses a conditional (three angles → triangle) and a disjunctive (triangle angles types) to conclude about the shape's angles. These examples illustrate the flexibility of the iqtirani form across different premise types.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Conditions for Valid Conjunctive Syllogism
Author questions whether every combination of premises yields a valid syllogism and gives a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given combination of premises in a conjunctive syllogism is a valid syllogism or a sterile combination.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism requires necessary entailment, not just true premises.

In previous lessons, you learned the basic structure of a conjunctive syllogism. But not every combination of two premises with a shared middle term automatically counts as a valid syllogism. The author warns that some combinations are 'sterile' (aqim), yielding no necessary conclusion. In this lesson, you will learn the critical distinction between a genuine syllogism and an accidental conjunction of true statements, using the example of two negative premises.

Guided Path
1
Extract the premises and middle term
From the example 'No human is a stone. No stone is an animal', identify the middle term (stone) and note that both premises are universal negatives.
2
Test a possible conclusion
What conclusion might be drawn? The author mentions 'No human is an animal' or 'Some human is not an animal'. Check if this conclusion follows necessarily from the premises.
The attempted conclusion is not entailed by the premises.
3
Evaluate the necessity of the conclusion
Even though the premises are true, does the conclusion have to be true? The author notes that changing the example (stone to plant) could yield a true conclusion by chance, but the form itself does not guarantee it. So the conclusion is not necessary.
4
Classify the combination as sterile
Because the form does not always produce a true conclusion when premises are true, it is called 'aqim (sterile). A genuine syllogism must be productive in all cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هل كل اقتران بين القضايا قياس؟ :
علمنا أن القياس الاقتراني طريقه مثل ما عرضنا في الأمثلة السابقة، من القياس الاقتراني الحملي، ومن القياس الاقتراني الشرطي .
ولكن ينبغي أن نعلم أنه ليس كل اقتران بين قضايا يجمعها حد أوسط مفيداً نتيجة صحيحة ملزمة .
وكل اقتران لا يفيد بشكله المنضبط نتيجةً صحيحةً ملزمة حتماً لدى التسليم بالقضيتين المقترنتين، إذ ينخرم في بعض أحواله، فإنه لا يسمى قياساً أصلاً، فمثلاً إذا قلنا :
[لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر. ولا شيء من الحجر بحيوان].
لم ينتج إنتاجاً صحيحاً، لأننا سنقول :
[لا شيء من الإنسان بحيوان، أو بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان]
وكلاهما كاذبتان .
إذن : فالاقتران على مثل هذه الصيغة في قضيتين سالبتين اقتران عقيم غير منتج، رغم التسليم بصدق القضيتين، ورغم تقديمه نتيجة صحيحة في بعض صوره كما لو غيرنا في المثال الحيوان بالنبات .
لذلك كان لا بد لنا من تحديد للصور المنتجة بأشكالها، وشروطها، وكيفية إنتاجها .
لم سُمِّي هذا القياس اقترانياً؟
وبسبب ما يظهر في القياس من اقتران قضاياه دون أن تتوسط بينها أداة استثناء أو استدراك تبين لنا لم سُمِّي قياساً اقترانياً؟ .
Is every combination of propositions a syllogism?:
We know that the conjunctive syllogism proceeds as presented in the previous examples, including categorical conjunctive syllogisms and conditional conjunctive syllogisms.
But we must know that not every combination of propositions united by a middle term yields a valid necessary conclusion.
And any combination that, in its regulated form, does not necessarily yield a valid conclusion when the two combined propositions are accepted, because it fails in some cases, is not called a syllogism at all. For example, if we say:
[No human is a stone. No stone is an animal.]
It does not produce a valid production, because we would say:
[No human is an animal, or Some human is not an animal.]
Both are false.
Thus, a combination in this form of two negative premises is a sterile, unproductive combination, despite accepting the truth of the two premises, and despite it yielding a correct conclusion in some forms, as if we changed 'animal' to 'plant' in the example.
Therefore, we must specify the productive forms, their figures, conditions, and how they produce.
Why is this syllogism called 'conjunctive'?
And because of what appears in the syllogism of the conjunction of its propositions without an exception or retraction particle intervening between them, we understand why it is called a conjunctive syllogism.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage addresses a crucial question: does any combination of two premises with a shared middle term automatically constitute a valid syllogism? The author clarifies that it does not. Using the example of two universal negatives (no human is a stone, no stone is an animal), the author shows that the premises are true but do not yield a necessary true conclusion. The attempted conclusion (no human is an animal) is false. Interestingly, the author notes that changing the example (e.g., substituting 'plant' for 'animal') might accidentally produce a true conclusion, but the form itself remains invalid. Therefore, to be a genuine qiyas iqtirani, the combination must necessarily produce a valid conclusion in all cases. Finally, the author explains the naming: 'iqtirani' (conjunctive) because the premises are simply conjoined without any exceptive or retractive particle (like 'illa' or 'lakin'), distinguishing it from qiyas istithna'i (exceptive syllogism).

Key Terms

اقتران عقيم

Checkpoint
Why does the combination of 'No human is a stone' and 'No stone is an animal' fail to produce a valid conclusion?
A) Because one premise is false.
B) Because two negative premises cannot yield a necessary conclusion in categorical logic.
C) Because the middle term is not distributed.
D) Because the conclusion is always false.

Key Takeaway
Not every conjunction of true premises with a shared term is a valid syllogism; the form must guarantee the conclusion in all cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Construct an example of a sterile conjunctive syllogism using terms from Islamic law (e.g., halal/haram, wajib/mubah). Identify the middle term and explain why it fails.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Not Every Pairing of Propositions Is a Valid Syllogism
Author establishes that valid syllogisms require specific forms and begins deriving the four figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a sterile syllogistic form (two negative premises) and distinguish it from a formally valid syllogism.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism requires a specific form, not just a shared middle term.

In logic, it is tempting to think that any two propositions with a common term automatically yield a conclusion. But the author warns us: not every iqtiran (conjunction) is a qiyas (syllogism). He gives a powerful counterexample: two universal negative premises produce a sterile combination. Let's examine why this fails and learn what makes a syllogism truly valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example
Write the two premises: 'Nothing of man is stone' and 'Nothing of stone is animal'.
2
Attempt a conclusion
The author tries 'Nothing of man is animal' or 'Some man is not animal'. Why are these false? Because some men are animals (humans are animals).
3
Recognize the sterility
Even though the premises are true, the form does not guarantee a true conclusion. The conjunction is 'sterile' (aqim) – it does not produce a necessary result.
4
Understand formal validity
Occasional true results (e.g., changing 'animal' to 'plant') do not make the form valid. Validity requires that the conclusion necessarily follows in every instance of the form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هل كل اقتران بين القضايا قياس؟ : علمنا أن القياس الاقتراني طريقه مثل ما عرضنا في الأمثلة السابقة، من القياس الاقتراني الحملي، ومن القياس الاقتراني الشرطي . ولكن ينبغي أن نعلم أنه ليس كل اقتران بين قضايا يجمعها حد أوسط مفيداً نتيجة صحيحة ملزمة . وكل اقتران لا يفيد بشكله المنضبط نتيجةً صحيحةً ملزمة حتماً لدى التسليم بالقضيتين المقترنتين، إذ ينخرم في بعض أحواله، فإنه لا يسمى قياساً أصلاً، فمثلاً إذا قلنا : [لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر. ولا شيء من الحجر بحيوان]. لم ينتج إنتاجاً صحيحاً، لأننا سنقول : [لا شيء من الإنسان بحيوان، أو بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان] وكلاهما كاذبتان . إذن : فالاقتران على مثل هذه الصيغة في قضيتين سالبتين اقتران عقيم غير منتج، رغم التسليم بصدق القضيتين، ورغم تقديمه نتيجة صحيحة في بعض صوره كما لو غيرنا في المثال الحيوان بالنبات . لذلك كان لا بد لنا من تحديد للصور المنتجة بأشكالها، وشروطها، وكيفية إنتاجها . لم سُمِّي هذا القياس اقترانياً؟ وبسبب ما يظهر في القياس من اقتران قضاياه دون أن تتوسط بينها أداة استثناء أو استدراك تبين لنا لم سُمِّي قياساً اقترانياً؟ .
أشكال القياس الاقتراني وضروبه المنتجة انطلاقاً من وضع الحد الأوسط في مقدمتي القياس بالنسبة إلى الحدين الآخرين الأصغر والأكبر، نستطيع بمقتضى القسمة العقلية المستقصية، أن نستخرج أربعة أشكال للقياس الاقتراني بقسميه الحملي والشرطي. فلنأخذ واقع المقدمتين الصغرى والكبرى بأجزائهما في الشكل التالي: هيكل مقدمتي قياس اقتراني الصغرى: موضوع + محمول (في الحمليات) / مقدم + تالي (في الشرطيات). الكبرى: موضوع + محمول / مقدم + تالي. هذه أجزاء أربعة، يأخذ الحد الأوسط منها جزأين ليسقطا في النتيجة، ويبقى للنتيجة جزءان. الصغرى الكبرى رمزه ١ - فقد يأخذ الجزأين الثاني والثالث: المحمول و الموضوع (ح - ض) / التالي و المقدم (ت - م) ٢ - وقد يأخذ الجزأين الثالث والرابع: المحمول و المحمول (ح - ح) / التالي و التالي (ت - ت)
Is every conjunction of propositions a syllogism? We have known that the conjunctive syllogism proceeds as we presented in the previous examples, from the categorical conjunctive syllogism and from the conditional conjunctive syllogism. But it is necessary for us to know that not every conjunction of propositions that a middle term brings together yields a correct necessary result. And every conjunction that does not in its precise form yield a correct necessary result necessarily, given the admission of the two conjoined propositions, because it fails in some of its conditions, is not called a syllogism at all. For example, if we say: 'Nothing of man is stone. And nothing of stone is animal.' It does not produce a correct production, because we will say: 'Nothing of man is animal, or some man is not animal,' and both are false. Therefore, the conjunction in such a form of two negative propositions is a sterile, non-productive conjunction, despite admitting the truth of the two premises, and despite it giving a correct result in some of its forms, as if we changed in the example 'animal' to 'plant.' Therefore it was necessary for us to define the productive forms with their figures, their conditions, and how to produce them. Why is this syllogism called 'conjunctive'? And because of what appears in the syllogism of the conjunction of its propositions without an exception or correction tool intervening between them, it becomes clear to us why it is called a conjunctive syllogism.
The figures of the conjunctive syllogism and its productive moods. Starting from the position of the middle term in the two premises of the syllogism in relation to the other two terms, the minor and the major, we are able, by virtue of exhaustive rational division, to extract four figures for the conjunctive syllogism with its two divisions: categorical and conditional. Let us take the reality of the minor and major premises with their parts in the following form: The structure of two premises of a conjunctive syllogism. Minor: subject + predicate (in categoricals) / antecedent + consequent (in conditionals). Major: subject + predicate / antecedent + consequent. These are four parts. The middle term takes two of them to fall away in the conclusion, and there remain two parts for the conclusion. Minor Major Code: 1 - It might take the second and third parts: the predicate and the subject (P - S) / the consequent and the antecedent (C - A). 2 - And it might take the third and fourth parts: the predicate and the predicate (P - P) / the consequent and the consequent (C - C).
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the conditions for a valid conjunctive syllogism. It begins with the question: Is every combination of propositions a syllogism? The author clarifies that not every pairing, even if united by a middle term, yields a necessary conclusion. An example is given: 'No human is a stone' and 'No stone is an animal'—these two negative premises lead to a conclusion that is false (either 'No human is an animal' or 'Some human is not animal'). Thus, the combination is sterile. However, the author notes that in some instances, such as substituting 'animal' with 'plant', the same form might yield a true conclusion, but that does not make the form universally valid. Therefore, it is necessary to systematically determine which forms are productive. The author then introduces the method for deriving the four figures based on the position of the middle term in the two premises. Using exhaustive division, four possible arrangements are identified. The passage outlines the structure with two premises each having two parts (subject/predicate or antecedent/consequent), totaling four parts. The middle term occupies two of these, leaving two for the conclusion. The first two arrangements are listed: (1) middle term as predicate of the minor and subject of the major; (2) middle term as predicate of both premises. The text cuts off before listing the remaining two figures. The commentary should note that this passage serves as an introduction to the figures of the syllogism, emphasizing that validity requires specific formal conditions, not just any conjunction.

Checkpoint
Why does the example 'Nothing of man is stone' and 'Nothing of stone is animal' fail to produce a valid syllogistic conclusion?
A) Because the premises are both negative, which never yields a conclusion.
B) Because the middle term 'stone' is not in the correct position.
C) Because the conclusion would necessarily be false, as shown.
D) Because the form is sterile despite sometimes giving a true result; the form itself is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Not every conjunction of two propositions, even with a shared middle term, is a valid syllogism; the form must be productive in all cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid syllogism about an Islamic legal ruling (e.g., about nabidh) using the first figure. Then write an invalid version with two negative premises and explain why it fails.
?

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Deriving the Figures of the Syllogism
Author establishes that valid syllogisms require specific forms and begins deriving the four figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the four figures of the conjunctive syllogism based on the position of the middle term and identify the first two figures.

Core Idea
The middle term's position in the premises determines the figure of the syllogism.

Having established that not every pairing is valid, the author introduces the method for finding valid forms: exhaustive rational division based on where the middle term appears. The premises have four parts (subject, predicate for categoricals; antecedent, consequent for conditionals). The middle term occupies two of these; the remaining two form the conclusion. By systematically examining all possibilities, we derive four figures. This lesson covers the division and the first two figures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parts of premises
For categorical syllogisms, each premise has a subject and predicate. For conditionals, antecedent and consequent. So two premises give four parts.
2
Understand the middle term's role
The middle term appears in both premises and takes up two of the four parts. The other two parts become the subject and predicate of the conclusion.
3
List the four possible positions
The author enumerates two: (1) middle term as predicate of minor and subject of major; (2) as predicate of both. The other two by analogy: (3) subject of both; (4) subject of minor and predicate of major.
These correspond to the four figures (shakl).
4
Link to standard figures
In classical logic, these arrangements are the four figures. The first figure (most productive) has the middle term as subject of major and predicate of minor.
Check which figure matches the first arrangement given.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
The Four Figures of the Categorical Syllogism
Author classifies the four figures based on middle term placement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four figures of the categorical syllogism based on the placement of the middle term in the premises.

Core Idea
Four figures arise from four possible positions of the middle term.

In Islamic logic, the categorical syllogism is a cornerstone of reasoning in jurisprudence and theology. This lesson teaches you to classify syllogisms into four figures by examining where the middle term sits in each premise. Mastering this classification is the first step to evaluating argument validity.

Guided Path
1
Understand premise structure
Each premise in a categorical syllogism has a subject and a predicate. For conditional syllogisms, each premise has an antecedent and a consequent. The author uses symbols: S (subject), P (predicate) for categorical; A (antecedent), C (consequent) for conditional.
2
Identify the middle term
The middle term is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. It connects the minor and major terms.
3
Determine its position in each premise
For each premise, decide whether the middle term is the subject or the predicate (or antecedent/consequent in conditionals). There are four possible combinations.
4
Match to figure symbols
Use the author's symbols: For categorical, 1st figure: P-S (predicate in minor, subject in major); 2nd: P-P; 3rd: S-S; 4th: S-P. For conditionals, replace P with C (consequent) and S with A (antecedent).
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is haram. Every nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore, every nabidh is haram.' The middle term 'intoxicant' is predicate in the major premise and subject in the minor, so it is first figure (P-S).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أشكال القياس الاقتراني وضروبه المنتجة انطلاقاً من وضع الحد الأوسط في مقدمتي القياس بالنسبة إلى الحدين الآخرين الأصغر والأكبر، نستطيع بمقتضى القسمة العقلية المستقصية، أن نستخرج أربعة أشكال للقياس الاقتراني بقسميه الحملي والشرطي. فلنأخذ واقع المقدمتين الصغرى والكبرى بأجزائهما في الشكل التالي: هيكل مقدمتي قياس اقتراني الصغرى موضوع + محمول مقدم + تالي ١ ٢ الكبرى موضوع + محمول مقدم + تالي ٣ ٤ في الحمليات في الشرطيات هذه أجزاء أربعة، يأخذ الحد الأوسط منها جزأين ليسقطا في النتيجة، ويبقى للنتيجة جزءان. الصغرى الكبرى رمزه ١ - فقد يأخذ الجزأين الثاني والثالث: المحمول و الموضوع ح - ض التالي و المقدم ت - م ٢ - وقد يأخذ الجزأين الثالث والرابع: المحمول و المحمول ح - ح التالي و التالي ت - ت ٣ - وقد يأخذ الجزأين الأول والثالث : الموضوع و الموضوع ض - ض المقدم و المقدم م - م ٤ - وقد يأخذ الجزأين الأول والرابع : الموضوع و المحمول ض - ح المقدم و التالي م - ت رموز الأشكال الأربعة في الحملي رموز الأشكال الأربعة في الشرطي ١ - ح ض ١ - ت - م ٢ - ح ح ح = محمول ٢ - ت - ت ت = تالي ٣ - ض ض ض = موضوع ٣ - م - م م = مقدم ٤ - ض ح ٤ - م - ت فهذه أربعة أشكال : الشكل الأول : يكون الحد الأوسط فيه محمولاً في الصغرى موضوعاً في الكبرى الأول (ح ض) (ت م) الشكل الثاني : يكون الحد الأوسط فيه محمولاً في الصغرى محمولاً في الكبرى الثاني (ح ح) (ت ت) الشكل الثالث : يكون الحد الأوسط فيه موضوعاً في الصغرى موضوعاً في الكبرى الثالث (ض ض) (م م) الشكل الرابع : يكون الحد الأوسط فيه موضوعاً في الصغرى محمولاً في الكبرى الرابع (ض ح) (م ت)
The figures of the categorical syllogism and its productive moods. Starting from the position of the middle term in the two premises of the syllogism relative to the other two terms, the minor and the major, we can, by means of exhaustive rational division, extract four figures for the categorical syllogism in its two divisions: categorical and conditional. Let us take the reality of the minor and major premises with their parts in the following form: Structure of the two premises of a categorical syllogism: Minor: Subject + Predicate, Antecedent + Consequent 1 2; Major: Subject + Predicate, Antecedent + Consequent 3 4. In categoricals, in conditionals. These are four parts; the middle term takes two of them so that they drop out in the conclusion, and two parts remain for the conclusion. Minor Major Symbol: 1 - It may take the second and third parts: Predicate and Subject P - S, Consequent and Antecedent C - A. 2 - It may take the third and fourth parts: Predicate and Predicate P - P, Consequent and Consequent C - C. 3 - It may take the first and third parts: Subject and Subject S - S, Antecedent and Antecedent A - A. 4 - It may take the first and fourth parts: Subject and Predicate S - P, Antecedent and Consequent A - C. Symbols of the four figures in categorical: 1 - P S, 2 - P P (P = Predicate), 3 - S S (S = Subject), 4 - S P. Symbols of the four figures in conditional: 1 - C - A, 2 - C - C (C = Consequent), 3 - A - A (A = Antecedent), 4 - A - C. These are four figures: First figure: the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major. First (P S) (C A). Second figure: the middle term is predicate in the minor and predicate in the major. Second (P P) (C C). Third figure: the middle term is subject in the minor and subject in the major. Third (S S) (A A). Fourth figure: the middle term is subject in the minor and predicate in the major. Fourth (S P) (A C).
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage defines the four figures of the categorical syllogism (al-qiyas al-iqtirani) by analyzing the placement of the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) in the two premises. The author begins by stating that the figures are derived from an exhaustive rational division (al-qisma al-‘aqliyya al-mustaqsiya) based on how the middle term is positioned relative to the minor term (al-asghar) and major term (al-akbar). The syllogism has two divisions: categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti), each with its own terms—subject/predicate for categorical, and antecedent/consequent for conditional. The author presents a diagram of the four parts: each premise has two parts (subject and predicate for categorical; antecedent and consequent for conditional), making four parts total. The middle term occupies two of these parts, which drop out of the conclusion, leaving two parts for the conclusion (the minor and major terms). The four possible combinations of the middle term's places yield four figures. Using symbols: P (predicate/محمول), S (subject/موضوع) for categorical; C (consequent/تالي), A (antecedent/مقدم) for conditional. The figures are enumerated: First figure: middle term is predicate in minor and subject in major (P S / C A). Second: predicate in both (P P / C C). Third: subject in both (S S / A A). Fourth: subject in minor and predicate in major (S P / A C). The author then names each figure with its symbol. This classification is foundational for determining valid syllogistic moods. For example, in Islamic legal reasoning, a syllogism like 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden (haram). Every nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore, every nabidh is forbidden' fits the first figure (middle term 'intoxicant' is predicate in the major premise and subject in the minor premise). Understanding these figures allows one to evaluate the validity of arguments in fiqh, kalam, and other Islamic sciences.

The Four Figures and Their Symbols
Author defines the four figures with symbols for categorical and conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the symbol pair for each figure of the syllogism (categorical and conditional) and state the position of the middle term in each figure.

Core Idea
The four figures are defined by the position of the middle term in the premises.

To work with syllogisms in Islamic logic, you must first recognize the four possible arrangements of the middle term. This lesson gives you the symbols and patterns to quickly identify any figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parts of a proposition
A categorical proposition has a subject (موضوع) and predicate (محمول); a conditional has an antecedent (مقدم) and consequent (تالي). The author combines these parts in two ways.
First and third parts = subject-subject or antecedent-antecedent; first and fourth = subject-predicate or antecedent-consequent.
2
Recognize the four combination patterns
The combinations yield four patterns corresponding to the four figures. The author presents them in a table of symbols: ح ض, ح ح, ض ض, ض ح for categorical and ت م, ت ت, م م, م ت for conditional.
ح = predicate (محمول), ض = subject (موضوع), م = antecedent (مقدم), ت = consequent (تالي).
3
Match symbols to middle term position
For each figure, describe where the middle term appears. First figure: predicate in minor, subject in major (ح ض). Second: predicate in both (ح ح). Third: subject in both (ض ض). Fourth: subject in minor, predicate in major (ض ح).
The same pattern holds for conditional with م and ت.
4
Memorize the four symbol pairs
Use the mnemonic: First figure: predicate–subject; second: predicate–predicate; third: subject–subject; fourth: subject–predicate.
Practice writing the symbols and saying the position aloud.

Commentary
This passage defines the four figures of the syllogism, both categorical (حملي) and conditional (شرطي), provided symbols for each, describes the position of the middle term in each figure, discusses the order of perfection of the figures, and begins to introduce the moods. The author first explains that the parts of a proposition can be combined in different ways: taking the first and third parts (subject and subject, antecedent and antecedent) or first and fourth parts (subject and predicate, antecedent and consequent). These combinations yield four figures. A table of symbols is given: for categorical syllogisms, the symbols use ḥ (for predicate), ḍ (for subject); for conditional syllogisms, t (for consequent) and m (for antecedent). The four figures are then described in terms of the position of the middle term (الحد الأوسط). In the first figure, the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major (i.e., consequent in the minor, antecedent in the major). In the second figure, it is predicate in both (consequent in both). In the third figure, it is subject in both (antecedent in both). In the fourth figure, it is subject in the minor and predicate in the major (antecedent in the minor, consequent in the major). The author then states that the figures are ordered by perfection, with the first figure being the most perfect because it follows the natural progression from smallest to largest, its validity is self-evident, and it alone produces all four types of categorical propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). The singular proposition is treated as equivalent to the universal, and the indefinite as equivalent to the particular. Finally, the author introduces the concept of moods (ضروب), which arise from the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the premises. A rational division yields 16 possible moods for each figure, some of which are productive in all materials. The text cuts off, but the discussion of moods continues beyond this window.

The Perfection of the First Figure and Introduction to Moods
Author discusses the order of perfection of the figures and introduces the concept of moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the first figure is the most perfect and define the concept of a mood (ḍarb) in a syllogism.

Core Idea
The first figure is most perfect because it is self-evidently valid and yields all four proposition types.

Now that you know the four figures, the author explains why the first figure stands out as the most perfect. He also begins to introduce the notion of moods, which classify syllogisms by the quantity and quality of their premises.

Guided Path
1
Understand the order of perfection
The author states that the figures are ordered according to their perfection (كمال). The first figure is the most perfect.
The order is first, second, third, fourth from most to least perfect.
2
Identify the reasons for the first figure's perfection
Three reasons: (1) natural sequence of thought (smallest to middle to largest), (2) self-evident productivity (ظاهر الإنتاج بنفسه), (3) it produces all four categorical propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative).
Singular propositions are treated as universal; indefinite as particular.
3
Define a mood (ḍarb)
A mood is the combination of the two premises in terms of quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative). Each premise can be one of four types.
Quantity and quality are called الكم والكيف.
4
Calculate the number of moods per figure
Since each premise has 4 possibilities, there are 4×4=16 possible moods per figure. Some are productive in all materials, others only in some.
The passage begins to list the combinations but is cut off.

The Fourth Figure and the Perfection of the First Figure
Defines the fourth figure, ranks figures by completeness, and introduces the 16 possible moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining position of the middle term in each figure and explain why the first figure is considered most perfect.

Core Idea
The first figure mirrors natural reasoning and yields all four categorical conclusions.

This passage classifies the four figures of the categorical syllogism by the position of the middle term. The author defines the fourth figure and then ranks the figures, declaring the first the most perfect because of its natural sequence and self-evident productivity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term's position in the fourth figure
In the fourth figure, the middle term is subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major premise. Contrast with the first figure where the middle is subject in the major and predicate in the minor.
The symbols (ض ح) and (م ت) are mnemonic patterns; ignore them for now.
2
Extract the criteria for ranking perfection
The text gives two reasons: natural sequence of thought (from smallest to largest term) and self-evident productivity. The first figure does not need conversion or reduction.
3
Compare productivity across figures
The first figure produces all four standard categorical propositions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Other figures do not. This makes it the most perfect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشكل الرابع : يكون الحد الأوسط فيه موضوعاً في الصغرى محمولاً في الكبرى مقدماً في الصغرى تالياً في الكبرى (ض ح) (م ت) وهذه الأشكال مرتبة بحسب كمالها، ولا بد أن نلاحظ أن أكملها هو الشكل الأول، لأنه أكثر ملاءمة للتسلسل الطبيعي، إذ ينتقل فيه الفكر من الأصغر إلى الأوسط فالأكبر، ولأنه ظاهر الإنتاج بنفسه، ثم إنه ينتج القضايا الأربع دون غيره من الأشكال، فيأتي في إنتاجه (الكلية الموجبة، والكلية السالبة، والجزئية الموجبة، والجزئية السالبة). أما الشخصية فهي بقوة الكلية، وأما المهملة فهي بقوة الجزئية. ولكل شكل من هذه الأشكال الأربعة، ضروب ناتجة عن اتفاق مقدمتي القياس في الكم والكيف، أو اختلاف المقدمتين فيهما أو في أحدهما، وبيان ذلك فيما يلي : الصغرى الكبرى كلية موجبة كلية موجبة سالبة سالبة جزئية موجبة جزئية موجبة سالبة سالبة فالضروب التي تتحصل لدينا بموجب القسمة العقلية لكل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة هي «١٦» ضرباً. وبعض هذه الضروب منتج في كل المواد التي توضع فيه، وبعضها عقيم لاحتمال فساد إنتاجه في بعض المواد. فلنبحث في كل شكل من هذه الأشكال، وفي ضروبه المنتجة.
The fourth figure: the middle term is subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major premise, antecedent in the minor and consequent in the major. (د ح) (م ت). These figures are arranged according to their perfection. We must note that the most perfect is the first figure, because it is more suitable for the natural sequence, as thought moves from the smallest to the middle to the largest, and because it is self-evidently productive. Moreover, it produces the four propositions without other figures: it produces (the universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative). The singular proposition has the force of the universal, and the indefinite has the force of the particular. For each of these four figures there are moods resulting from agreement of the two premises in quantity and quality, or difference of the two premises in both or one of them. The explanation follows: Minor premise / Major premise: Universal affirmative / Universal affirmative, Negative / Negative, Particular affirmative / Particular affirmative, Negative / Negative. Thus the moods we obtain by rational division for each of the four figures are 16. Some of these moods are productive in all materials placed in them, and some are barren due to the possibility of corruption of their production in some materials. So let us examine each of these figures and their productive moods.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the four figures of the categorical syllogism and an introduction to their moods. It begins by defining the fourth figure: the middle term (حد أوسط) is the subject (موضوع) in the minor premise (صغرى) and the predicate (محمول) in the major premise (كبرى), and it is antecedent in the minor and consequent in the major. The author then explains that these figures are ranked by perfection. The first figure is the most perfect because it aligns with natural reasoning — the mind moves from the smallest term to the middle to the largest — and because it is obviously productive on its own. It can produce all four categorical propositions: universal affirmative (كلية موجبة), universal negative (كلية سالبة), particular affirmative (جزئية موجبة), and particular negative (جزئية سالبة). Singular propositions have the force of universals, and indefinite propositions have the force of particulars. For each figure, moods (ضروب) arise from the agreement or difference of the premises in quantity (كلية/جزئية) and quality (موجبة/سالبة). The author lists the possible combinations for the two premises: both universal affirmative, both universal negative, both particular affirmative, both particular negative — yielding 16 moods via rational division (قسمة عقلية). Some of these moods are productive (منتج) in all materials, while others are barren (عقيم) in some materials due to the risk of corruption. The text concludes by stating the intention to examine each figure and its productive moods.

الشكل الرابع
الضرب
منتج

Introduction to Syllogistic Moods: 16 Possibilities, Some Productive
Defines the fourth figure, ranks figures by completeness, and introduces the 16 possible moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the 16 possible moods derived from quantity and quality combinations and explain the difference between productive and barren moods.

Core Idea
Out of 16 possible mood combinations, only some yield necessary conclusions.

The passage introduces the concept of moods (ضروب) arising from the agreement or difference of the two premises in quantity and quality. It lists the four basic premise combinations and states that rational division yields 16 moods, some productive and some barren.

Guided Path
1
List the four basic premise combinations
The author gives: both universal affirmative, both universal negative, both particular affirmative, both particular negative. These are the four patterns for the two premises.
Quantity: universal or particular. Quality: affirmative or negative.
2
Multiply by the number of figures
Since there are four figures, and each figure can have these four premise combinations, we get 4 × 4 = 16 total moods across all figures.
The text says 'for each figure' but it means across all figures the division produces 16 moods.
3
Distinguish productive from barren moods
Productive moods yield a necessary conclusion in all materials. Barren moods may produce a false conclusion in some materials even if premises are true. The text says it will examine each figure's productive moods.

The First Figure: Definition, Productive Moods, and Conditions
Defines the first figure, provides four examples of productive moods, and states the requirement of two conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first figure of a categorical syllogism and determine whether a given mood is productive based on the position of the middle term and the two conditions.

Core Idea
The first figure is the perfect syllogism: the middle term logically connects premises directly.

In this passage, the author defines the first figure and provides four concrete examples from Islamic contexts. These examples illustrate the varieties of productive moods—universal, particular, affirmative, and negative. Understanding the first figure is foundational for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Locate the middle term in each example
Identify which term appears twice: once in each premise. In example (a), 'sincere believer' is the middle term; in (b), 'animal'; in (c), 'apple'; in (d), 'fruit'.
2
Check the position rule for the first figure
Verify that the middle term is predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major premise. For example (b): minor 'Every human is animal' (predicate), major 'Every animal is endowed with voluntary movement' (subject).
This is the defining characteristic of the first figure.
3
Classify each premise by quantity and quality
Determine if each premise is universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), or particular negative (O). Example (a): minor is A (singular treated as universal), major is A. Example (c): minor is I, major is E.
4
Identify the mood and confirm productivity
The mood is the combination of premise types. Example (b) is AAA (Barbara) – always valid. Example (c) is EIO (Ferio) – also valid. All four examples are productive moods of the first figure.
The text states that conditions must be met for consistent production, but these moods already satisfy them.
5
Reflect on the two conditions mentioned
The author says two conditions (ضابطان) are needed. Although not given here, common conditions are: the minor premise must be affirmative, and the major premise must be universal. Check if the examples fulfill these.
This hints at the next lesson.

The first figure and its productive moods. We have learned that the first figure of the categorical syllogism is: that in which the middle term is predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major. [Figure 1 (ح ض)] Examples: Subject Predicate Subject Predicate a - Ali - a sincere believer. And every sincere believer enters Paradise. (Minor term, middle term, middle term, major term) Therefore Ali enters Paradise (conclusion). b - Every human is an animal. And every animal is endowed with voluntary movement. Therefore every human is endowed with voluntary movement. c - Some fruit is apple. And no apple is grape. Therefore some fruit is not grape. d - Every apple is fruit. And no fruit is tar. Therefore no apple is tar. This figure does not produce a correct and consistent production except by fulfilling two conditions in it.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage focuses on the first figure of the categorical syllogism and its productive moods. It begins by restating the definition: the middle term is predicate (محمول) in the minor premise and subject (موضوع) in the major premise. Then it provides four examples (أمثلة) that illustrate different combinations of quantity and quality that are productive in this figure. The first example (a) is a syllogism about Ali being a sincere believer and entering Paradise: 'Ali is a sincere believer' (minor), 'Every sincere believer enters Paradise' (major), therefore 'Ali enters Paradise'. This is a valid mood with universal affirmative major and singular minor (which has the force of universal). The second example (b) is a standard Barbara mood: 'Every human is an animal', 'Every animal is endowed with voluntary movement', therefore 'Every human is endowed with voluntary movement'. The third example (c) is a syllogism with a particular affirmative minor and universal negative major: 'Some fruit is apple', 'No apple is grape', therefore 'Some fruit is not grape' — a Ferio mood. The fourth example (d) uses a universal affirmative minor and universal negative major: 'Every apple is fruit', 'No fruit is tar', therefore 'No apple is tar' — a Celarent mood. The text concludes by stating that this figure does not produce correct and consistent conclusions unless two conditions (ضابطان) are met, which will presumably be explained next. The examples all come from Islamic contexts: faith, Paradise, creation, and fruits, using terms familiar from fiqh and everyday life.

First Figure of Categorical Syllogism: Definition, Conditions, and Productive Moods
Defines first figure, states two conditions, lists four productive moods among sixteen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a categorical syllogism is in the first figure and determine if it is productive by checking the two conditions.

Core Idea
Only four of sixteen first-figure moods yield valid conclusions; the rest are barren.

In this lesson, we explore the first figure of the categorical syllogism, which is the most straightforward figure for deriving certain conclusions. The author defines the first figure by the position of the middle term and then lays down two necessary conditions for its validity. We will walk through examples and understand how to identify the productive moods among the sixteen possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term's position
In the first figure, the middle term is the predicate of the minor premise and the subject of the major premise. Look at example A: 'A believer of sincere faith' is predicate in 'Ali is a believer' and subject in 'Every believer enters paradise.'
2
Recognize the conditions for validity
The author states two conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative (quality condition) and the major premise must be universal (quantity condition). These are necessary for any first-figure syllogism to produce a necessary conclusion.
3
List the four productive moods
From the table, the productive moods are: (1) universal affirmative minor + universal affirmative major → universal affirmative conclusion; (2) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major → universal negative conclusion; (3) particular affirmative minor + universal affirmative major → particular affirmative conclusion; (4) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major → particular negative conclusion.
These correspond to Barbara, Celarent, Darii, and Ferio in traditional Western logic.
4
Verify why other moods are barren
The quadratic table shows that any mood violating the conditions (e.g., negative minor or particular major) yields no necessary conclusion. For instance, if the minor is negative, the middle term fails to connect the terms properly.
5
Apply to Islamic examples
Test the provided examples: Example A (Ali) has an affirmative minor and universal major, so it is productive. Example C (some fruit) also satisfies the conditions. Check that each example's premises meet the two conditions.

The first figure and its productive moods

Commentary
This passage defines the first figure of the conjunctive (categorical) syllogism, states the necessary conditions for its validity, and enumerates the four productive moods among the sixteen possible combinations. The author opens by recalling that the first figure is defined by the position of the middle term: it is predicated in the minor premise and subject in the major premise. Four examples are given: (A) Ali is a believer of sincere faith; every believer of sincere faith enters paradise; therefore Ali enters paradise. (B) Every human is an animal; every animal is self-moving; therefore every human is self-moving. (C) Some fruit is apple; no apple is grape; therefore some fruit is not grape. (D) Every apple is fruit; no fruit is tar; therefore no apple is tar. The author then states that this figure produces validly only under two conditions: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative, (2) the major premise must be universal. These conditions pertain to quality and quantity respectively. Out of sixteen possible mood combinations, only four satisfy both conditions. The four productive moods are: 1) universal affirmative minor + universal affirmative major ➝ universal affirmative conclusion; 2) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major ➝ universal negative conclusion; 3) particular affirmative minor + universal affirmative major ➝ particular affirmative conclusion; 4) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major ➝ particular negative conclusion. The passage concludes with a quadratic table (like a truth table) showing which combinations produce and which are barren. This systematic classification is essential for evaluating the validity of categorical syllogisms in the first figure.

First Mood of the First Figure: Affirmative Syllogism
Author exemplifies first mood first figure with prophetic sayings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first mood of the first figure (both premises universal affirmative) and construct a valid example from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Two universal affirmative premises yield a universal affirmative conclusion.

In this lesson, we examine the first mood of the first figure, where both premises are universal affirmative (K M). The author shows that this form is not limited to rigid formulas; even natural speech from the Prophet ﷺ can be analysed as a valid syllogism. You will learn to recognize this mood and apply it to Islamic sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism's components
In each example, find the minor premise (صغرى) containing the subject of the conclusion, the major premise (كبرى) containing the predicate, and the conclusion (نتاج). Look for the marker 'فكل' or '∴' indicating the conclusion.
The author uses the notation (K M) for universal affirmative.
2
Check that both premises are universal affirmative
Verify that each premise begins with 'كل' (every/all) and affirms a predicate of its subject. In Arabic, universal affirmative (K M) is indicated by 'كل ... هو ...' or similar constructions like 'كلكم' (all of you) treated as universal.
The second prophetic example uses a particular ('كلكم') but has the force of universal.
3
Confirm the conclusion is universal affirmative
The conclusion should also begin with 'كل' (or equivalent) and affirm the predicate. Check that the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises.
The author shows how to rephrase a natural statement into strict K M form.
4
Translate natural language into syllogistic form
Practice taking a Prophetic saying and breaking it into minor, major, and conclusion. For instance, 'كل بني آدم خطاء وخير الخطائين التوابون' becomes: minor (K M): 'كل بني آدم خطاء', major (K M): 'كل خطاء خيرهم التوابون', conclusion: 'كل بني آدم خيرهم التوابون'.
The author emphasises that rigid formulas are not required; any speech conveying the logical relation suffices.

Applications of the first figure (H D):
Minor, major produce.
A. According to its first mood: (K M) + (K M) ∴ (K M)
Examples from categorical conjunctive syllogism:
K M K M produces K M
• Every bat is a bird and every bird has wings ∴ Every bat has wings
• Every fish is an animal and every animal moves voluntarily ∴ Every fish moves voluntarily
One does not require maintaining rigid forms like these in syllogisms; rather every speech that conveys what the productive form conveys suffices for formulating a valid syllogism.
Examples of this mood in the speech of the Messenger ﷺ are his saying:
K M K M
• Every son of Adam errs, and the best of those who err are the repentant
This syllogism produces ∴ The best of the sons of Adam are the repentant
It is apparent that the minor of this syllogism is (K M). As for the major, we can resolve it as follows: (And all those who err, the best of them are the repentant). Then it appears to us that it is (K M). Then we conclude from the syllogism (K M) by saying: (Every son of Adam, the best of them are the repentant), and this equals what we concluded: so the best of the sons of Adam are the repentant.
And also from examples of this mood in the speech of the Messenger ﷺ is his saying:
K M particular, but with the force of (K M)
• All of you are from Adam, and Adam is from dust

• All of you are shepherds, and every shepherd is responsible for his flock ∴ All of you are responsible for your flock

Commentary
This passage is an application lesson showing how the first mood of the first figure (where both premises are universal affirmative, i.e., K M = kulilyya mujiba) works in practice. The author first states the general rule: minor premise (صغرى) + major premise (كبرى) produce a conclusion. Then he gives two clear categorical examples: 'Every bat is a bird' and 'Every bird has wings' yields 'Every bat has wings'; similarly for fish. He then notes that the syllogistic form need not be expressed in rigid formulas; any discourse that conveys the same logical relation suffices. To illustrate, he provides examples from the Prophet's sayings. The first prophetic example: 'Every son of Adam errs' and 'The best of those who err are the repentant' is parsed: the minor is 'Every son of Adam errs' (K M), and the major is resolved as 'All those who err, the best are the repentant' (also K M), so the conclusion is 'Every son of Adam, the best are the repentant'. The second prophetic example: 'All of you are from Adam, and Adam is from dust' yields 'All of you are from dust'. The third: 'All of you are shepherds, and every shepherd is responsible' yields 'All of you are responsible'. These show how universal affirmative premises in natural language yield universal affirmative conclusions, fitting the first mood of the first figure.

The Syllogism
intermediate

First-Figure Conditional Syllogisms: Pure and Mixed
Author provides three examples of first-figure conditional syllogisms, two pure and one mixed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate first-figure conditional syllogisms, including those mixing conditional and categorical premises, by identifying the middle term and applying transitivity.

Core Idea
The first figure works for conditionals by chaining middle conditions, even when combined with categorical premises.

This passage presents three examples demonstrating that the first figure of categorical syllogisms seamlessly applies to conditional arguments. In the first two examples, both premises are universal affirmative conditionals (كلما... كان), showing how the middle condition links antecedent to consequent in a transitive chain. The third example mixes a conditional premise with a categorical premise, yet the first-figure structure still holds. By analyzing these examples, you will learn to recognize the pattern and extend your syllogistic toolkit to conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises in each example
List the two premises for each example. Example 1: P1: Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists. P2: Whenever daytime exists, seeking livelihood is more beneficial. Example 2: P1: Whenever they kindle a fire for war, God extinguishes it. P2: Whenever God extinguishes a fire of war, its evil stops. Example 3: P1: Whatever good you send ahead, you will find it with Allah (conditional). P2: Every good with Allah is a beautiful reward (categorical).
The third premise is explicitly marked 'hamliyya' (categorical) in the text.
2
Locate the middle term/condition
In examples 1 and 2, the middle term is the condition that appears in both premises: 'daytime exists' and 'God extinguishes the fire' respectively. In example 3, the middle term is 'what you find with Allah' – it is the consequent of the conditional premise and the subject of the categorical premise.
The middle term must connect the two premises; here it does so by appearing in each.
3
Check first-figure ordering
In the first figure, the middle term must be subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor. For conditionals, this translates to: the middle condition is the consequent of the first premise and the antecedent of the second (or subject in a categorical). Verify this ordering for each example.
Example 1: 'daytime exists' is consequent of P1 and antecedent of P2. Example 3: 'what you find with Allah' is consequent of the conditional and subject of the categorical.
4
Derive the conclusion
Using transitivity (for pure conditionals) or substitution (for mixed), draw the conclusion. Example 1: Sun rising ⇒ seeking livelihood beneficial. Example 2: Fire kindling ⇒ evil stops. Example 3: Sending good ⇒ finding beautiful reward.
In all cases, the conclusion follows necessarily.
5
Confirm universal affirmative mood
All premises and conclusions are universal affirmative (كلما for conditionals, 'every' for categorical). This corresponds to the AAA mood (Barbara) in categorical first-figure, which is valid.
The author marks each premise with 'ك م' (universal affirmative), reinforcing the mood.

Commentary
This passage continues the application of the first figure but now for conjunctive conditional syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani sharti). The author gives three examples. The first: 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists' and 'Whenever daytime exists, seeking livelihood is more beneficial' yields 'Whenever the sun is rising, seeking livelihood is more beneficial'. This shows the transitivity of the conditional, with the middle term being 'daytime exists'. The second example uses a similar chain: 'Whenever they kindle a fire for war, God extinguishes it' and 'Whenever God extinguishes a fire of war, its evil stops' leads to 'Whenever they kindle a fire for war, its evil stops'. The third example mixes a conditional premise and a categorical premise: 'Whatever good you send ahead, you will find it with Allah' (conditional) and 'Every good with Allah is a beautiful reward' (categorical) yields a conditional conclusion: 'Whatever good you send ahead, you will find its reward a beautiful reward'. This mixed type still conforms to the first figure as the middle term (what you find with Allah) is connected. All examples use universal affirmative (K M) propositions, showing the first mood extends to conditionals.

The Syllogism
beginner
