Mixing Categorical and Conditional Premises
Combining a conditional premise with a categorical premise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a mixed syllogism combining categorical and conditional premises and verify that the conclusion follows logically.

Core Idea
A syllogism can mix categorical and conditional premises as long as the middle term links them.

The author presents Quranic examples where a conditional premise is combined with a categorical premise to produce a conditional conclusion. This shows that logical validity does not require both premises to be of the same type. In this lesson, we will dissect one such example to understand how the syllogism works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Find the two premises in the example: 'Whatever good you send forth for yourselves, you will find it with Allah' (conditional) and 'Every good that is with Allah is a good gift' (categorical).
The first premise is a universal conditional (sharṭiyya muttaṣila). The second is a universal affirmative (kulli mawjib).
2
Extract the logical structure
Rewrite the premises in standard logical form: (P1) If you send forth good, then you find it with Allah. (P2) Whatever is with Allah is a good gift. The middle term is 'found with Allah' or 'being with Allah'.
The conclusion will link the antecedent of P1 with the consequent of P2 via the middle term.
3
Formulate the conclusion
Combine the premises: Since sending forth good implies finding it with Allah, and finding it with Allah implies it is a good gift, therefore sending forth good implies a good gift. This is a valid transportation of the conditional.
The conclusion is itself a conditional: 'Whatever good you send forth, you will find its reward as a good gift.'
4
Check validity
The syllogism is valid because the condition 'if A then B' and 'if B then C' yields 'if A then C'. No logical error occurs.
This matches the form of a hypothetical syllogism (also called chain argument).

Examples of the conditional conjunctive syllogism: (ك م) • Whenever the sun is rising, the day exists. ك م And whenever the day exists, seeking livelihood is more beneficial. ك م ∴ Whenever the sun is rising, seeking livelihood is more beneficial. • Whenever they kindle a fire for war, Allah extinguishes it; and whenever Allah extinguishes a fire of war, its evil stops. ∴ Whenever they kindle a fire for war, its evil stops. ك م (conditional conjunctive) • Whatever good you send forth for yourselves, you will find it with Allah. ك م (categorical) And every good that is with Allah is a good gift. ك م (conditional conjunctive) ∴ Whatever good you send forth for yourselves, you will find its reward as a good gift. ك م (conditional) • Whatever you spend, Allah has promised to replace it. ك م (categorical) And whatever Allah has promised to replace, it is replaced. ك م (conditional) ∴ Whatever you spend, it is replaced. ك م (categorical) • For every deed coupled with intention is a recompense. ك م (disjunctive conditional) And always either the recompense is a recompense of reward or of punishment. ك م (disjunctive conditional) ∴ Always for the deed coupled with intention there is a recompense of reward or of punishment. • The saying of the Messenger ﷺ: 'All people go out in the morning selling themselves, either freeing themselves or ruining themselves.' Commentary
This passage provides multiple examples of syllogisms (qiyas) to illustrate how categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti) premises can be combined to produce a necessary conclusion. The author first gives a brief example: 'This syllogism produces ∴ so all of you are from dust.' Then a more complete example: 'All of you are shepherds and every shepherd is responsible for his flock ∴ so all of you are responsible for his flock,' which is a categorical syllogism. The author then explicitly turns to 'Examples of the conditional conjunctive syllogism' (أمثلة من القياس الاقتراني الشرطي). Each example is marked with (ك م), likely indicating the type of premises. The first conditional example uses a conjunctive conditional (متصل): 'Whenever the sun is rising, the day exists' and 'Whenever the day exists, seeking livelihood is more beneficial,' leading to the conclusion that whenever the sun is rising, seeking livelihood is more beneficial. This is a valid transportation of the conditional. The next example is similar with a fire of war. Then a mixed example combines a conditional premise derived from the Quran ('Whatever good you send forth for yourselves, you will find it with Allah') with a categorical premise ('Every good that is with Allah is a good gift'), concluding with a conditional. This shows that a syllogism can have premises of different types. The following example similarly mixes conditional and categorical premises about spending and replacement. A later example uses a disjunctive conditional (منفصلة) about deeds and intention, demonstrating that the conclusion can also be disjunctive. Finally, the Hadith of the Prophet ﷺ is resolved into two possible syllogistic forms: one with major as disjunctive and one with major as categorical, both yielding a disjunctive conclusion. All examples are drawn from Islamic scripture or common knowledge, reinforcing the rule that logical examples should be rooted in the Islamic tradition.

Disjunctive Syllogisms from Islamic Sources
Resolving a prophetic hadith into a disjunctive syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a disjunctive syllogism (شرطية منفصلة) in an Islamic text and reformulate it into standard logical form.

Core Idea
A disjunctive syllogism presents mutually exclusive alternatives; eliminating one proves the other.

The author gives two examples of disjunctive syllogisms: one about deeds and intention, and another from a hadith of the Prophet ﷺ about selling oneself. These examples show how Islamic texts contain logical disjunctions that can be analyzed as valid syllogisms. In this lesson, we will dissect the hadith example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hadith and its parts
The hadith says: 'All people go out in the morning selling themselves, either freeing themselves or ruining themselves.' The author resolves it into a syllogism with a categorical premise and a disjunctive premise.
The hadith contains an implicit logical structure.
2
Extract the categorical premise
The first premise is categorical: 'All people go out in the morning selling themselves.' This is a universal affirmative (ك م).
The subject is 'All people', predicate 'selling themselves'.
3
Extract the disjunctive premise
The second premise is disjunctive: 'And always either a seller of himself frees himself if he sells himself to the Merciful, or ruins himself if he sells himself to Satan.' The author also gives a simpler version: 'Every seller of himself either frees himself or ruins himself.'
This is a disjunctive conditional (منفصلة) with two exclusive options.
4
Formulate the conclusion
From the two premises, the conclusion follows: 'All people go out in the morning either freeing themselves or ruining themselves.' This is a disjunctive conclusion.
The syllogism is valid by simple composition: if all A are B, and all B are either C or D, then all A are either C or D.

∴ فما تنفقوا من شيء فهو مخلوف

This syllogism produces ∴ so all of you are from dust. (ك م) (ك م) • All of you are shepherds and every shepherd is responsible for his flock ∴ so all of you are responsible for his flock. Examples of the conditional conjunctive syllogism: (ك م) • Whenever the sun is rising, the day exists. ك م And whenever the day exists, seeking livelihood is more beneficial. ك م ∴ Whenever the sun is rising, seeking livelihood is more beneficial. • Whenever they kindle a fire for war, Allah extinguishes it; and whenever Allah extinguishes a fire of war, its evil stops. ∴ Whenever they kindle a fire for war, its evil stops. ك م (conditional conjunctive) • Whatever good you send forth for yourselves, you will find it with Allah. ك م (categorical) And every good that is with Allah is a good gift. ك م (conditional conjunctive) ∴ Whatever good you send forth for yourselves, you will find its reward as a good gift. ك م (conditional) • Whatever you spend, Allah has promised to replace it. ك م (categorical) And whatever Allah has promised to replace, it is replaced. ك م (conditional) ∴ Whatever you spend, it is replaced. ك م (categorical) • For every deed coupled with intention is a recompense. ك م (disjunctive conditional) And always either the recompense is a recompense of reward or of punishment. ك م (disjunctive conditional) ∴ Always for the deed coupled with intention there is a recompense of reward or of punishment. • The saying of the Messenger ﷺ: 'All people go out in the morning selling themselves, either freeing themselves or ruining themselves.' It is resolved into a syllogism in the following manner: ك م (categorical) All people go out in the morning selling themselves. ك م (disjunctive conditional) And always either a seller of himself frees himself if he sells himself to the Merciful, or ruins himself if he sells himself to Satan. ∴ Always either a seller of himself from the people frees himself or ruins himself. Or the major premise is resolved into an affirmative major in the following manner: And every seller of himself either frees himself or ruins himself. So the result becomes: ∴ All people go out in the morning either freeing themselves or ruining themselves.

Commentary
This passage provides multiple examples of syllogisms (qiyas) to illustrate how categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti) premises can be combined to produce a necessary conclusion. The author first gives a brief example: 'This syllogism produces ∴ so all of you are from dust.' Then a more complete example: 'All of you are shepherds and every shepherd is responsible for his flock ∴ so all of you are responsible for his flock,' which is a categorical syllogism. The author then explicitly turns to 'Examples of the conditional conjunctive syllogism' (أمثلة من القياس الاقتراني الشرطي). Each example is marked with (ك م), likely indicating the type of premises. The first conditional example uses a conjunctive conditional (متصل): 'Whenever the sun is rising, the day exists' and 'Whenever the day exists, seeking livelihood is more beneficial,' leading to the conclusion that whenever the sun is rising, seeking livelihood is more beneficial. This is a valid transportation of the conditional. The next example is similar with a fire of war. Then a mixed example combines a conditional premise derived from the Quran ('Whatever good you send forth for yourselves, you will find it with Allah') with a categorical premise ('Every good that is with Allah is a good gift'), concluding with a conditional. This shows that a syllogism can have premises of different types. The following example similarly mixes conditional and categorical premises about spending and replacement. A later example uses a disjunctive conditional (منفصلة) about deeds and intention, demonstrating that the conclusion can also be disjunctive. Finally, the Hadith of the Prophet ﷺ is resolved into two possible syllogistic forms: one with major as disjunctive and one with major as categorical, both yielding a disjunctive conclusion. All examples are drawn from Islamic scripture or common knowledge, reinforcing the rule that logical examples should be rooted in the Islamic tradition.

The Syllogism

4 min
Transportation of Conditionals in Islamic Examples
A pure conditional chain about sun and livelihood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid chain of conditional propositions (hypothetical syllogism) from Islamic examples and verify its logical form.

Core Idea
If A implies B and B implies C, then A implies C -- this is the pure transportation of a conditional.

The author gives two examples of pure conditional syllogisms: one about the sun and livelihood, another about kindling a fire of war. Both follow the pattern 'if A then B, if B then C, therefore if A then C'. In this lesson, we will analyze the first example to master the concept of transitive conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditionals
First conditional: 'Whenever the sun is rising, the day exists.' Second conditional: 'Whenever the day exists, seeking livelihood is more beneficial.' Both are universal conjunctive conditionals.
The form is: If A then B; If B then C.
2
Trace the chain
The antecedent of the first conditional is 'sun rising', the consequent is 'day exists'. In the second, the antecedent is 'day exists' (same as first consequent), and the consequent is 'seeking livelihood beneficial'.
The middle term (B) is 'day exists'.
3
Derive the conclusion
By transitivity: If the sun is rising, then day exists; if day exists, then seeking livelihood beneficial; therefore, if the sun is rising, then seeking livelihood beneficial.
The conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent as the first premise and the consequent of the second.
4
Check validity
This is a valid hypothetical syllogism. It requires no additional premises and is acceptable in Islamic logic as a necessary inference.
The author marks both premises with (ك م) confirming their universal affirmative nature.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Analyzing Mixed Syllogisms in First Figure
Author presents syllogism examples using categorical and conditional propositions from religious texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the major and minor premises and their proposition types (categorical, conditional, disjunctive) in a first-figure syllogism from a religious text, and verify the validity of the inference.

Core Idea
First-figure syllogisms can validly mix categorical and conditional premises when the middle term links them.

Syllogisms in Islamic argumentation often combine different proposition types. The author uses examples from the Qur'an and Hadith to demonstrate how to recognize the premises and check the form. You will learn to dissect these arguments step by step, ensuring the middle term connects properly and the conclusion follows necessarily.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Minor Premise
Look for the premise that contains the subject term of the conclusion. In the first example, 'Whatever you spend, Allah has promised to replace it' is about 'what you spend', which becomes the subject of the conclusion.
The minor premise is often marked with 'ك م (شرطية)' or 'ك م (حملية)' in the text.
2
Identify the Major Premise
Find the premise that contains the predicate term of the conclusion. In the first example, 'Whatever Allah has promised to replace, it is replaced' gives the predicate 'replaced'.
The major premise is also labeled 'ك م (حملية)' or 'ك م (شرطية)'.
3
Determine the Proposition Types
Classify each premise as categorical (حملیة), conditional (شرطية), or disjunctive (منفصلة). This affects how the middle term relates the subject and predicate.
In the first example, the minor is conditional, the major is categorical.
4
Check the Middle Term Link
Ensure the middle term (the term common to both premises but not in the conclusion) properly links the subject and predicate. Here, 'Allah's promise of replacement' is the middle term, connecting 'what you spend' and 'replaced'.
If the middle term is not properly linked, the syllogism is invalid.
5
Verify the Conclusion Follows
Confirm that the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises in the first figure: the middle term is subject of the major and predicate of the minor (or vice versa depending on mood). The conclusion should combine the subject of the minor and predicate of the major.
In the first example, the conclusion 'So whatever you spend, it is replaced' correctly follows.

K M (conditional)
• And whatever you spend, Allah has promised to replace it.
K M (categorical)

K M (conditional)
∴ So whatever you spend, it is replaced.

• For every action coupled with intention, there is a recompense.
K M (disjunctive conditional)
And always the recompense is either a recompense with reward or with punishment.

• The saying of the Messenger ﷺ :
'All people go forth, selling themselves, so either freeing themselves or destroying themselves.' It is analyzed into a syllogism as follows:
K M (categorical)
All people go forth selling themselves.
K M (disjunctive conditional)
And always one who sells himself either frees himself if he sells himself to the Merciful, or destroys himself if he sells himself to Satan.
∴ So always one who sells himself from among people either frees himself or destroys himself.

And every seller of himself either frees himself or destroys himself.
So the conclusion: ∴ All people go forth, so either freeing themselves or destroying themselves.
• Whoever is given wisdom has been given much good. And whoever is given much good is happy.
So whoever is given wisdom is happy.
And in this manner one should free oneself from rigidity of examples in order to benefit from the rules of syllogism in the sciences.

Commentary
This passage presents a series of syllogistic examples drawn from the Qur'an and Hadith to illustrate how propositions can be combined into valid arguments. The author uses shorthand notation: 'ك م (شرطية)' likely stands for 'major premise (conditional)' and 'ك م (حملية)' for 'major premise (categorical)'. The first example is a categorical syllogism in the first figure: the minor premise is 'Whatever you spend, Allah has promised to replace it' (conditional), the major is 'Whatever Allah has promised to replace, it is replaced' (categorical), yielding the conclusion 'So whatever you spend, it is replaced' (conditional). The second example involves a disjunctive conditional: 'For every action coupled with intention, there is a recompense' (categorical), and 'Always the recompense is either with reward or with punishment' (disjunctive), leading to a disjunctive conclusion. The author then analyzes the hadith 'All people go forth...' into two possible syllogistic forms: one with a disjunctive major and one converted into an affirmative categorical major, both yielding a disjunctive conclusion about freeing or destroying oneself. Finally, the example 'Whoever is given wisdom...' shows a simple categorical syllogism (from the first figure mood Barbara). The passage concludes with a pedagogical note urging flexibility in applying syllogistic rules rather than being bound by rigid examples.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Second Mood of the First Figure (Celarent) in Categorical Syllogisms
Second mood of first figure with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of the second mood of the first figure and verify its conclusion in a categorical syllogism from Islamic theological texts.

Core Idea
Universal affirmative major + universal negative minor yield universal negative conclusion.

This passage introduces the second mood of the first figure, known as Celarent in Western logic. The author provides three examples drawn from Islamic beliefs about piety, salvation, and divine eternity. Understanding this mood is essential for constructing valid syllogistic arguments in Islamic theology (kalam) and jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh).

Guided Path
1
Extract the terms S, M, P
In the first example, identify the subject (S: believer), middle term (M: people of piety), and predicate (P: eternally punished). The major premise is 'Every believer takes a portion of piety' (all S are M). The minor premise is 'No one from the people of piety is eternally punished' (no M are P).
2
Check quantity and quality of premises
The major premise is universal affirmative (ك م); the minor premise is universal negative (ك س). This matches the pattern given: ك م + ك س → ك س.
In the notation, ك م stands for universal affirmative, ك س for universal negative.
3
Verify middle term position
In the first figure, the middle term is the subject of the major premise and the predicate of the minor premise. Here, 'people of piety' is the predicate of the major and the subject of the minor, confirming first figure.
4
Determine mood and figure
The mood is the second mood of the first figure: major universal affirmative (A), minor universal negative (E), conclusion universal negative (E) – i.e., the AEE pattern in the first figure.
In classical terminology, this mood is Celarent.
5
Validate the conclusion
If all S are M and no M are P, then no S are P. The conclusion follows necessarily. This is a valid syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - تطبيقات على الضرب الثاني من الشكل الأول (ح - ض)
صغرى كبرى ينتج
وهو (ك م) + (ك س) ∴ (ك س)
أمثلة من القياس الاقتراني الحملي :
(ك م) حملية
• كل مؤمن آخذ بنصيب من التقوى
(ك س) حملية
ولا أحد من أهل التقوى مخلَّد في العذاب ومحروم من الجنة
(ك س) حملية
∴ فلا أحد من المؤمنين مخلد في العذاب ومحروم من الجنة
(ك م) حملية (ك س) حملية
• كل نفس بما كسبت رهينة ولا رهين بكسبه إلا مخير
(ك س) حملية
∴ فلا نفس كاسبة إلا مخيرة
(ك م) حملية (ك س) حملية
• كلُّ مَن عليها فانٍ ويبقى وجه ربك ولا فاني هو أزلي قديم
(ك س) حملية
∴ فما عليها من أزلي قديم
B - Applications of the Second Mood of the First Figure (Categorical)
Minor Major Produces
It is (K M) + (K S) ∴ (K S)
Examples from the categorical conjunctive syllogism:
(K M) categorical
• Every believer takes a portion of piety.
(K S) categorical
And no one from the people of piety is eternally punished and deprived of Paradise.
(K S) categorical
∴ So no one from the believers is eternally punished and deprived of Paradise.
(K M) categorical (K S) categorical
• Every soul is pledged for what it earns, and no one pledged for what he earns except he is given choice.
(K S) categorical
∴ So no earning soul is except given choice.
(K M) categorical (K S) categorical
• Everyone upon it (the earth) is perishing, and your Lord's face remains, and nothing perishable is eternal and ancient.
(K S) categorical
∴ So nothing upon it (the earth) is eternal and ancient.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage explains the second mood of the first figure in categorical syllogisms. The notation 'ضرب ثاني من الشكل الأول (ح - ض)' indicates it is the second mood of the first figure, where 'ح' stands for categorical (ḥamli) and 'ض' for the mood number? Actually 'ح - ض' may be an abbreviation. The pattern is given: 'صغرى كبرى ينتج وهو (ك م) + (ك س) ∴ (ك س)'. Here 'ك م' is the major premise (كلية موجبة = universal affirmative) and 'ك س' is the minor premise (كلية سالبة = universal negative); combined they produce a universal negative conclusion. In standard Aristotelian terms, this is the mood Cesare (or Camestres depending on figure; for first figure, Cesare is not valid; but here it is presented as second mood of first figure. Actually, in classical logic, the first figure moods are Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio. The second mood of the first figure is Celarent: universal affirmative major, universal negative minor, universal negative conclusion. That matches the description: 'ك م' (affirmative) + 'ك س' (negative) yields 'ك س' (negative). The examples confirm: 
1. Every believer takes piety (affirmative major). No one pious is eternally punished and deprived (negative minor). Therefore, no believer is eternally punished and deprived (negative conclusion). 
2. Every soul is pledged for its deeds (affirmative; implied universal), but the text says 'كل نفس بما كسبت رهينة' which is affirmative, and 'ولا رهين بكسبه إلا مخير' which is negative ('no pledged soul except...'). The conclusion 'فلا نفس كاسبة إلا مخيرة' is negative. 
3. 'كل من عليها فانٍ' affirmative, 'ولا فاني هو أزلي قديم' negative, conclusion 'فما عليها من أزلي قديم' negative. 
Thus, the author correctly illustrates the Celarent mood of the first figure.

الشكل الأول
ك م
ك س
قياس اقتراني حملي

Key Takeaway
The second mood of the first figure (Celarent) requires a universal affirmative major and a universal negative minor to validly conclude a universal negative.

The Wisdom Syllogism: Form Over Content
Author illustrates a categorical syllogism and emphasizes flexible application of logical rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the middle term in a categorical syllogism and explain why the author cautions against rigidity with examples.

Core Idea
The form of the syllogism, not its content, guarantees validity.

This passage presents a simple categorical syllogism about wisdom and happiness. The author then draws a crucial lesson for students of logic: do not become attached to specific examples; instead, internalize the logical rules that make the argument work. This lesson walks you through the syllogism and the methodological takeaway.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
List the three distinct terms in the syllogism: wisdom (الحكمة), much good (الخير الكثير), and happiness (السعادة). The conclusion combines wisdom and happiness, so wisdom is the subject and happiness is the predicate of the conclusion.
The term 'much good' appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.
2
Find the middle term
The middle term is the one that occurs in both premises but not in the conclusion. Here it is 'much good' (الخير الكثير). It connects wisdom to happiness.
3
Determine figure and mood
This is a first-figure Barbara syllogism: all A is B, all B is C, therefore all A is C. The mood is AAA. This is always valid.
Barbara is the most basic valid mood in categorical logic.
4
Extract the methodological lesson
The author says: 'On this pattern, one should liberate oneself from rigidity of examples to benefit from the rules of syllogism in the sciences.' The lesson is that the logical form applies universally, regardless of the concrete terms. Do not memorize examples; master the rules.
This prepares students to apply syllogistic reasoning in fiqh, kalam, tafsir, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من يؤت الحكمة فقد أوتي خيراً كثيراً. ومن يؤت الخير الكثير سعد. فمن يؤت الحكمة سعد. وعلى هذا النمط ينبغي التحرر من جمود الأمثلة للانتفاع من قواعد القياس في العلوم.
Whoever is given wisdom has been given much good. And whoever is given much good is happy. So whoever is given wisdom is happy. And in this manner, one should liberate oneself from rigidity of examples in order to benefit from the rules of syllogism in the sciences.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents a valid categorical syllogism in the first figure (Barbara) as a demonstration. The author then extracts a methodological principle: one should not become rigidly attached to specific examples but rather grasp the underlying logical rules (قواعد القياس) to apply them in various sciences. The syllogism itself moves from the universal premise 'Whoever is given wisdom has been given much good' through 'Whoever is given much good is happy' to the conclusion 'Whoever is given wisdom is happy.' The lesson is that the form of the syllogism, not its content, ensures validity. This reflects the Islamic intellectual tradition's emphasis on the utility of logic across fiqh, kalam, and tafsir.

Categorical Syllogisms in the Second Mood
Author provides multiple categorical examples of the second mood of the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second mood in categorical syllogisms and verify that the conclusion follows using Celarent.

Core Idea
A universal affirmative major and universal negative minor yield a universal negative conclusion.

The second mood of the first figure, known as Celarent, is frequently used in Islamic theological reasoning. The author provides several categorical examples from concepts like faith, piety, and the soul. Let's analyse them step by step to see how the mood works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise
The major is a universal affirmative proposition (ك م). In the first example, it is 'Every believer takes a portion of piety.'
2
Identify the minor premise
The minor is a universal negative proposition (ك س). In the first example, it is 'No one from the people of piety is eternally punished.'
3
Determine the mood
With A major and E minor, the mood is EAE (Celarent) in the first figure.
All categorical examples follow this pattern.
4
Verify the conclusion
The conclusion is universal negative (ك س): 'Therefore, no believer is eternally punished.' Check that it negates the minor's subject.

B - Applications on the second mood of the first figure (categorical-conditional). Minor premise, major premise, conclusion. It is (K M) + (K S) ∴ (K S). Examples from categorical conjunctive syllogism: (K M) categorical: Every believer takes a portion of piety. (K S) categorical: No one from the people of piety is eternally punished and deprived of paradise. (K S) categorical: Therefore, no believer is eternally punished and deprived of paradise. (K M) categorical, (K S) categorical: Every soul is pledged for what it earns, and no one pledged for his earning is except free to choose. (K S) categorical: Therefore, no earning soul is except free to choose. (K M) categorical, (K S) categorical: Everyone on it is perishing, and there remains the face of your Lord, and no perishable thing is eternal and pre-eternal. (K S) categorical: Therefore, nothing on it is eternal and pre-eternal. (K M) categorical: Every soul tastes death. (K S) categorical: No one dies unless he has life. (K S) categorical: Therefore, no soul is except that it has life. Examples from conditional conjunctive syllogism: (K M) conditional conjunctive: Whenever a messenger came to them with what their souls did not desire, some they denied and some they kill. (K S) categorical: No one among the deniers of messengers or their killers is safe from punishment. (K S) conditional: Therefore, it is absolutely not that whenever a messenger came to them with what their souls did not desire, they are safe from punishment. (K M) conditional disjunctive: Always, either the number is even or odd. (K S) categorical: No odd number is divisible into equal halves. (K S) conditional: Therefore, it is absolutely not that the number is either even or divisible into equal halves.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents applications of the second mood of the first figure in both categorical and conditional syllogisms. The author first explains the mood notation: major premise (ك م) combined with minor premise (ك س) yields conclusion (ك س). Then several categorical examples are given, all following the pattern of a universal affirmative major and a universal negative minor leading to a universal negative conclusion (Celarent). For instance, the first example: major 'Every believer takes a portion of piety,' minor 'No one from the people of piety is eternally punished and deprived of paradise,' conclusion 'No believer is eternally punished and deprived of paradise.' This is followed by conditional examples, including conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals. The conditional conjunctive example uses the form 'Whenever a messenger came... some they denied and some they kill' as major, and 'No one among deniers/killers is safe' as minor, concluding that it is not the case that whenever a messenger came they are safe. The disjunctive example uses 'Either the number is even or odd' as major and 'No odd number is divisible equally' as minor, concluding that it is not the case that the number is either even or divisible equally. These examples demonstrate the application of the second mood to different proposition types, showing the universality of syllogistic rules across content domains.

Conditional and Disjunctive Syllogisms in the Second Mood
Author provides conditional and disjunctive examples of the second mood of the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second mood when applied to conditional conjunctive and disjunctive premises and verify the conclusion.

Core Idea
The second mood can be applied to conditional propositions, producing a negated conditional conclusion.

The author extends the second mood to conditional logic, showing its versatility. One example uses a conjunctive conditional about messengers, another uses a disjunctive about numbers. Let's examine how the mood works with these different proposition types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of major premise
In the first conditional example, the major is a conjunctive conditional (if-then: 'Whenever a messenger came...'). In the disjunctive example, the major is a disjunction (either-or: 'Either the number is even or odd').
Both are universal in scope.
2
Identify the categorical minor premise
The minor is always a categorical (حملي) universal negative (ك س). In the conjunctive example: 'No one among the deniers of messengers or their killers is safe from punishment.'
3
Observe how the conclusion negates the original conditional
In the conjunctive example, the conclusion is the negation of the conditional: 'It is not that whenever a messenger came... they are safe.' In the disjunctive example, the conclusion negates the disjunction: 'It is not that the number is either even or divisible equally.'
The conclusion is a negated conditional or disjunction.
4
Check that the mood structure is preserved
The major is treated as universal affirmative (in the sense that the conditional holds for all cases), the minor is universal negative, and the conclusion is universal negative (negated conditional).
This mirrors the categorical second mood.

The Syllogism

Three Types of Syllogisms with Islamic Examples
Author provides examples of categorical, conditional, and disjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify categorical, conditional conjunctive, and disjunctive syllogisms and extract their premises and conclusions from Quranic and mathematical examples.

Core Idea
Syllogisms reveal necessary conclusions from given premises, whether categorical or conditional.

This passage from the classical logic textbook presents three concrete syllogisms: a categorical syllogism about the soul and death, a mixed conditional syllogism about messengers, and a disjunctive syllogism about numbers. By working through these examples, you will learn how to recognize each type and verify its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism type
For each example, determine whether it is categorical, conditional conjunctive (if-then), or conditional disjunctive (either-or). The first uses only categorical propositions; the second uses a conditional conjunctive premise; the third uses a disjunctive premise.
2
Extract the premises
Note the universal quantity and quality (affirmative/negative) of each premise as indicated by abbreviations like 'ك م' (universal affirmative) and 'ك س' (universal negative). For conditional premises, identify the antecedent and consequent.
3
Check the middle term (if categorical)
In the categorical example, identify the middle term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. Here 'tasting death' appears in the minor premise and 'dies' in the major; the middle term relates to death.
4
Validate the conditional structure
For the conditional examples, see how the categorical premise eliminates one possibility (e.g., 'none are safe') or negates a part to yield a negative conditional conclusion.
5
Verify the conclusion
Ensure the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises. In valid syllogisms, if premises are true, conclusion must be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ك م) حملية
• كل نفس ذائقة الموت
(ك س) حملية
ولا أحد يموت إلا إذا كان ذا حياة
(ك س) حملية
∴ فلا نفس إلا هي ذات حياة
أمثلة من القياس الاقتراني الشرطي:
(ك م) شرطية متصلة
• كلما جاءهم رسول بما لا تهوى أنفسهم، فريقاً كذبوا وفريقاً يقتلون.
(ك س) حملية
ولا أحد من مكذبي الرسل أو قاتليهم بناجين من العذاب.
(ك س) شرطية
∴ فليس البتة كلما جاءهم رسول بما لا تهوى أنفسهم بناجين من العذاب.

• دائماً إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو فرداً.

∴ فليس البتة إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو ينقسم بمتساويين.
(Universal affirmative) categorical: Every soul is tasting death. (Universal negative) categorical: And no one dies unless they have life. (Universal negative) categorical: Therefore no soul except it has life. Examples of conditional conjunctive syllogism: (Universal affirmative) conditional conjunctive: Whenever a messenger came to them with what their souls disliked, a group they denied and a group they killed. (Universal negative) categorical: And none of the deniers of the messengers or their killers are safe from punishment. (Universal negative) conditional: Therefore it is absolutely not the case that whenever a messenger came to them with what their souls disliked they are safe from punishment. (Universal affirmative) conditional disjunctive: Always the number is either even or odd. (Universal negative) categorical: And no odd number is divisible into equal parts. Therefore it is absolutely not the case that the number is either even or divisible into equal parts.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage provides concrete examples of three types of syllogisms: categorical, conditional conjunctive (muttaṣil), and conditional disjunctive (munfaṣil). The first example is a categorical syllogism: the minor premise 'Every soul is tasting death' is universal affirmative (ك م), the major premise 'No one dies unless they have life' is universal negative (ك س) but logically equivalent to 'All who die have life,' and the conclusion 'No soul except it has life' (i.e., every soul has life) is universal negative. This is a valid first-figure syllogism (Celarent? Actually Barbara with a negative major? It is a valid form). The second set illustrates a mixed conditional conjunctive syllogism: the conditional premise 'Whenever a messenger came...' (ك م شرطية متصلة) is combined with a categorical premise 'None of the deniers...are safe' (ك س حملية) to produce a negative conditional conclusion. The third set is a disjunctive syllogism: the disjunctive premise 'Always the number is either even or odd' (ك م شرطية منفصلة) is combined with a categorical premise 'No odd number is divisible into equal parts' (ك س حملية) to yield a negative disjunctive conclusion. All examples demonstrate how syllogistic reasoning moves from premises to necessary conclusions.

D) Particular negative

Key Takeaway
Syllogisms come in categorical and conditional forms; each requires specific validation of logical necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Third Mood of the First Figure: From Particular to General
Author defines the third mood of the first figure and gives Quranic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct valid categorical syllogisms of the third mood (particular-minor, universal-major) using Quranic examples.

Core Idea
When the minor is particular and the major universal, the conclusion is also particular.

The third mood of the first figure in categorical logic is a powerful tool for deriving limited but certain conclusions. In this lesson, you'll learn its structure and see how the Qur'an itself exemplifies logical reasoning through this mood. By analyzing real Quranic arguments, you'll master the pattern (ج م)+(ك م) ∴ (ج م).

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood pattern
The third mood requires a particular affirmative minor premise (ج م) and a universal affirmative major premise (ك م), yielding a particular affirmative conclusion (ج م). Memorize this pattern.
Remember: the conclusion matches the particularity of the minor premise.
2
Extract premises from the first example
In the verse 'And among people are those who say: We believe in Allah and the Last Day, but they are not believers', identify that this is a particular affirmative proposition (some people). The major premise is 'Whoever says that lying is a hypocrite' – a universal affirmative (every such person is a hypocrite).
3
Verify the conclusion
The conclusion 'Therefore among people are hypocrites' is particular affirmative. Check that it follows: the middle term 'those who say lying' connects the two premises, and the mood is valid.
The conclusion is particular because the minor is particular; you cannot deduce 'all people are hypocrites'.
4
Apply to the second example
'Many people are defiantly disobedient' (particular affirmative) and 'Every defiantly disobedient is unguided' (universal affirmative, derived from 'Allah does not guide them') yield 'Many people are unguided'. Note how the Quranic expression is interpreted as a universal proposition.
The word 'al' in 'al-fāsiqūn' indicates generality, making the major universal.

C - Applications of the third mood of the first figure (ḥ-ḍ). Minor major. It is: (particular affirmative) + (universal affirmative) ∴ (particular affirmative). Examples from categorical conjunctive syllogism: 'And among people are those who say: We believe in Allah and the Last Day, but they are not believers.' (Universal affirmative) categorical: 'And whoever says: I believe in Allah and the Last Day lying, then he is a hypocrite.' (Particular affirmative) categorical: 'Therefore among people are hypocrites.' (Particular affirmative) categorical: 'And indeed many of the people are defiantly disobedient.' (Universal affirmative): 'And the defiantly disobedient – Allah does not guide them (i.e., does not decree guidance for them).' So it is by force (Every defiantly disobedient is unguided) because the 'al' is for generality. Particular affirmative: 'Therefore many people are unguided.' (Particular affirmative) categorical: 'And among people is one who sells himself seeking the pleasure of Allah.' (Universal affirmative) categorical: 'And whoever sells himself seeking the pleasure of Allah, for him is Paradise.' Particular affirmative: 'So among people is a group who have Paradise.' 'Some animals are fish, and every fish is permissible [to eat] when dead' ∴ 'So some animals are permissible [to eat] when dead.'

Commentary
This passage specifies the third mood (الضرب الثالث) of the first figure (الشكل الأول) in categorical syllogisms. The mood is defined as: minor premise particular affirmative (ج م), major premise universal affirmative (ك م), conclusion particular affirmative (ج م). The author then provides four examples from the Quran and everyday reasoning. The first example: the minor premise 'Among people are those who say they believe but are not believers' is particular affirmative (ج م); the major premise 'Whoever says they believe lying is a hypocrite' is universal affirmative (ك م); the conclusion 'Among people are hypocrites' is particular affirmative. The second example: 'Many people are defiantly disobedient' (particular affirmative), and 'Every defiantly disobedient is unguided' (since the phrase 'Allah does not guide them' is interpreted as universal negative but equivalent to universal affirmative 'Every defiantly disobedient is unguided' due to the 'al' for generality), conclusion 'Many people are unguided.' The third example: 'Among people is one who sells himself seeking Allah's pleasure' (ج م), 'Everyone who does so has Paradise' (ك م), conclusion 'Among people is a group who have Paradise.' The fourth example: 'Some animals are fish' (ج م), 'Every fish is permissible when dead' (ك م), conclusion 'Some animals are permissible when dead.' All follow the (ج م)+(ك م) ∴ (ج م) pattern.

Analyzing Examples of the First Figure Categorical Syllogism
Author illustrates the first figure categorical syllogism with Quranic and everyday examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to deconstruct a first figure categorical syllogism into its minor, major, and conclusion, and identify the middle term.

Core Idea
The first figure's validity depends on the middle term being distributed and correctly positioned.

This passage gives four examples of the first figure: three from the Quran and one from everyday life (fish). By working through each, you will master the pattern of the first figure syllogism.

Guided Path
1
State the general form
The author writes: (ج م) + (ك م) ∴ (ج م). This means: minor premise with subject ج and predicate م, major premise with subject ك and predicate م, conclusion with subject ج and predicate م. The middle term appears twice.
This notation is a placeholder; the actual terms vary by example.
2
Analyze the first example
Minor: 'Among people are those who say they believe but are not believers' → some people are 'sayers of false belief'. Major: 'Every such sayer is a hypocrite' → all sayers of false belief are hypocrites. Conclusion: 'Among people are hypocrites' → some people are hypocrites. Middle term: 'sayer of false belief'.
Valid first figure (Darii mood).
3
Analyze the second example
Minor: 'Many people are defiantly disobedient' → some people are fasiq. Major: 'The fasiq are not guided by Allah' → all fasiq are non-guided (with 'al' for generality). Conclusion: 'Many people are not guided' → some people are non-guided. Middle term: 'fasiq'.
Valid first figure; note the use of the definite article for universality.
4
Analyze the third example
Minor: 'Among people is one who sells himself seeking Allah's pleasure' → some people are 'self-sellers for Allah'. Major: 'Everyone who does so will have Paradise' → all such sellers get Paradise. Conclusion: 'Some people will have Paradise'. Middle term: 'seller seeking Allah's pleasure'.
Valid first figure.
5
Analyze the fourth example
Minor: 'Some animals are fish' → some animals are fish. Major: 'Every fish its dead is lawful' → all fish have lawful dead. Conclusion: 'Some animals have lawful dead'. Middle term: 'fish'.
Valid first figure; an everyday example.

The key point is that these are valid categorical syllogisms in the first figure. We can simply explain each example, showing how the premises lead to the conclusion. For instance, the first example: minor: some people are those who say they believe while not believing (specific), major: all such liars are hypocrites, conclusion: some people are hypocrites. The second example: minor: many people are defiantly disobedient (fasiq), major: the fasiq are not guided by Allah (interpreted as universal: every fasiq is not guided), conclusion: many people are not guided. The fourth: minor: some animals are fish, major: all fish have lawful dead (i.e., their dead are halal), conclusion: some animals have lawful dead. First Figure Conditional Syllogism: Examples and Structure
Author provides examples of first figure conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a first figure conditional syllogism and determine the validity of given examples using conjunctive and disjunctive premises.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms link propositions through implication, just as categoricals link terms through inclusion.

In this passage, the author illustrates the first figure conditional syllogism (al-iqtirani al-sharti). Unlike categorical syllogisms which relate terms, conditional syllogisms relate whole propositions via logical connectives. The examples show how conjunctive conditionals (if...then) and disjunctive conditionals (either...or) can be combined to yield a conditional conclusion. Understanding these patterns is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic legal and theological reasoning, where conditional statements appear frequently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise types
Determine whether each premise is a conjunctive conditional (shartiyya muttasila) or disjunctive conditional (shartiyya munfasila). In the examples, the minor premise is always conjunctive, while the major can be conjunctive or disjunctive.
2
Extract the antecedent and consequent of each premise
For each conditional, note what is being assumed (antecedent) and what is claimed to follow (consequent). This reveals the logical connections.
3
Check the transitivity of implication
In the first two examples, the consequent of the minor premise matches the antecedent of the major premise, forming a chain: if A then B, if B then C, so if A then C. This is the core of the first figure conditional syllogism.
4
Handle mixed premises
In the third example, the major is a disjunction. The consequent of the minor implies the disjunction, so the conclusion attaches the antecedent to that disjunction. Verify that the conclusion follows.
5
Construct the conclusion
Based on the logical link, formulate the conditional conclusion. Compare with the author's conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمثلة من الاقتراني الشرطي: (ج م) شرطية متصلة • قد يكون إذا كان الثمر مأكولاً كان طيب الرائحة. (ك م) شرطية متصلة وكلما كان المأكول طيب الرائحة كان مطعوماً ومشموماً (ج م) شرطية متصلة ∴ فقد يكون إذا كان الثمر مأكولاً كان مطعوماً ومشموماً. (ج م) شرطية متصلة • أحياناً إذا كانت الشمس طالعة كان القمر مرئياً في السماء (ك م) شرطية متصلة وكلما كان القمر مرئياً في السماء فهو يجري في فلكه (ج م) شرطية متصلة ∴ فقد يكون إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالقمر يجري في فلكه (ج م) شرطية متصلة • ربما إذا درس الطالب علوم الشريعة صار عالماً. (ك م) شرطية منفصلة ودائماً إما أن يكون العالم عاملاً بما يعلم أو غير عامل (ج م) شرطية متصلة ∴ فقد يكون إذا درس الطالب علوم الشريعة كان عاملاً بما يعلم أو غير عامل. (ج م) شرطية منفصلة أو ∴ فأحياناً إما أن يكون دارس علوم الشريعة عاملاً بما علم أو غير عالم.
Examples from the conditional conjunctive: (ج م) conditional conjunctive • Sometimes if the fruit is edible, it is pleasant-smelling. (ك م) conditional conjunctive • And whenever the edible is pleasant-smelling, it is both tasted and smelled. (ج م) conditional conjunctive ∴ Then it may be that if the fruit is edible, it is both tasted and smelled. (ج م) conditional conjunctive • Occasionally if the sun is shining, the moon is visible in the sky. (ك م) conditional conjunctive • And whenever the moon is visible in the sky, it is moving in its orbit. (ج م) conditional conjunctive ∴ Then it may be that if the sun is shining, the moon is moving in its orbit. (ج م) conditional conjunctive • Perhaps if a student studies the sciences of Sharia, he becomes a scholar. (ك م) conditional disjunctive • And always the scholar is either acting on his knowledge or not acting. (ج م) conditional conjunctive ∴ So it may be that if a student studies the sciences of Sharia, he is either acting on what he knows or not acting. (ج م) conditional disjunctive Or ∴ So sometimes the student of Sharia sciences is either acting on what he knows or not a scholar.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage provides examples of the first figure syllogism but using conditional (hypothetical) premises, specifically the 'iqtirani sharti' (conjunctive conditional) syllogism. The first three examples use only conjunctive conditionals (muttasil), while the last example mixes a conjunctive conditional minor with a disjunctive conditional (munfasil) major. The form is analogous to the categorical: the middle term is the consequent of the first conditional and the antecedent of the second? In conditional syllogisms, the 'middle term' is the link between the two conditionals. For instance, the first example: minor: 'If a fruit is edible, then it is pleasant-smelling' (where the antecedent is 'fruit is edible' and consequent 'pleasant-smelling'); major: 'Whenever something is pleasant-smelling, it is both tasted and smelled' (antecedent 'pleasant-smelling', consequent 'tasted and smelled'); conclusion: 'If a fruit is edible, then it is both tasted and smelled.' This is valid by transitivity of implication (hypothetical syllogism). The second example similarly: 'If sun shining then moon visible' and 'If moon visible then moon moving' yields 'If sun shining then moon moving'. The third example: minor: 'If student studies Sharia then becomes scholar'; major: 'Always the scholar is either acting or not acting' (a disjunction); conclusion: 'If student studies Sharia then he is either acting or not acting' (a conditional with disjunctive consequent). This is valid because the consequent of the minor ('becomes scholar') implies the disjunction from the major. The last line gives an alternative conclusion: 'So sometimes the student of Sharia sciences is either acting or not a scholar' which is a disjunction of two conditions. The text shows that by combining a conditional with a disjunctive, we can derive a disjunctive conclusion as well. These examples illustrate how the first figure syllogism can incorporate different types of conditional propositions.

Key Terms
شرطية متصلة (shartiyya muttasila): A conjunctive conditional proposition (if...then...) where the truth depends on the connection between antecedent and consequent. [Note: Standard in Mantiq; used in contrast to munfasila.]
شرطية منفصلة (shartiyya munfasila): A disjunctive conditional proposition (either...or...) where the truth depends on exclusive or inclusive disjunction. [Note: Standard in Mantiq; sub-types distinguished later.]
اقتراني شرطي (iqtirani sharti): A syllogism consisting of two conditional premises, typically in the first figure, leading to a conditional conclusion. [Note: Sometimes called qiyas sharti; in later texts, it is often treated as a variant of the categorical?]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Categorical
B) Conditional conjunctive only
C) Mixed conjunctive and disjunctive
D) Disjunctive only

Key Takeaway
The first figure conditional syllogism relies on the logical chain from antecedent to consequent through a middle proposition, analogous to the categorical first figure.

If it is inimitable, then it is a miracle. Therefore, if the Qur'an is the word of Allah, then it is a miracle.' Ensure your syllogism follows the pattern shown in the lesson.

The Fourth Mood of the First Figure: Pattern and Examples
Author presents multiple examples of the fourth mood for both categorical and conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth mood of the first figure (particular affirmative minor + universal negative major → particular negative conclusion) in both categorical and conditional syllogisms and verify its validity using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
The fourth mood proves particular negations from a particular affirmation and a universal negation.

In this lesson, we examine the fourth mood of the first figure, which combines a particular affirmative minor premise with a universal negative major premise to yield a particular negative conclusion. The author presents multiple examples from both categorical (حملي) and conditional (شرطي) syllogisms, demonstrating how this pattern appears in everyday and Islamic contexts. Mastering this mood equips you to evaluate arguments in fiqh, kalam, and tafsir that rely on this valid form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises in each example
Locate the minor premise (the one containing the subject of the conclusion) and the major premise (containing the predicate). Determine their quantity (particular or universal) and quality (affirmative or negative).
In the categorical examples, look for words like 'some' (جزئية) and 'none' (لا أحد).
2
Verify the mood pattern
Check whether the minor is particular affirmative (ج م) and the major is universal negative (ك س). If so, the conclusion should be particular negative (ج س). Confirm that the conclusion matches in the example.
The pattern is sometimes written as صغرى+كبرى → نتيجة.
3
Apply to conditional syllogisms
In conditional examples, treat the antecedent and consequent of the conditionals as terms. Ensure the minor conditional is particular affirmative (e.g., 'sometimes if A then B') and the major is universal negative (e.g., 'never if B then C') to yield the particular negative conclusion.
The same quantity/quality rules apply to the conditional propositions.
4
Construct your own example
Think of an Islamic subject (e.g., 'some acts are recommended') and a universal negative major (e.g., 'no recommended act is sinful'). Deduce the correct conclusion and verify it matches the pattern.
Use topics from fiqh, kalam, or tafsir to make the exercise relevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمثلة من الاقتراني الشرطي:
(ج م) شرطية متصلة
• قد يكون إذا كان الثمر مأكولاً كان طيب الرائحة.
(ك م) شرطية متصلة
وكلما كان المأكول طيب الرائحة كان مطعوماً ومشموماً
(ج م) شرطية متصلة
∴ فقد يكون إذا كان الثمر مأكولاً كان مطعوماً ومشموماً.
(ج م) شرطية متصلة
• أحياناً إذا كانت الشمس طالعة كان القمر مرئياً في السماء
(ك م) شرطية متصلة
وكلما كان القمر مرئياً في السماء فهو يجري في فلكه
(ج م) شرطية متصلة
∴ فقد يكون إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالقمر يجري في فلكه
(ج م) شرطية متصلة
• ربما إذا درس الطالب علوم الشريعة صار عالماً.
(ك م) شرطية منفصلة
ودائماً إما أن يكون العالم عاملاً بما يعلم أو غير عامل
(ج م) شرطية متصلة
∴ فقد يكون إذا درس الطالب علوم الشريعة كان عاملاً بما يعلم أو غير عامل.
(ج م) شرطية منفصلة
أو ∴ فأحياناً إما أن يكون دارس علوم الشريعة عاملاً بما علم أو غير عالم.
د ـ تطبيقات على الضرب الرابع من الشكل الأول (ح ـ ض).
صغرى كبرى
وهو (ج م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س)
أمثلة من الاقتراني الحملي:
• بعض الناس يعملون الصالحات.
ولا أحد من الذين يعملون الصالحات مغبون
(ج س) حملية
∴ فبعض الناس ليس بمغبون
• بعض العاملين مجزيون خيراً عند الله.
ولا أحد من المجزيين خيراً عند الله شقي
∴ فليس بعض العاملين بأشقياء
• من الفاكهة عنب. ولا شيء من العنب بمر الطعم.
∴ فليس بعض الفاكهة بمر الطعم.
أمثلة من الاقتراني الشرطي:
• قد يكون الماء حلواً أو مراً.
ولا شيء من المر بصالح للشرب
∴ فقد لا يكون الماء إما حلواً أو صالحاً للشرب
• قد يكون إذا استغفر العاصي ربه غفر له.
ولا أحد ممن يغفر الله لهم بمعذب.
∴ فليس كلما استغفر العاصي ربه كان معذباً.
• ربما إذا نام المجهد استعاد نشاطه.
ولا أحد يستعيد نشاطه يُحَسّ منه الكسل عن العمل.
∴ فليس كلما نام المجهد يُحَسّ منه الكسل عن العمل.
Examples of the conditional conjunctive: (ج م) connected conditional. • It may be that if the fruit is edible, it has a good smell. (ك م) connected conditional. And whenever the edible has a good smell, it is eaten and smelled. (ج م) connected conditional. ∴ Then it may be that if the fruit is edible, it is eaten and smelled. (ج م) connected conditional. • Sometimes if the sun is rising, the moon is visible in the sky. (ك م) connected conditional. And whenever the moon is visible in the sky, it moves in its orbit. (ج م) connected conditional. ∴ Then it may be that if the sun is rising, the moon moves in its orbit. (ج م) connected conditional. • Perhaps if the student studies the religious sciences, he becomes a scholar. (ك م) disjunctive conditional. And always the scholar is either acting upon what he knows or not acting. (ج م) connected conditional. ∴ Then it may be that if the student studies the religious sciences, he is either acting upon what he knows or not acting. (ج م) disjunctive conditional. Or ∴ Then sometimes the student of religious sciences is either acting upon what he learned or not a scholar. D — Applications of the fourth mood of the first figure (H — D). Minor premise, major premise. And it is (ج م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س). Examples of the categorical conjunctive: • Some people do righteous deeds. And none of those who do righteous deeds are losers. (ج س) categorical. ∴ Then some people are not losers. • Some workers are rewarded well by God. And none of those rewarded well by God are miserable. ∴ Then some workers are not miserable. • Among fruits is the grape. And nothing from the grape is bitter in taste. ∴ Then some fruits are not bitter in taste. Examples of the conditional conjunctive: • Water may be sweet or bitter. And nothing bitter is suitable for drinking. ∴ Then water may be either not sweet or not suitable for drinking. • It may be that if the sinner seeks forgiveness from his Lord, He forgives him. And none whom God forgives are punished. ∴ Then it is not that whenever the sinner seeks forgiveness from his Lord, he is punished. • Perhaps if the tired person sleeps, he regains his energy. And none who regains his energy shows laziness in work. ∴ Then it is not that whenever the tired person sleeps, he shows laziness in work.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage provides concrete examples of the fourth mood (الضرب الرابع) of the first figure (الشكل الأول) in both categorical (حملي) and conditional (شرطي) syllogisms. The author first introduces conditional examples using connected (متصلة) and disjunctive (منفصلة) conditionals, then states the pattern: (ج م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س), which likely represents a particular affirmative minor premise and a universal negative major premise leading to a particular negative conclusion. For instance, in the categorical examples: 'Some people do righteous deeds' (particular affirmative), 'None of those who do righteous deeds are losers' (universal negative), therefore 'Some people are not losers' (particular negative). Similarly, a conditional example: 'Water may be sweet or bitter' and 'Nothing bitter is suitable for drinking' yields 'Water may be either not sweet or not suitable for drinking'. The examples span both premise types to illustrate the same mood, showing that the conclusion is always particular and follows the quantity and quality of the premises.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
General Rule for Conclusion Quality in Syllogisms
Author derives a rule: conclusion is particular if any premise particular, negative if any premise negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to predict the quantity and quality of a syllogism's conclusion from its premises using the rule of particularity and negativity.

Core Idea
The conclusion inherits the weakest quantity and quality from the premises.

In the previous examples, the author noticed a pattern: the conclusion always reflects the 'weakest' premise. If any premise is particular (some), the conclusion must be particular. If any premise is negative (not), the conclusion must be negative. This rule saves you from constructing the full syllogism every time, allowing quick verification of argument validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantity and quality of each premise
For each premise in the syllogism, determine if it is universal or particular (quantity) and affirmative or negative (quality).
Premises may be A (universal affirmative), E (universal negative), I (particular affirmative), or O (particular negative).
2
Check for a particular premise
If any premise is particular (I or O), then the conclusion must be particular. If all premises are universal, the conclusion may be universal.
The presence of any 'some' forces the conclusion to be 'some'.
3
Check for a negative premise
If any premise is negative (E or O), then the conclusion must be negative. If all premises are affirmative, the conclusion is affirmative.
A single negative premise makes the conclusion negative.
4
Apply both conditions together
If a premise is both particular and negative (O), or if two premises provide one particular and one negative, the conclusion will be particular negative (O).
For example, premises I and O yield an O conclusion.
5
Test the rule on a real syllogism
Take a valid syllogism from Islamic sciences, e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Some drinks are intoxicants. Therefore, some drinks are forbidden.' The minor premise is particular, so the conclusion is particular affirmative.
Check that the conclusion matches the rule.

من هذه الأمثلة نلاحظ أن النتيجة دائماً تكون جزئية إذا وجد في القياس قضية جزئية، وتكون سالبة إذا وجد في القياس قضية سالبة، وتكون جزئية وسالبة إذا وجد في القياس قضية جزئية وقضية سالبة.
From these examples we observe that the conclusion is always particular if a particular proposition is present in the syllogism, and it is negative if a negative proposition is present, and it is particular and negative if a particular and a negative proposition are present.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage states a fundamental rule of syllogistic logic: the conclusion inherits the 'weakest' quality and quantity from the premises. Specifically, the conclusion is particular if any premise is particular, and negative if any premise is negative; if both a particular and a negative appear, the conclusion is both particular and negative. For example, in a categorical syllogism: 'Every pious person is saved' (universal affirmative) and 'Some people are not pious' (particular negative) would yield 'Some people are not saved' (particular negative). This rule is derived from the preceding examples and is consistent across the first figure's moods. The author emphasizes that this is a general observation (نلاحظ) that helps predict the conclusion without constructing the full syllogism.

سالبة (sāliba): negative proposition

The Rule of the Inferior Conclusion in Syllogisms
Author provides examples and derives the rule of the inferior conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the quantity and quality of a syllogism's conclusion based on the inferior premise rule.

Core Idea
The conclusion always follows the inferior premise: partial beats universal, negative beats affirmative.

This lesson reveals a powerful rule derived from examples: the conclusion of any valid syllogism is determined by the 'inferior' premise. Inferior means the premise that is partial in quantity or negative in quality. Once you master this rule, you can predict the conclusion of any syllogism simply by inspecting its premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises of a syllogism
Take any valid syllogism from the examples. Write down its major and minor premises as categorical propositions.
In the categorical examples, the premises are categorical. In the conditional examples, they are conditional but the rule still applies.
2
Determine the quantity and quality of each premise
For each premise, note if it is universal or partial (quantity), and affirmative or negative (quality).
Use the standard square of opposition: universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), partial affirmative (I), partial negative (O).
3
Apply the rule: conclusion follows the inferior premise
If any premise is partial, the conclusion is partial. If any premise is negative, the conclusion is negative. If both partial and negative exist, the conclusion is partial negative.
Inferior means the weaker: partial is inferior to universal; negative is inferior to affirmative.
4
Verify with the four figures
The author shows how the rule yields the correct conclusion for each syllogistic figure. Match the pattern: first figure (both inferiors absent → universal affirmative), second (qualitative inferior only → universal negative), third (quantitative inferior only → partial affirmative), fourth (both inferiors present → partial negative).
This mapping helps memorize the conclusion types for each figure.

وجاء الأخس (الكيفي والكمي) معاً في الشكل الرابع فجاءت النتيجة (ج س).
Examples of categorical conjunctive: Some people do righteous deeds. And none of those who do righteous deeds are cheated. (P N) categorical. Therefore some people are not cheated. Some workers are rewarded well by Allah. And none of those rewarded well by Allah are wretched. Therefore some workers are not wretched. Some fruits are grapes. And none who regains his energy is perceived lazy from work. Therefore it is not the case that whenever the tired person sleeps, he is perceived lazy from work. From these examples we observe that the conclusion is always partial if there is a partial proposition in the syllogism, and negative if there is a negative proposition in the syllogism, and partial and negative if there is a partial proposition and a negative proposition in the syllogism. And if we know that the partial is inferior to the universal, and that the negative is inferior to the affirmative, we know that the conclusion follows the inferior always. If the inferior (quantitative) is not present in the syllogism, the conclusion comes universal. If the inferior (qualitative) is not present in the syllogism, the conclusion comes affirmative. And both have come together in the first figure, so the conclusion came (U A). And the inferior (qualitative) alone came in the second figure, so the conclusion came (U N). And the inferior (quantitative) alone came in the third figure, so the conclusion came (P A). And the inferior (qualitative and quantitative) came together in the fourth figure, so the conclusion came (P N).

Commentary
This passage is a practical demonstration and rule extraction regarding syllogistic conclusions. The author first presents three examples of categorical conjunctive syllogisms (حملي) and three of conditional conjunctive syllogisms (شرطي). Each example shows a valid inference where the conclusion is partial and negative. After the examples, the author derives a general rule: the conclusion always follows the 'inferior' (الأخس) premise – meaning that if any premise is partial, the conclusion is partial; if any premise is negative, the conclusion is negative; if both partial and negative exist, the conclusion is both. Then the author explains the quantitative and qualitative inferiority: partial is inferior to universal, negative is inferior to affirmative. Applying this rule to the four figures, the author states: In the first figure, both inferiors (quantitative and qualitative) are absent? Actually the text says 'both have come together' meaning the inferiors are absent? Wait: 'وقد اجتمعا في الشكل الأول فجاءت النتيجة (ك م)' – it says both (the inferior quantitative and qualitative) have come together? That seems contradictory because the first figure typically yields universal affirmative conclusions. Actually the phrasing might mean that the inferiors (qualitative and quantitative) are absent (i.e., the premises are all universal affirmative) so the conclusion is universal affirmative. The author uses the abbreviations: ك م = كلية موجبة (universal affirmative), ك س = كلية سالبة (universal negative), ج م = جزئية موجبة (partial affirmative), ج س = جزئية سالبة (partial negative). So the rule is consistent: the conclusion takes the inferior quality/quantity present. The passage then maps these to the four figures, which is a common mnemonic in Islamic logic.

Identifying the Second Figure of the Categorical Syllogism
Author defines the second figure by the position of the middle term and gives an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second figure of a categorical syllogism by locating the middle term and verifying it is predicate in both premises.

Core Idea
The second figure's middle term is always the predicate of both premises.

In the first figure, the middle term is subject in the minor and predicate in the major. Here, the author defines the second figure by a different arrangement: the middle term serves as predicate in both premises. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the validity of syllogisms used in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor and major premises
In a categorical syllogism, the minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion; the major premise contains the predicate of the conclusion. Find which premise is which.
In the passage, the minor is 'Every mujahid in the path of Allah is pious' (subject: mujahid) and the major is 'None of the traitors are pious' (subject: traitors).
2
Locate the middle term
The middle term appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. It is the term that connects the two premises. Find the repeated term.
The repeated term is 'pious' (the predicate in both premises).
3
Check the position of the middle term
In each premise, determine whether the middle term is the subject or the predicate. For the second figure, it must be the predicate in both.
In the example, 'pious' is predicate in both: 'Every mujahid is pious' (predicate) and 'None of the traitors are pious' (predicate).
4
Verify the mood and figure
Once you know the figure, you can determine the mood by the type (A, E, I, O) of the premises and conclusion. The second figure has specific valid moods.
The example gives A (minor), E (major), and the implied conclusion is E (No mujahid is a traitor). This is Cesare (EAE), a valid second figure mood.

الشكل الثاني وضروبه المنتجة
وهو ما يكون الحد الأوسط فيه (محمولاً) في الصغرى (محمولاً) في الكبرى. [الشكل ٢ (ح ح)]
أمثلة :
أ ـ كل مجاهد في سبيل الله تقي . ولا أحد من الخائنين بتقي
The second figure and its productive moods. It is that in which the middle term is (predicate) in the minor premise and (predicate) in the major premise. [Figure 2 (P P)] Examples: A – Every mujahid in the path of Allah is pious. And none of the traitors are pious.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage defines the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The key characteristic is that the middle term (الحد الأوسط) serves as the predicate (محمول) in both the minor premise (الصغرى) and the major premise (الكبرى). This contrasts with the first figure where the middle term is subject in the minor and predicate in the major. The author then provides an example: the minor premise 'Every mujahid in the path of Allah is pious' (All M are P) and the major premise 'None of the traitors are pious' (No T are P). The intended conclusion, though not given here, would be a universal negative: 'No mujahid is a traitor' (No M are T) or its equivalent. This example illustrates a productive mood of the second figure, likely Cesare (EIO) or Camestres (AEE) depending on the missing conclusion.

Rule of Conclusion Following the Inferior in Quantity and Quality
Author explains rule that conclusion follows inferior in quantity and quality, applies to four figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of following the inferior to determine the conclusion's quantity and quality in any categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
The conclusion always follows the weaker premise: partial over universal in quantity, negative over affirmative in quality.

The author presents a powerful rule that governs the conclusion of every categorical syllogism. Instead of memorizing separate results for each figure, you can use this rule to deduce the conclusion's quantity and quality directly from the premises. Let's unpack it step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two dimensions: quantity and quality
Quantity refers to whether a proposition is universal (كلية) or partial (جزئية); quality refers to whether it is affirmative (موجبة) or negative (سالبة). The 'inferior' (الأخس) in quantity is the partial, and in quality the negative.
Memorize: partial < universal, negative < affirmative.
2
Identify the inferior attributes in the premises
Examine both premises. Note if any premise is partial (not universal) and if any premise is negative (not affirmative). These are the inferior attributes present.
If both premises are universal, no inferior in quantity; if both affirmative, no inferior in quality.
3
Apply the rule: the conclusion takes the inferior in each dimension
If no premise is partial, the conclusion is universal; if no premise is negative, the conclusion is affirmative. If a partial appears, the conclusion is partial; if a negative appears, the conclusion is negative. The rule works independently for quantity and quality.
The conclusion is the combination: e.g., if inferior in quantity only → partial affirmative; both inferiors → partial negative.
4
Test the rule on the four figures
In the first figure, both premises are universal affirmative → no inferior → conclusion universal affirmative. Second figure: one negative → conclusion universal negative. Third figure: one partial → conclusion partial affirmative. Fourth figure: one partial and one negative → conclusion partial negative.
Cross-check with known mood rules (e.g., Barbara, Celarent, etc.).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا علمنا أن الجزئية أخس من الكلية، وأن السالبة أخس من الموجبة، علمنا أن النتيجة تتبع الأخس دائماً، فإن لم يوجد في القياس الأخس (الكمي)، جاءت النتيجة (كلية)، وإن لم يوجد في القياس الأخس (الكيفي) جاءت النتيجة (موجبة). وقد اجتمعا في الشكل الأول فجاءت النتيجة (ك م). وجاء الأخس (الكيفي) وحده في الشكل الثاني فجاءت النتيجة (ك س). وجاء الأخس (الكمي) وحده في الشكل الثالث فجاءت النتيجة (ج م). وجاء الأخس (الكيفي والكمي) معاً في الشكل الرابع فجاءت النتيجة (ج س).
And if we know that the partial is inferior to the universal, and that the negative is inferior to the affirmative, we know that the conclusion always follows the inferior. For if the inferior (in quantity) does not exist in the syllogism, the conclusion comes (universal); and if the inferior (in quality) does not exist in the syllogism, the conclusion comes (affirmative). And both have been combined in the first figure, so the conclusion came (universal affirmative). And the inferior (in quality) alone came in the second figure, so the conclusion came (universal negative). And the inferior (in quantity) alone came in the third figure, so the conclusion came (partial affirmative). And both inferiors (in quality and quantity) came together in the fourth figure, so the conclusion came (partial negative).
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage explains a key rule in syllogistic logic: the conclusion of a categorical syllogism always follows the 'inferior' (الأخس) of its premises. The inferior in quantity is the partial proposition (جزئية), which is weaker than the universal (كلية). The inferior in quality is the negative proposition (سالبة), which is weaker than the affirmative (موجبة). The author states that if neither inferior is present in the premises (i.e., both premises are universal and affirmative), the conclusion is universal affirmative. If the inferior in quality (negative) is not present, but the inferior in quantity (partial) is, the conclusion is partial affirmative, etc. The author then applies this rule to the four figures. In the first figure, both inferiors are absent, so the conclusion is universal affirmative (ك م). In the second figure, only the inferior in quality (negative) appears, so the conclusion is universal negative (ك س). In the third figure, only the inferior in quantity (partial) appears, so the conclusion is partial affirmative (ج م). In the fourth figure, both inferiors appear, so the conclusion is partial negative (ج س). This rule is a useful mnemonic for determining the conclusion's quantity and quality based on the premises.

الأخس
الكمي
الكيفي
كلية
جزئية

D) Partial negative

Key Takeaway
The rule of following the inferior is a concise principle that determines the conclusion of any categorical syllogism based on the weakest elements of the premises.
