The Second Figure: Conditions and Productive Moods
Author defines second figure, states two necessary conditions, lists four productive moods with tables

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two necessary conditions for a valid second-figure syllogism and list the four productive moods.

Core Idea
Second figure requires one negative premise and a universal major premise to produce a negative conclusion.

The second figure is defined by the middle term being the predicate in both premises. However, not every combination of premises yields a valid argument. The author provides two crucial conditions that must be met for the syllogism to be productive. Understanding these conditions allows you to quickly determine which moods are valid in this figure.

Guided Path
1
Recall the figure definition
Remember that in the second figure, the middle term (M) is the predicate of both premises. So both premises are of the form 'S is M' and 'P is M' (or negative variants).
This contrasts with the first figure where the middle term is subject of one and predicate of the other.
2
State the two conditions
The author gives two necessary controls (ضابطين): (1) the two premises must differ in quality, i.e., one affirmative and one negative; (2) the major premise must be universal.
These are conditions for any valid syllogism in this figure.
3
Check the example
The author provides an example: 'Every mujahid in the path of Allah is pious' and 'None of the treacherous is pious'. The first premise is universal affirmative, the second is universal negative. They differ in quality, and the major (first premise) is universal. So it meets the conditions.
The conclusion would be 'No mujahid is treacherous' (universal negative).
4
Identify the productive moods
From the table, only four moods satisfy the two conditions. They are: (1) UA + UN → UN, (2) UN + UA → UN, (3) PA + UN → PN, (4) PN + UA → PN. Memorize these patterns.
UA = universal affirmative, UN = universal negative, PA = partial affirmative, PN = partial negative.
5
Verify using the square table
The square table shows all sixteen combinations of minor and major premises. Blank cells indicate barren (non-productive) moods. Only the four listed above have valid conclusions.
This visual helps to see why the other twelve are invalid.

Commentary
This passage defines the second figure of the categorical syllogism and explains its valid moods. The second figure is characterized by the middle term being a predicate in both premises (i.e., both premises are of the form 'S is M' and 'P is M' or 'S is M' and 'P is not M', etc.). The author gives an example: 'Every mujahid in the path of Allah is pious' (major premise) and 'None of the treacherous is pious' (minor premise). Note that the example illustrates the second figure with a universal affirmative and universal negative premise. The author then states two necessary conditions for a valid syllogism in this figure: (1) the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and (2) the major premise must be universal. These conditions correspond to the inferior rules mentioned earlier: the second figure requires the inferior in quality (negative), so at least one premise is negative; and since the major is universal, the conclusion is universal negative when both premises are universal. The author then explains that out of the sixteen possible combinations of premises (four types each for minor and major), only four moods satisfy these conditions and are productive. A table lists these four moods: (1) UA + UN → UN; (2) UN + UA → UN; (3) PA + UN → PN; (4) PN + UA → PN. The first two produce universal negative conclusions, and the last two produce partial negative conclusions. Finally, a square table (جدول تربيعي) shows all sixteen combinations, marking which are productive (produces UN or PN) and which are barren (فراغ : عقيم). This is a systematic presentation of the valid moods of the second figure.

First Mood of the Second Figure (AEE)
Author gives the formula and examples of the first mood of the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first mood of the second figure (AEE) in categorical and conditional syllogisms and verify its validity.

Core Idea
The second figure's first mood (AEE) always yields a universal negative conclusion from A+E premises.

The author introduces the first mood of the second figure (AEE) with the formula: universal affirmative + universal negative → universal negative. He provides multiple examples, both categorical (e.g., about laughing people and sad people) and conditional (e.g., about fetal growth). This mood is distinct because the middle term is the predicate in both premises, forcing a negative conclusion. Understanding this mood helps you quickly evaluate arguments where an affirmative and a negative universal premise appear together.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Check that the middle term is the predicate in both premises. In the second figure, the middle term is in the predicate position of both the major and minor premises.
For example, in 'Every laughing person is pleased' and 'No sad person is pleased', the middle term 'pleased' is the predicate of both.
2
Determine the mood
Assess the quantity and quality of the two premises: the major premise must be universal affirmative (A), and the minor premise must be universal negative (E). This combination is the first mood (AEE).
The author uses abbreviations 'ك م' for universal affirmative and 'ك س' for universal negative.
3
Verify the conclusion
The conclusion must be a universal negative (E) proposition, denying the subject of the major premise of the subject of the minor premise.
In the example, the conclusion 'No laughing person is sad' is universal negative.
4
Apply to conditional syllogisms
In conditional forms, the same mood appears with universal conditionals. Identify the universal affirmative conditional (e.g., 'Whenever the fetus grows...') and the universal negative conditional (e.g., 'Absolutely not when the fetus dies...') leading to a universal negative conclusion.
The structure mirrors categorical AEE but with temporal or material implication.

Second Figure Second Mood: Pattern Recognition
Author lists examples of the second figure second mood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a valid second figure second mood syllogism by checking the middle term position and the quantity/quality of premises in both categorical and conditional forms.

Core Idea
The second figure second mood combines a universal negative minor with a universal affirmative major to yield a universal negative conclusion.

In this lesson, we examine examples of the second mood of the second figure (al-shakl al-thani). The pattern is (ك س) + (ك م) → (ك س). The author provides four categorical examples and two conditional examples. By parsing these, you will learn to spot the structure in any syllogism, whether it uses subject-predicate or conditional propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and mood
The second figure has the middle term as predicate in both premises. The second mood requires a universal negative minor (ك س) and universal affirmative major (ك م). Check that the premises match these patterns.
The second mood is also known as Camestres in Latin tradition.
2
Find the middle term in categorical examples
In each categorical example, locate the term that appears as predicate in both premises. For instance, in the first example, 'happy' (مسرور) is the middle term appearing in both 'No sad person is happy' and 'Everyone actually laughing is happy'.
3
Verify the conclusion
The conclusion should be universal negative (ك س). Its subject is the minor term (subject of minor premise) and predicate is the major term (subject of major premise). Confirm that the minor term is the subject and the major term is the predicate.
In the first example, the minor term is 'sad person' and the major term is 'actually laughing'.
4
Apply to conditional examples
Conditional examples follow the same mood. Identify the middle term (e.g., 'dead' in the body example, 'divisible into two equals' in the number example) and verify that the premises and conclusion match the (ك س)+(ك م)→(ك س) pattern.
The propositions are conditionals, but the syllogistic structure remains categorical.
5
Test consistency across all examples
Ensure that each example satisfies the mood: minor premise is universal negative, major premise is universal affirmative, and conclusion is universal negative. Any deviation indicates a different mood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - تطبيقات على الضرب الثاني من الشكل الثاني (ح - ح). وهو (ك س) + (ك م) ∴ (ك س). أمثلة من الاقتراني الحملي: لا حزين مسرور وكل ضاحك بالفعل مسرور ∴ فلا حزين بضاحك بالفعل. لا حال مرفوع وكل فاعل مرفوع ∴ فلا حال هو فاعل. لا فرس هو إنسان وكل ضاحك إنسان ∴ فلا فرس هو ضاحك. لا لغو في الجنة وكل خير هو في الجنة ∴ فلا لغو هو خير. أمثلة من الاقتراني الشرطي: ليس البتة إذا كان الجسد يتحرك بالإرادة فهو ميت. وكل جسد لا يأكل ولا يتنفس هو ميت. ∴ فليس البتة إذا كان الجسد يتحرك بالإرادة فهو لا يأكل ولا يتنفس. ليس البتة إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو ينقسم إلى متساويين. وكل ما ليس بفرد ينقسم إلى متساويين. ∴ فليس البتة إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو هو ليس بفرد.
B - Applications on the second mood of the second figure (ح-ح). It is (universal negative) + (universal affirmative) ∴ (universal negative). Examples from the categorical conjunctive: No sad person is happy, and everyone actually laughing is happy, ∴ no sad person is actually laughing. No state (grammatical case) is nominative, and every active participle is nominative, ∴ no state is an active participle. No horse is human, and every laughing thing is human, ∴ no horse is laughing. No vain talk is in Paradise, and every good is in Paradise, ∴ no vain talk is good. Examples from the conditional conjunctive: It is never the case that if the body moves by will, it is dead. And every body that does not eat and does not breathe is dead. ∴ It is never the case that if the body moves by will, it does not eat and does not breathe. It is never the case that the number is either even or divisible into two equals. And everything that is not odd is divisible into two equals. ∴ It is never the case that the number is either even or it is not odd.

Commentary
This passage presents examples of the second mood of the second figure (al-shakl al-thani). In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. Here the mood is defined by the pattern: universal negative minor (ك س) + universal affirmative major (ك م) yields universal negative conclusion (ك س). The author first gives four categorical (hamli) examples, each following the same pattern. For instance, 'No sad person is happy' and 'everyone actually laughing is happy' gives 'no sad person is actually laughing'. The middle term 'happy' appears as predicate in both premises. Then two conditional (sharti) examples are given. The first conditional uses 'if-then' (muttasil) structure: the major premise is universal affirmative about bodies not eating and breathing being dead, and the minor is universal negative about bodies moving by will being dead; the conclusion negates the conjunction of movement and lack of eating/breathing. The second conditional uses disjunctive (munfasil) structure about evenness and divisibility. The pattern is consistent: the second figure second mood always produces a universal negative conclusion.

Analyzing the Syllogism Pattern (ج م) + (ك س) = (ج س)
Author provides categorical and conditional examples for the syllogism form (ج م) + (ك س) = (ج س).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the premises and conclusion in a syllogism of the form (ج م) + (ك س) = (ج س) and extract the terms using a real example.

Core Idea
The pattern links two premises through shared terms to produce a conclusion.

This lesson introduces a specific syllogism pattern expressed with Arabic variables. We will examine a concrete categorical example from the text and learn to map the terms to the pattern. This tool sharpens your ability to see the form behind real arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the pattern definition
The pattern is written as (ج م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س). It means: from a premise relating ج and م, and another relating ك and س, the conclusion relates ج and س.
The letters are variables that stand for terms. The pattern does not specify whether the propositions are universal or particular, affirmative or negative.
2
Identify the first categorical example
Take the example: 'Some people are writers; no illiterate is a writer; therefore some people are not illiterate.' Write each proposition in Arabic and label the terms.
First premise: بعض الناس كاتب → ج = people, م = writers. Second premise: ولا أحد من الأميين بكاتب → ك = illiterate, س = writers. Conclusion: فليس بعض الناس بأمي → ج = people, س = illiterate.
3
Map the terms to the pattern
Check that the conclusion's subject is ج (people) and predicate is س (illiterate). Note that the second premise's predicate (س) also appears in the conclusion's predicate. This matches the pattern (ج م) + (ك س) = (ج س).
In this example, the predicate of the conclusion is the subject of the second premise (ك), not س. However, the pattern as stated uses س in the conclusion. This inconsistency is discussed in the commentary.
4
Repeat with a conditional example
Now look at the first conditional example: 'It may be that if a person is European, he is fair-skinned. No black person is fair-skinned. Therefore it may not be that if a person is European, he is a black person.' Identify the terms: ج = human, م = fair-skinned (in the conditional), ك = black person, س = fair-skinned. Conclusion relates ج (human) and ك (black person).
Again, the conclusion's predicate is ك, not س. This suggests the pattern may be interpreted flexibly.
5
Assess the logical validity
Check whether the conclusion follows from the premises. In the categorical example, it does: if some people are writers and no illiterate are writers, then those writers (the some people) cannot be illiterate. This is a valid inference.
The pattern itself does not guarantee validity; the quantifiers and polarity matter.

وهو (ج م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س). أمثلة من الاقتراني الحملي: • بعض الناس كاتب ولا أحد من الأميين بكاتب ∴ فليس بعض الناس بأمي • فريق من الناس في الجنة ولا أحد من الكافرين في الجنة ∴ فليس بعض الناس بكافرين • ﴿ومن الناس من يعجبك قوله في الحياة الدنيا ويشهد الله على ما في قلبه وهو ألد الخصام﴾. ولا أحد من المؤمنين المتقين هذا وصفه ∴ فبعض الناس ليس هذا وصفه أمثلة من الاقتراني الشرطي: • قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان أوروبياً كان أبيض البشرة. ولا أحد من الزنوج بأبيض البشرة. ∴ فقد لا يكون إذا كان الإنسان أوروبياً فهو واحد من الزنوج. • ربما يكون إذا كان القمر مضيئاً جداً فالشهر في منتصفه. ولا ليلة غير الليلة الخامسة عشر يكون الشهر في منتصفه. ∴ فليس دائماً إذا كان القمر مضيئاً جداً فالليلة غير الليلة الخامسة عشر. • أحياناً إما أن تكون الخيل دُهماً أو غُبراً. ولا أحد من حسان الخيل بأغبر ∴ فليس دائماً إما أن تكون الخيل دهماً أو من حسان الخيل.
It is (J M) + (K S) therefore (J S). Examples of the categorical conjunctive: • Some people are writers and no illiterate is a writer, therefore some people are not illiterate. • A group of people are in Paradise and no disbeliever is in Paradise, therefore some people are not disbelievers. • And among people is he whose speech pleases you in worldly life and he calls Allah to witness as to what is in his heart, yet he is the fiercest of opponents. And none of the believing righteous are described as such, therefore some people are not so described. Examples of the conditional conjunctive: • It may be that if a person is European, he is fair-skinned. And no black person is fair-skinned. Therefore it may not be that if a person is European, he is a black person. • It might be that if the moon is very bright, then the month is at its midpoint. And no night other than the fifteenth is the month at its midpoint. Therefore it is not always that if the moon is very bright, then the night is other than the fifteenth. • Sometimes either horses are dark or grulla. And none of the beautiful horses are grulla. Therefore it is not always that either horses are dark or they are beautiful horses.

Commentary
This passage provides examples for a syllogism pattern expressed as (ج م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س). The author first gives three categorical examples. The first: "Some people are writers, and no illiterate is a writer, therefore some people are not illiterate." This is valid. The second: "A group of people are in Paradise, and no disbeliever is in Paradise, therefore some people are not disbelievers." The third includes a Quranic verse and concludes that some people are not of that description. Then follow three conditional examples. The first: "It may be that if a person is European, he is fair-skinned. And no black person is fair-skinned. Therefore it may not be that if a person is European, he is a black person." This shows a conditional syllogism with possibility. The second deals with moon brightness and month midpoint. The third with horses being dark or grulla. All illustrate the same pattern applied to different proposition types.

Applications of the Fourth Mood of the Second Figure (H-H)
Author demonstrates the fourth mood of the second figure with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and validate the fourth mood of the second figure in both categorical and conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
The fourth mood of the second figure requires a particular negative and universal affirmative premise.

In this lesson, we explore the fourth mood of the second figure, denoted as (حـ ح). The author provides examples from categorical and conditional syllogisms. We'll analyze the pattern and confirm its validity.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the pattern
The mood is (ج س) + (ك م) ∴ (ج س). The first premise is particular negative, second universal affirmative, conclusion particular negative.
This is the OAO mood in the second figure.
2
Identify the terms
For each example, determine the subject, predicate, and middle term (the term common to both premises).
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises.
3
Verify the figure
Check that the middle term is the predicate of both premises. In the examples, the middle term (e.g., 'domestic', 'bitter', 'enters Paradise') appears as predicate.
4
Check the mood
Ensure the quantities and qualities match: first premise particular negative (O), second universal affirmative (A), conclusion particular negative (O).
This mood is valid in the second figure.
5
Apply to conditionals
For conditional syllogisms, treat the conditional as a categorical proposition. Verify that the same OAO pattern holds.
The conditional examples mirror the categorical pattern.

D. Applications of the fourth mood of the second figure (H-H). It is (J S) + (K M) therefore (J S). Examples of the categorical conjunctive: • Some donkeys are not domestic, and every non-striped donkey is domestic, therefore some donkeys are not non-striped donkeys. • Some fruit is not bitter, and every colocynth fruit is bitter, therefore some fruit is not colocynth. • Some people do not enter Paradise, and every believer enters Paradise, therefore some people are not believers. Examples of the conditional conjunctive: • It may not be that if a person is tall, he is black-skinned. And every black person is black-skinned. Therefore it may not be that if a person is tall, he is a black person. • Sometimes it is not that one of two things is either greater than the other or smaller. And whenever one of two things is equal to the other, it is not smaller. Therefore it may not be that one of two things is either greater than the other or equal to it. Or therefore it may not be that whenever one of two things is not greater than the other, it is equal to it.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth mood of the second figure, denoted as (حـ ح). The pattern is (ج س) + (ك م) therefore (ج س). Categorical examples: (1) Some donkeys are not domestic, every non-striped donkey is domestic, so some donkeys are not non-striped donkeys. (2) Some fruit is not bitter, every colocynth is bitter, so some fruit is not colocynth. (3) Some people do not enter Paradise, every believer enters Paradise, so some people are not believers. These show the mood is valid with a particular negative conclusion. Conditional examples: (1) It may not be that if a person is tall, he is black-skinned. Every black person is black-skinned. So it may not be that if a person is tall, he is a black person. (2) Sometimes one of two things is not either greater or smaller. Whenever one is equal to the other, it is not smaller. So it may not be that one is either greater or equal. An alternative conclusion is given. These demonstrate the mood's application to conditionals.

The Third Figure: Conditions and Productive Moods
Author defines third figure, conditions, and lists moods in table

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a valid third-figure syllogism and list its six productive moods from the table.

Core Idea
The third figure always yields a particular conclusion, never a universal one.

This passage defines the third figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term is the subject in both premises. The author then lays down two necessary conditions for validity: the minor premise must be affirmative, and one premise must be universal. He then presents a table of the six productive moods out of sixteen possibilities. Understanding this figure is crucial for evaluating many arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining characteristic of the third figure
In the third figure, the middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ) is the subject in both the minor and major premises. Confirm this from the passage: 'الحد الأوسط فيه موضوعاً في الصغرى موضوعاً في الكبرى'.
This distinguishes it from the first figure (middle term is subject in minor, predicate in major) and second figure (predicate in both).
2
Extract the two validity conditions
The passage states two conditions: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative, and (2) one of the premises must be universal. The first is about quality (kayf), the second about quantity (kamm).
These conditions are necessary for any conclusion to follow.
3
Understand the notation for premise types
The author uses abbreviations: 'ك م' for universal affirmative, 'ك س' for universal negative, 'ج م' for particular affirmative, 'ج س' for particular negative. Familiarize yourself with these symbols as they appear in the table.
4
Trace out the six productive moods from the table
The table lists six moods. For each, note the minor, major, and conclusion. Observe that all conclusions are particular (either affirmative or negative). For example, mood one: minor = ك م, major = ك م, conclusion = ج م.
Memorize this table; you can reconstruct it by applying the conditions.
5
Apply the conditions to an Islamic example
Consider: 'Every believer is righteous' (universal affirmative), 'Every believer enters Paradise' (universal affirmative). This satisfies both conditions (minor affirmative, one universal) and yields 'Some righteous enter Paradise' (particular affirmative). Check that this matches mood one.
This shows how the third figure is used in theological reasoning.

الشكل الثالث وضروبه المنتجة وهو ما يكون الحد الأوسط فيه (موضوعاً) في الصغرى، (موضوعاً) في الكبرى. [الشكل ٣ (ض ض)] ولا ينتج هذا الشكل من أشكال القياس إنتاجاً صحيحاً مطرداً إلا بتحقق ضابطين فيه. الضابط الأول: أن تكون المقدمة الصغرى فيه موجبة. الضابط الثاني: أن تكون إحدى المقدمتين فيه كلية. إيجاب الصغرى كلية إحدى المقدمتين وهو شرط من جهة (الكيف) وهو شرط من جهة (الكم) وبتتبع تحقيق هذين الضابطين في ضروبه الستة عشر نلاحظ أنه لا ينتج منها إلا ستة أضرب فقط، وهي الموضحة في الجدول التالي مع كيفية إنتاجها. الضرب مقدمته الصغرى مقدمته الكبرى إنتاجه الأول كلية موجبة (ك م) كلية موجبة (ك م) جزئية موجبة (ج م) الثاني كلية موجبة (ك م) كلية سالبة (ك س) جزئية سالبة (ج س) الثالث كلية موجبة (ك م) جزئية موجبة (ج م) جزئية موجبة (ج م) الرابع كلية موجبة (ك م) جزئية سالبة (ج س) جزئية سالبة (ج س) الخامس جزئية موجبة (ج م) كلية موجبة (ك م) جزئية موجبة (ج م) السادس جزئية موجبة (ج م) كلية سالبة (ك س) جزئية سالبة (ج س) فهو لا ينتج إلا جزئية موجبة أو سالبة، والضروب الأخرى لهذا الشكل
The third figure and its productive moods. It is that in which the middle term is the subject in the minor premise and the subject in the major premise. [Figure 3 (subject, subject)] This figure does not produce a consistently correct conclusion from the syllogistic forms unless two conditions are met. The first condition: the minor premise must be affirmative. The second condition: one of the two premises must be universal. Affirmation of the minor and universality of one of the two premises — the first is a condition in respect of quality, and the second in respect of quantity. By examining the fulfillment of these two conditions in its sixteen possibilities, we note that only six moods are productive, as shown in the following table along with how they are produced. | Mood | Minor premise | Major premise | Conclusion | |------|---------------|---------------|------------| | First | Universal affirmative (ك م) | Universal affirmative (ك م) | Particular affirmative (ج م) | | Second | Universal affirmative (ك م) | Universal negative (ك س) | Particular negative (ج س) | | Third | Universal affirmative (ك م) | Particular affirmative (ج م) | Particular affirmative (ج م) | | Fourth | Universal affirmative (ك م) | Particular negative (ج س) | Particular negative (ج س) | | Fifth | Particular affirmative (ج م) | Universal affirmative (ك م) | Particular affirmative (ج م) | | Sixth | Particular affirmative (ج م) | Universal negative (ك س) | Particular negative (ج س) | Thus it produces only a particular affirmative or negative; the other moods of this figure...
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage defines the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The third figure is characterized by the middle term being the subject in both premises. The author then states that for the third figure to yield a valid conclusion, two conditions must be met: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative, and (2) one of the premises must be universal. The first condition concerns the quality (affirmative vs. negative), the second concerns quantity (universal vs. particular). The author then explains that out of the sixteen possible combinations of premises (considering quantity and quality), only six are productive. These six are presented in a table. For example, the first mood has universal affirmative minor and universal affirmative major, producing a particular affirmative conclusion. The sixth mood has particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, producing a particular negative conclusion. The key point is that the third figure never yields a universal conclusion; it always produces a particular conclusion (affirmative or negative). The student should memorize these six moods and the conditions. For instance, using an Islamic example: 'Every believer is righteous, and every believer enters Paradise, therefore some righteous enter Paradise' is a valid third figure syllogism (mood one).

The Syllogism

6 min
The Third Figure: Conditions and Productive Moods
Definition of third figure, two validity conditions, and six productive moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third figure of a categorical syllogism, apply its two validity conditions, and list its six productive moods.

Core Idea
The third figure requires an affirmative minor and a universal premise, yielding only particular conclusions.

The third figure is one of four figures in categorical logic. Unlike the first figure, its middle term is the subject in both premises. The author of this passage explains that this figure only produces valid conclusions under two specific conditions. We will examine those conditions and see which moods survive the test.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining property of the third figure
The middle term is the subject in both the minor premise and the major premise. This is the key structural feature that distinguishes it from other figures.
The notation 'ض ض' (subject-subject) is used to denote this.
2
Learn the two necessary conditions for validity
Condition 1: The minor premise must be affirmative. Condition 2: At least one of the two premises must be universal. These conditions ensure a necessary conclusion.
These are conditions of quality and quantity, respectively.
3
Recognize that only six moods satisfy both conditions
From the 16 possible combinations (4 premise types × 4 premise types), only six satisfy both conditions. These are the productive moods listed in the table.
The author shows that all other moods are 'sterile' (عقيم).
4
Memorize the six productive moods and their conclusions
All productive moods end with a particular conclusion (either affirmative or negative). The six are: (U A)+(U A)→(P A); (U A)+(U N)→(P N); (U A)+(P A)→(P A); (U A)+(P N)→(P N); (P A)+(U A)→(P A); (P A)+(U N)→(P N).
The conclusion is always particular because the minor premise, being affirmative, gives a distributed middle only in the major.
5
Apply the conditions to a given syllogism
Check that the minor premise is affirmative and at least one premise is universal. If both hold, identify the mood and verify that it matches one of the six productive moods.
Use the given examples (human/animal, gold/metal) as practice.

تطبيقات على الشكل الثالث (ض ض):
صغرى كبرى نتيجة
أ - على الضرب الأول منه ( ك م ) + ( ك م ) ∴ ( ج م )
من الاقتراني الحملي:
• كل إنسان حيوان وكل إنسان قابل للعلم وصنعة الكتابة
∴ بعض الحيوان قابل للعلم وصنعة الكتابة

The third figure and its productive moods. It is that in which the middle term is subject in the minor premise and subject in the major premise. [Figure 3 (subject-subject)] This figure of the figures of the syllogism does not produce a correct and consistent production except by the verification of two conditions in it. The first condition: that the minor premise be affirmative. The second condition: that one of the two premises be universal. Positivity of the minor; universality of one of the two premises. It is a condition regarding quality, and it is a condition regarding quantity. By examining the fulfillment of these two conditions in its sixteen moods, we note that only six moods produce, which are shown in the following table with how they produce. The mood: its minor premise, its major premise, its conclusion. First: universal affirmative (U A) + universal affirmative (U A) → particular affirmative (P A). Second: universal affirmative (U A) + universal negative (U N) → particular negative (P N). Third: universal affirmative (U A) + particular affirmative (P A) → particular affirmative (P A). Fourth: universal affirmative (U A) + particular negative (P N) → particular negative (P N). Fifth: particular affirmative (P A) + universal affirmative (U A) → particular affirmative (P A). Sixth: particular affirmative (P A) + universal negative (U N) → particular negative (P N). Thus it only produces a particular affirmative or negative, and the other moods of this figure are sterile, all shown together with the productive moods in the following square table: Figure 3 (subject-subject) productive with symbols and sequence. 1 - (U A) + (U A) ∴ (P A); 2 - (U A) + (U N) ∴ (P N); 3 - (P A) + (U A) ∴ (P A); 4 - (U A) + (P N) ∴ (P N); 5 - (P A) + (U A) ∴ (P A); 6 - (P A) + (U N) ∴ (P N). Moods of the third figure: their productive and sterile. The major: U A, P A, U N, P N; minor: U A (productive: P A, productive: P A, productive: P N, productive: P N); P A (productive: P A, productive: P A); U N; P N; blank: sterile. Applications on the third figure: A - on the first mood (U A)+(U A) ∴ (P A) from categorical conjunctive: Every human is an animal and every human is capable of knowledge and writing. ∴ Some animal is capable of knowledge and writing. Every gold is a metal and every gold does not oxidize (i.e., it is not affected by acids). ∴ Some metal does not oxidize. From conditional conjunctive: Whenever a fruit is an apple, it is a fruit; and whenever it is an apple, it is capable of being pressed into juice. ∴ So it may be that if a fruit is fruit, it is capable of being pressed into juice.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage defines the third figure of the categorical syllogism and explains its productive moods. The author begins by stating that in the third figure, the middle term is the subject in both premises (subject-subject, hence the notation ض ض). The figure does not yield valid conclusions except under two conditions: first, the minor premise must be affirmative; second, one of the two premises must be universal. These conditions regulate the quality (كيف) and quantity (كم) of the premises. After applying these conditions to all sixteen possible moods (4×4 combinations of quantity and quality), only six are productive. A table lists them: all produce only particular conclusions (either affirmative or negative). A square table further organizes productive and sterile moods. The author then provides applications. For the first mood (universal affirmative minor and major), two categorical examples are given: from human to animal (yielding "some animal is capable of knowledge and writing") and from gold to metal (yielding "some metal does not oxidize"). A conditional conjunctive example is also given: if a fruit is an apple, it is a fruit and pressable; thus it may be that if a fruit is fruit, it is pressable (though the conditional conclusion is particular and problematic).

Key Terms
الشكل الثالث (al-shakl al-thālith): The third figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term is subject in both premises. [Note: Standard across Aristotelian and Islamic logic; no significant shift.]
الحد الأوسط (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ): The middle term, which appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]
ضرب (ḍarb): A mood of the syllogism, determined by the quantity and quality of the premises. [Note: Equivalent to 'mood' in Western logic; stable.]
إنتاج (intāj): Productivity of a syllogism; a mood produces a necessary conclusion. [Note: Standard term for validity/usefulness.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the third figure of the categorical syllogism, which two conditions are necessary for a mood to be productive?
A) Minor negative and major universal
B) Minor affirmative and both premises universal
C) Minor affirmative and at least one premise universal
D) Minor affirmative and both premises particular

Key Takeaway
The third figure always yields a particular conclusion and requires an affirmative minor premise and at least one universal premise for productivity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the premises: 'Every mufti is a scholar' (U A) and 'Scholars are some of the people of knowledge' (P A). First check if this is a third-figure mood. Then apply the conditions and state the conclusion if valid.
?
Construct a valid third-figure syllogism on an Islamic topic (e.g., types of worship) using mood 2 (U A + U N → P N). Ensure both conditions are met.
